THE RED PAGE
ILLUSION.
She beckoned me over the misty rise,
Her radiant feet were bare,
The grey-blue dawn dreamed in her eyes,
Stars glimmered in her hair.
She murmured the spell of Paradise —
And Paradise was there!
“O follow me, Love, wherever I will,
And lose thy soul with me,
Deep in the shadowy scrub, where still
Broods grey-winged mystery,
And strange dim flowers are dreaming, till
I waken them for thee.”
I fled with her far from the golden light,
Deep into the brooding shade,
Where startled pinions swooped from sight,
And dim dream-flowers swayed,
But ever I followed the flickei'ing white
Bare feet that never stayed.
“0 follow me, Love, where the mountains
tower,
And the eagle,' circling high,
Sails over the dazzling clouds, that lower
Above earth’s tarnished sky,
And the glaciers guard the azure flower
Of immortality.”
I climbed in search of the glittering bloom
Through chasms black as night,
And staggered on through the blinding gloom,
Of everlasting light,
Where I stumbled over a nameless tomb —
And still I followed her flight.
“O follow me down where the lighthouse
glows
‘ Good-bye' to the dying sun,
And the silent river seaward flows,
Whose course is nearly run,
And sinless quietude shall close
Life’s battle lost —and won.”
I followed her, dreaming of dreamless sleep—
The sleep that never shall be!
For whenever the wings of the southerly
sweep
Through the dream of the slumbering sea,
Her white feet glimmer along the deep,
And I hear her calling me —
“O follow me, Love, wherever I will,
And lose thy soul with me,
Deep in the shadow of death, where still
Broods grey-winged mystery.
And strange star-flowers are dreaming, till
I waken them for thee.”
N.s.w.
D.
The Pervertosensicon
From my earliest remembrance of him
Upsigh Doune was addicted to doing things
wrong way up.
Last week he burst into my attic, his eyes
aglow with enthusiasm, his clothes ou, shirt
outside, as usual —dragging behind him a
weird mass of machinery upon wheels,
“This,” he exclaimed, forgetting in his ex-
citement to talk backwards, or stand upon
his hands, “ This is the PERVERTO-
SENSICON. ... It is the greatest triumph
in inversion of the era, and can be
constructed in various types. No. IA, for
instance, immediately inverts the truth of
anything placed in its receiving slot. I ex-
pect a gi-eat sale of 1A among explorers,
mining experts, and Tory leader-writers. And
the machine before you possesses the ines-
timable property of reversing the idea con-
tained in any poem, expeditiously, and with-
out pain to the operator. One should be
found in every verse-writer’s household. In-
valuable for inverted psalms, and will not
injure the most delicate poetic fabric! Al-
low me” He pounced upon a book f-rom
my shelves and tore a page out at random.
“ ‘Never Again,’ by that dangerous fellow
McCrae,” he continued. “We will just
feed this into the main cylinder.”
Suiting the action to the word he crawled
carefully underneath his invention, feet
first. Emerging, he wrenched over a lever,
and the whole mechanism sprang into move-
ment —whirring, throbbing, and hooting, till
the whole building shook with the vibration.
Finally, with a mechanical spasm, the Per-
vertosensicon vomited forth at our feet a
square foot of paper imprinted with irregu-
lar type of a scarlet color.y I seized it
eagerly and read :
EVER AGAIN.
She looks on me with sterner eye than dun,
And, swooping with a comprehensive spring
Pounces upon me, crying “ You’re the One!”
At mid-day: and the boarders, as we cling,
Laugh from their secret lairs like anything.
Ever indefors, or on the holey pave,
Beyond the limit of the green flag bent
By Board of Works about its street-hewn
cave,
She waits to grab me: no more sure event
Than she: as sure as Agent for the Rent.
Ever again ! Although beneath a couch
I crawl, half-stifled, full of fluff, and swear,
Or low behind the parlor-piano, crouch
Waiting her figure—forty, fat and fair —
To re-ascend th’ unvarnishable stair. ...
I hide from her in labyrinthine bars
Of pubs distilling alcoholic bane,
Or from a cab-rank catch th’ electric cars —
A pathway from her to the Melbourne train;
In vain, in vain: She’ll capture me again!
While I still stood spellbound in horror at this
desecration, Mr. Doune had already fed his
infernal machine with another leaf. I had
just time to observe that Brennan’s “Ro-
mance” was missing from my “Golden Trea-
sury,” when the miscreant handed me an-
other red-typed effusion with a low bow, and
I perused:—
FACT.
To-night, in a room in a cafe
( . . . clear out! . . . don’t I tell you it s
Px-ivate! . . .)
O, folded close to her chest!
—and every dash’d petty annoyance
(was, it Cats? or a private detective
concealed in a shadowy corner,
that giggled and chuckled and coughed!)
—the weight of the lady,
made worse by my aching rheumatics . . .
supporting twelve stone on my knees —•
—clear out! ... can’t you see that’s its
Private!! . . .
close-locked in her terrible arm-grasp
no word do I utter . . . I’m smothered :
, the silence of choking . . . and swears . . .
only l-ound and over us
that cheerless, intimate gaslight,
and the waiter with serviette rustling,
hovering round for his tip.
. . . . clear out! . . . don’t I tell you it’s
PRIVATE!!.. . .
to-night, in a room in a cafS,
O, folded tight to her chest! . . .
“Vastly improved in the perverting, on
my honor!” cried the busy inventor, deftly
scissoring a page from another booklet, and
jamming one of Le Gay Brereton’s poems
into the main cylinder.
“But what will you produce as the oppo-
site idea to ‘Open Speech’?” I demanded.
“‘Backbiting,’ of-course,” responded Doune
confidently; but the mechanism must have
slipped a cog while puzzling out the anatomy,
for what we eventually got was—
THE EAR-BITER.
Good frenno’ mine, you’ve sush frank eyes:
You weep with me, you sympathise!
You’re—hie/ —the besht I ever knew:
Then shay—l want a quid from you !
One single quid—to spend in Beer!
Goo’ fren’, I won’t be alwaysh here —-
Might die, before the beer’sh in view;
Hie, fx-en’! I ask a quid from you.
Shee—when I pass you in the street,
It seems our thirsty spirits meet
In one large thirsty wish —that’sh true!
So shay—about that quid from you?
Are you, too, longing for a burst,
Yet wait to hear me broach it first?
Well, hang it all! You’ve got the cue!
And — hid— l’d like a quid from you.
Some way our thoughts together club,
Since both lift eyes towards a pub,
Towards the self-shame pints o’ brew:
Then shout—l want a quid from you.
“But, see here,” I protested weakly,
“that’s not a genuine upside-down idea.
If” —— The roar of the machine drowned
my voice. Looking over, I saw that the
inventor, had just crammed Bernard
G’Dowd’s “Mystery” into its seething in-;
terior. In 45 seconds it hooted cheerfully,;
and suddenly deposited in my lap,
REALITY.
The omnipresent “Mystery”
No sciences ignore:
She is thg yet-untravelled sea
To-morrow sailed by Lore.
If fever of rebellious vein
Call up her shadow dim,
Compel agnostic Solon’s reign
To pass to Her from Him,
Shall with the Mystic seers in these
Our drugged pretensions peer,
Read God in emptied granaries,
Praise primal prates of Fear —
The atavistic ravages,
The neolithic strain,
That lures the city savages
To-day to pray for rain?
Nay, Sense’s “No” to Mystic’s “Yes”!
The sense-paths, free from Doubt,
Have slowly built his Consciousness
From the Greater World without:
Have one by one made “Knowing” his—
Life’s real existence brought
From out the great material “Is”
In picture-gifts of thought.
The Ego’s part, these gifts to think:
This palace buiPt, to ’fine:
To sort, to add, to judge, to link,
Arrange and re-combine —-
Yea, credent sage shall eat his Word,
And in Truth’s mirror see
His Mystic World the image blurred
Of fair Reality!
“Why,” I exclaimed in astonishment, “it
makes a kind of sense!”
“I said the machine would give a true op-
posite,” replied Upsigh Doune, much
pleased. Then he fed the whirling cogs with
Bayldon’s “Sunset,” and the ejector ejacu-
lated —
DAWNBREAIv.
The purple p’liceman slumbers like a log:
Flaunting thro’ Sky’s blood-tinctured jew-
ell’ry /
Burst billion cannonades of stars — i.e.,
They are the flagrant diamond-studs which
clog
The vast white shirt-front of the red-gilled
Dawn.
In the proud East all midnight’s Black Hus-
sars
Take off their regal trousers gemm’d with
stax-s,
And put their ’mperious trombones into
Pawn. . . .
“This poet,” I said, greatly impressed,
“only needs Inversion to become truly
Great.”
“On the contrary,” sharply returned my
friend, as he pushed Arthur Adams’ “Bond
Street” into the feeding slot, “Inversion
would give him a sense of humor, and he’d
never write another line.”
The Pervertosensicon drowned further ar-
gument by a great outbreak of moans,
sparks, thuds and buzzing, which finally re-
sulted in a scarlet-lettered outbreak titled
BLUDGER LANE.
’Mid reek of stale mixed alcohols
Unseeing coppers stand like dolls:
While leprous Chinamen flit by
Past caravanserais of Molls.
Decayed bananas rot and strew
The lazy gutter’s channel through:
And 10, down stagnant alleys bloat
The stray dogs’ corpses, two by two!
I see a pale cop past me whiz.
Great need of speed from thence is his,
For Nick the Nark is dancing on
The gin-soaked chest of Lousy Liz.
A languid Burglar marks the brawl,
Bored by the dulness of it all:
And o’er his chiv
“ ’Ave the ’ouse down about our ears
next, no doubt, with yer rubbishy steam-
mangles—breakin’ the crockery in the kit-
chen, an’ makin’ more messes for the girl to
sweep up! You give over making that
noise this 'minnit, or out you go out of my
establishment neck-an’-crop. An’ I’ll trouble
yer for week-afore-last’s board, an’ the
clean wash-basin you borrered from the
upstairs-front, if it ain’t down with yer
clo’es in th’ pawn-shop . . .!!”.
’Twas my landlady! She stood in the
doorway—adjectival, raucous and defiant:
the very incarnation of plain, unvarnished
Prose! The temptation was too much.
With one triumphant yell I seized and
swung her off her feet, and all unheeding
the plaintive cries of the inventor, flung
her head-first into the receiver, and pressed
the lever.
The Pervertosensicon roused itself to a
supreme effort. Its roaring and hooting
rose to a frenzied whir and a shrill scream;
then suddenly, with a cascade of riven
screws, type and wheels, it burst . . . and
from the ruins there stepped unhurt, my
Landlady—but changed!
Now, my friends, I live in a Hash-house
Paradise. To me every morn, as I 101 l on
my pillows of eider-down in the star bed-
room, comes the Inverted Landlady, with
delicacies- of her own cooking. Payment for
board has become an insult —I have a latch-
key—crusted port is supplied me with meals
-—a marble bath has been purchased for my
benefit, and electric light installed
True, Upsigh Doune has never forgiven
my destroyal of his weird machine. I care
not. My Landlady is a Poem!
HUGH MCKAY.
The Anatomy of Verse
Prof. Saintsbury has compressed his three-
volume History into a one-volume Manual
of English Prosody (Angus and Robertson,
65.), with a new introduction. Prosody is
to Poetry what logic is to thinking; it will
not help a man to make poetry, but it will
help a poet to correctness, and help the
reader to perceive the more delicate beauties
of vei’se.
So far the theorists have been at war
with one another over the rules, particularly
the vital one of measui’ement by feet, syl-
lables or accent. Guest, the author of a
large History of English Rhythm, was an-
noyed with some of the poets because they
had written many lines which could not be
fitted to his preconceived system. Saints-
bury has surveyed the whole procession of
English poetry down to Swinburne, together
with the works of all the prosodists, and
then derived a theory from his examination.
His theory is that English verse can best
be scanned by a system of feet —long and
short, with an allowance for substitution of
equivalent feet, and the variation of the
middle or internal pause.
Saintsbury gives the rules of his foot
system, and then applies them to English
verse from Beowulf onwards. A glossary
fully explains the jargon used in the scien-
tific examination of verse —such terms as
“anacrusis,” “enjambment,” “galliambic,”
etc. There is a good bibliography of works
relating to metre, and “a reasoned list of
poets, with special regard to their prosodic
quality and influence.” Robert Browning,
for instance, is dealt with in this fashion:
“Often described as a loose and rugged
metrist and a licentious if not criminal
rhymester. Nothing of the sort. Extraor-
dinarily bold in both capacities, and some-
times, perhaps, as usually happens in these
cases, a little too bold ; but in metre practi-
cally never, in rhyme very seldom ....
overstepping actual bounds. A great mas-
ter of broken metres, internal rhyme, heavily
equivalenced lines and all the tours de force
of English prosody.”
.This manual is very good reading for any-
one who is interested in the art of poetry,
and Saintsbury compels agreement with
his main arguments in support of a body of
doctrine—“subject not to the practically in-
variable uniformity of Science, but to the
wide variations of Art.” The '“laws” of
poetry, of course, .are derived from poetry,
and are liable to be extended or broken when
a new poet is big enough to do so.
“Pollio” informed us recently of a meet-
ing in London, at which Australian poetry
burst into Society. The way had been pre-
pared by a number of articles in such jour-
nals as United Empire and The Empire .Re-
view, and the February number of the latter
contains ■ a rambling commentary by Flor-
ence M. Burton, in which Australian poets
are served up with jam and tears. The
names of most of them are mangled—-but,
“Ivnuckleboy or Nickleby, it’ll be all the
same in a hundred years,” as Mrs. Squeers
wisely remarked. The most strangely
gifted of all our bards, who is spoken of
by admiring craftsmen as “Rare George
Reid,” is praised by Miss Burton for uphold-
ing the tradition of poetry and politics. This
glittering gem from his magnum opus, the
Exhibition Ode, adorns the tail of her ar-
ticle :
Alas, the space that shields thee from all
foreign harms,
Hides from the outer world thy most attrac-
tive charms.
Indeed in Paris but a short time back
Some of thy daughters wei - e disowned be-
cause not black.
How to dispel such ignorance has long been
felt
The hardest task with which our pati’iots
e’er dealt.
A happy inspix-ation and a bold replies—
Now let a Universal Exposition arise!
• * * *
A writer in the Queensland Worker, com-
menting on the two pictures reproduced
here from Norman Lindsay’s illustrations
of “Petronius,” says that they d 6 not carry
him further away than Woolloomooloo.
“That the city of Nero had its Woolloo-
mooloo we may be sure. But it was its own,
not ours.”
No one denies the Q. Worker man’s right
to speak authoritatively of the W’loo of
to-day, but what does he know about
that of Nero’s Rome? “Mankind pro-
gresses,” said Goethe, “but man remains the
same,” and if Lindsay’s characters resemble
men and women of to-day they are there-
fore likely to resemble similar people of
the past. It is in the minor matters of the
short shirts and the long noses that change
has taken place, and Lindsay thoroughly
studied his text, the architectural and other
remains of Nero’s time, in order to get his-
torical accuracy.
• * * *
Ishmael Dare: Daley’s last volume has
set me re-reading letters he wrote me when
the first was being printed. I had been
worrying him with cheerfully audacious
criticism, which he took with remarkable
good temper, even verging on humility now
and then. But he was never yielding about
his best work; I made suggestions—l am
glad I don’t remember what they were—
with regard to some re-writing of the
Hawkesbury pieces, and he wrote back:
Probably you are critically right in sug-
gesting other arrangements, but I have a
feeling that I am instinctively right.
He was.
The title of his book bothered him more
than anything else. The green pages full
of fine purple script—the color makes his
letters easy to track, but wearies the eye—
are mainly concerned with titles. One let-
ter contains nine, with a tenth in a post-
script ; a friend had sent along more, but
“they all contained the dreadful word
Eucalypt,” My own suggestion “Dreams
in Amber,” he thought pretty, but not likely
to strike the public,, so he flung out his
ten, beginning with the one finally adopted,
At Dawn and Dusk (“this isn’t very strik-
ing, either”) and ending, in the postscript,
with RabesquerOt (“too recondite for the
ordinary reader; but it might be all the
better for that, as suggestive of mystery”).
Wine and Roses, which turns up in another
letter, was, I think, his favorite:
It gives a suggestion of color. Too arro-
gant, perhaps? Anyhow, it is the best I
can imagine. A number of my literary and
professional friends here admire it.
It was at first proposed to have his por-
trait as a frontispiece, but he fought against
that. “In such a place,\ he wrote, “it
seems to me like a sort of patent medicine
advertisement, which should be accompanied
by the legend:
READ THIS. IT WILL CURE YOU OF
CONSTIPATION FOR EVER.
DO YOU SUFFER FROM GOUT, LUM-
BAGO OR SCIATICA? HERE IS AN
INFALLIBLE SPECIFIC.
IF ONE VOLUME DOES NOT CURE
YOU, TRY A SECOND. THE BOOK-
SELLERS HAVE STACKS.

CAPT. H. E. U. BURKE.
An African
Letter to ...
CLEMENTS TONIC
Testimony received from Capt. H. E. U. BURKE, late
3rd King’s African Rifles, British E. Africa, and
Assistant Master, Sydney Grammar School, College
Street, Sydney. States his opinion of what Clements
Tonic can do in cases of Fever, Wasting Illness, Loss
»f Sleep or Appetite, Indigestion, or Convalescence
after severe illness.
“ The Cottage,” 250 Ocean Street,
Paddington, 17/l/Tl.
CLEMENTS TONIC LTD.
“ I have benefited much by your medicine, principally in Africa
where it was first given to me by an Australian, after a severe attack of
malarial fever. I was taken tq Nairobi, the centre of Govt, in British
E. Africa, in a state of collapse. The greatest care had to be taken
during my convalescence.
“My Australian friend gave me three bottles. It improved my
appetite, creating desire for food, the beginning of a general restoration
to health, transforming a condition of extreme debility to one of
splendid strength.
“ I was so impressed with the medicine that I recommended it to
my brother officers. I have cause to be very grateful to this excellent
medicine, and, as I am now in Sydney, I am only fulfilling a duty in
testifying to its worth. You may use this letter as you like.”
(Signed) H. E. U. BURKE (Capt.).

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Work of the Wharves.
The ships are sunk and the crews are lost, and ever
the news is heard
Of this ship “Missing,” of that one “Wrecked,” of
the other “Still no word.”
But the Work of the World can’t wait for these—
the Factory, Loom and Mill,
So the wharves are never idle, and the derricks are
never still.
Along the wharves the liners lie—the long sheds fill
Fill arid "empty—swept to-day, and crammed again
to-night. , . ,
While clattering donkey-engine and clanking der-
rick-chain , .
Toil fiercely on through day and dusk, and glaring
arc-lamp’s light. . ~
Dust and heat and sunshine; wind and cold
and rain— , , . . .
They don’t count; hook on and hoist away.
Rush and clash and clamor; ceaseless stress
and strain,
So the wharves work on from day to day.
The men that drive the tunnel, fall the bush and
hold the plough,
The men that yard the scrubbers and the men
that shear the sheep,
Are all at work to send the stuff the wharves must
handle now,
To keep the winches screaming and the steamers
loading deep.
Lead and wheat and timber; meat and hides
and wool,
Sling ’em in and stow ’em down below.
Slack away your hawsers, as soon ’s your holds
are full.
“All clear aft!” so hurry up and go!
With crews half dead from work and wet and want
of food and sleep, ...
The sailing ships beat slowly in and reach the
And threshing up from leeward, with her schedule
time to keep, . , ._ j
The steamer runs her beaten route, through wind
or sea or tide. ,
Through toil and death and danger; through
, the risks they always run,
Above, and on, and underneath the seas,
Through blizzard, fog and ice-pack, or the
Tropics’ savage sun,
They come to drop their cargoes on our
quays.
A dainty Island schooner in the tideway gently
(Oh, the creaming surf along the reef; the still
lagoon within!) ,
And an ugly box-built cargo tramp, straight out o
London docks. ,
(Black oily water, smoky skies, and traffic s
1 vibrant din !)
Copra from the Coral Isles, boilers out o Brum,
All that earth can grow or man can make
Is cargo for the carriers that straight and
swiftly come.
To pile the wharves with all we give and
take.
A cargo swung aboard, perhaps, from a tossing
Kroo canoe
(Hushed Hinterland and roaring beach; long
days and fevered nights),
Or a lonely little jetty in the fjord still and blue.
(The sweet pine- scent, the clear, chill air and
flickering Northern Lights.)
From port and pier aaid roadstead ; from beach
and river-mouth
Across the way, or o’er the world so wide,
From the Humber or the Hooghli; from East,
West, North or South,
They’re coming in along our waterside.
Every nation’s speech, and oaths of all the seas
between,
Men of every color, creed and country, town and
g£ a £g
Who know the far-set cities as a boy his village-
green,
And a hundred harbors better than their own
front garden gate.
Dutchmen, Don and Dagp, coolie and Chm-ee,
Jews and Cockneys, Conways spruce and
neat —
The World’s Wide-Open Doorway is set along
obr quay,
And our string-piece is the Doorstep to its
feet.
A statesman fails, or an Empire falls, and ever the
swift word flies
. Of a nation’s might, of an army’s fight, of a king
who is crowned —or dies.
But the roaring looms tare still to feed, and the
hungry mouths to fill,
So the wharves are nevc.r idle and the derricks are
never still.
MAORILAND.
BOYD CABLE.
When We Are One.
When we are one, and in One Being lave,
For what desire’s fulfilment shall We crave ?
Is there for such an One in Being’s plan
Another mate like woman is to man ?
Or what shall be the poles of our desires?
An unimagined sex in astral fires ?
Or merely glow and .heat and joy and pan ,
Female and male, tlieir dramas o’er again?
Shall syjiphic fervors rise from passion’s mud?
Or oeld 1 angelic flames, transformed to blood,
Course through the ,veins our duplex form will
hold,
And warmer sylphs sir fairer mortals mould ?
Which shall We be, "the seeker or the sought,
The man, the maid, the hunter or the caught?
Shall We allure strong Him, for whom We sigh
With feigned retreat, with liquid lighted eye,
With pillowing curve, with soft adornment’s toil,
With lens-like robe* t*> magnify the spoil?
Pluck from some Phoenix plumes to make Our hair
A flame of love for moth-wings fluttering there?
Or shall we sight im Home star-cluster Her,
In skins arrayed or elfin gossamer,
And glow through all Our being when She flies,
And, stumbling over worlds, o’ertake Our prize,
As happy from the ra«.:e as if ’twere run
Here, long ago, ere :man and maid were One ?

SCIENTIFIC GLEANINGS=Liver Pill Talks
No. 1.
What every woman wants.
It is no use being well gowned if you have a blotchy
complexion.
A hobble skirt will not hide a pimple on your nose.
A pimple takes away your self-respect.
The being has to be born who can be dignified and
at the same time harbor a pimple.
Your pleasure is gone because you are thinking
about it all the time. You take surreptitious peeps into
mirrors.
You are wondering when it is going to burst.
It is Nature’s signal that your blood is out of order.
A course of FISHER’S BEAUTY PILLS for the Liver,
which will cost you a shilling, will remove the pimple,
and prevent others coming.
Don’t be afraid because the price is so small; they
don’t cost much money, but they do a power of good.
Give them a trial ; they go to the root of the trouble.
No. 2.
Bad blood saps your store of energy.
You need all your energy.
Your success in life depends upon it.
if you go into your office jaded and out of tone you
are a passenger. Someone else has to carry you.
You put in all your time watching the clock.
Clock watchers in an, age of competition, where
profits are fined down, are parasites. Parasites in the
long run get just what they deserve.
It is nothing to the employer that you are out of
sorts. He sees the effect. The cause is not his busi-
ness. But it is yours.
Take a course of FISHER’S BEAUTY PILLS for the
Liver; they make the blood run bright red.
They kill impurities in the blood by so gentle a pro-
cess that you don’t notice it. You only know that you
are another man.
Try them ; they go to the root of the trouble.
No. 3.
Is the previous night worth the next morning ?
You had a good time last night, but you are off-color
this morning, because you drank too much.
You have a tongue like the bottom of a bird-cage,
and you make only a pretence of eating the breakfast
chop.
You have already drunk the contents of the water
jug, and you still wish your tea-cup was a dipper—
you wouldn’t have to pass it down to the other end of
the table so often then.
You wish you hadn’t met Brown last night. Brown,
with his “Just another, old chap,” is responsible for
your trouble this morning.
But you will be always meeting Brown, so when you
are going to bed take a dose of FISHER’S BEAUTY
PILLS for the Liver.
in the morning you will have no “ head,” no bird-
cage tongue, a good appetite, and you will be able to
look your wife clear in the eye.
Give them a trial. They cost the price of two drinks,
and we will not ask you for a testimonial. Just tell
Brown and the others.

Beautiful, Clear Complexion and Healthy Liver Regained in One Week.
MISS B. BROWN’S RELIABLE TESTIMONY.
For some three or four months I suffered very badly from what I believe was an inactive liver. On and off I had a dull pain between
the shoulders. In the daytime I felt heavy and drowsy, whilst at night I was restless and wakeful. About every fortnight I had a bilious
attack, with severe sick headaches, when I couldn’t so much as look at my food. Often after meals my food would seem to stick in my chest
in a hard lump, giving me great pain, and causing wind ; indeed, I felt sick after almost every meal. Sometimes a sort of blackness would
come before my eyes, and a kind of dizziness \ r ould seize me, and I would be compelled to lie down. In the morning my tongue was furred
and discolored, and I felt dead tired the moment I got out of bed. My eyes, people said, looked.dull and heavy, my complexion became sallow
and sickly-looking, and, worse than all, a large number of pimples made their appearance all over my face. So unsightly were these erup-
tions that I d.dn’t care to go. out, except at night-time. People recommended different things, such as Epsom Salts, Seidlitz powders, claret,
and various well-known tonics. I tried these things, but found no improvement in my condition, and when a friend got me to take “Fisher’s
Beauty Pills for the Liver,” I didn’t expect anything worth mentioning. I was mistaken, however, for a dozen of these pills made quite a dif-
ference. I didn’t feel bilious or heavy any more, and I lost the headache and the pain in the back, and slept well. I took some more
BEAUTY PILLS for the Liver, and one morning (about a week after starting the pills) woke to find the pimples disappearing. In two days
more my complexion and my eyes were perfectly bright and clear, as they always are when I am in good health. Before the bottle
of BEAUTY PILLS was finished I was in perfect health, and felt as lively and cheerful as ever I did in my life.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, APRIL 13, 1911.
THE CONFLICTING VOICES AND WHAT
THEY SUGGEST.
The Referenda campaign is unique among
political fights in one respect. Nearly all
the opponents of the enlarged powers asked
for by the National Government claim to be
in favor of some of them —while many ap-
prove of most of them. It is thus pos-
sible, in any Tory gathering, to strike an
elector who is enthusiastic about the New
Protection, another who believes in railway
employees having a right of appeal to the
Federal Arbitration Court, and another who
goes bald-headed for Commonwealth control
of monopolies. Yet all of them, for one
vague, muddled reason or another, are dog-
gedly resolved to vote “No, No,” on April 26.
* * * *
It is natural, when one comes to study it.
Mankind is utterly selfish in the mass. When
factory legislation w r as first mooted, there
were thousands of right-thinking people
who, as a matter of principle, simply loathed
the idea of working-women being sweated
amid insanitary surroundings. But, for all
that, they opposed the specific laws which
were designed to bring relief to these unfor-
tunates. They had their bank accounts to
consider: and so they fought the Factory
Acts like well-nourished tigers, damning
them as flagrant samples of red-flag Social-
ism. and as manifestations that Private En-
terprise was doomed, and that the country
was careering down a steep place to ruin.
But the bulk of the population was not per-
sonally and financially concerned in the
sweating trade, so the protesters went down,
and the Factory Acts became law.
* * * *
Much the same thing is occurring in con-
nection with the Referenda proposals.
Hordes of Anti-Laborites approve warmly of
the New Protection. They may be identified
any day, with their halos adjusted, and the
garb of self-righteousness enwrapping them,
smugly denouncing a state of things under
which the manufacturer is able to reap the
full financial benefits of the tariff, and con-
tinue to pay his workmen on a Freetrade
wage-basis. N. S. Wales foreign-traders,
for example, are as clamantly in favor of
the New Protection as any Laborite. Ex-
Minister Garland and Ex-Hero Wade give
it an encouraging word every time they
orate. The reason is that the prosperous
suburban parts of Sydney—which, in the
eyes of our Wades and Garlands, connote
the Commonwealth of Australia —aren’t in-
habited to any great extent by manufac-
turers ; and so the representatives of these
centres can afford to be frank about at least
the New Protection feature of the “Social-
ists’ ” policy.
* ■» * *
Very different is the attitude of the Mel-
bourne Age, and the folks who support it.
The Age, to-day, is the Victorian manufac-
turers’ organ, and little else. The thought
of New Protection, an arrangement under
which its chief patrons would be forced, in
every case, to pay decent wages, or go out
of the highly-Proteeted manufacturing busi-
ness, afflicts the Age with a cold sweat. It
can stand a number of the amendments pro-
posed by the Australian Government, but the
reflection that, with them, it must swallow
the New Protection horror, causes it to
revolt at the lot. Here is the Age point
of view, as expressed in a recent leader: —
If each question had been put separately some
would, very possibly, have been carried by a large
majority, it is certain that others would have been
overwhelmingly negatived. Mr. Hughes saw this,
and he acted accordingly. In the Industrial Refer-
endum, therefore, he included a proposal that the
Federation should have supreme power to deal with
combinations, trusts and monopolies. ' . . . The
reason is obvious. Mr. Hughes effected this to in-
fuse a spurious species of popularity into the In-
dustrial Referendum, and to prevent those who want
the Federation to have full power over monopolies
and trusts from'- gaining their desire except by simul-
taneously voting “Yes” to three other questions,
which threaten to smash the Constitution in frag-
ments.
So, on tlie Sydney side, we have the
Wades and Garlands, and other “No-
Noes” saying that Isew Protection is a first-
rate thing, and it ij only the proposal to
deal with monopolies that is bad ; while on
the Melbourne side we have the Age de-
nouncing the New Protection as an abomin-
able thing, and declaring that it is only the
proposal to deal with monopolies that, is
good. The Sydney “No-Noes,” in the first
place, deny that Australia possesses any-
thing resembling an oppressive combine, or
other such conspiracy. The possibility of
a Meat or other Trust being formed here on
Yankee lines, they declare to be a bogey,
fabricated by brilliant special pleaders of the
B. R. Wise stamp. The Coal Trust is
limned as a benevolent concern, which would
not exploit the public, even if it had an
army to back its manipulations. The ab-
surdly affluent, and none the less predatory,
Gas, Ferry and Sugar monopolies are re-
presented as honest, hard-working concerns,
which are only reaping the legitimate fruits
of their business skill and foresight. It
is claimed that they should not be legislat-
ively interfered with, no matter how their
dividends rise, or how much the people pay,
in consequence, for the necessaries of life.
These people are quite in favor of interfering
with the manufacturer —because in Sydney
he is scarce, and not very powerful—but
they are quite opposed to interfering with
the monopolist, because in N.S.W. Anti-
Sosh, which is Liberalism, which is State
Frightism, is largely interested in coal,
sugar, ferries and gas. If the Victorian
Anti-Laborite, whose sentiments are voiced
by the Age, were personally concerned to
any extent in an Australian monopoly, his
attitude to the monopolies section of the Re-
ferenda questions would, for a certainty, be
just the same as that of N.S.W. “Liberal-
ism.” But he isn’t. When a Melbourne
Age writer or reader speaks of “trusts and
monopolies,” he only means one thing—
the Melbourne Metropolitan Gas Company.
And to the Age and to AGE-ites the Mel-
bourne Gas Co. is Swinburne ; and any-
thing connected with Swinburne merits
every fragment of trouble that is going.
There is a useful lesson in all this. The
leit-motiv of every Tory electioneering howl
in the past, has been —“The Labor policy
is rotten to the core, and there is no justice'
or sanity in it.” In the present campaign,
however, Toryism doesn’t pretend that the
proposals of Labor are iniquitous, and crude,
and anarchical, and generally impossible.
Speaking in a weirdly divided tone of voice,
Toryism alleges, in fact, that most of the
amendments asked for are perfectly sound
and reasonable. But Adipose, M.L.C., who
has a factory somewhere in Victoria, reckons
it is criminal folly for the Australian Govern-
ment to seek power to pass laws which must
mulct him in increased wages, and Fatte,
of Sydney, whose Gas shares return him an
inordinate dividend, considers the possibility
of cheap gas an outrage, and the beginning
of the end. In other words, even chronic
Anti-Laborites, in a large number of cases,
only claim that the Government is asking
for too much where it asks for powers which
may be used to the detriment of their par-
ticular vested interests. It is a novel situ-
ation, which should make those electors who
are unbiassed by any vested interest think
hard, and act accordingly.
A MINISTER CHANGES HORSES ON
THE BRINK OF THE CORONATION.
Just before hurrying aboard the vessel
which took him to the Coronation, Defence
Minister Pearce rearranged the duties of the
Military Board. Henceforward Colonel
Wallack, the Adjutant-General, will ad-
minister the Universal Training Scheme, in
place of Lieut.-Colonel Legge, the Quarter-
master-General, who has had charge of it
hitherto. Legge will draw up the uncom-
pleted portion of the regulations referring to
adult training, and then turn to other
work. Beyond remarking vaguely that the
new ukase “appears to make the Minister s
power absolute,” the daily papers see little
significance in it. As a matter of tact, it
not only contains that possibility, but others
considerably more dangerous.
* * * *
Under the absurd party system of govern-
ment which Australia adopted from England
when it was too young to know better, the
great offices of State are mostly held by Min-
isters who have no knowledge whatever of
the technicalities of their departments. That
is how it becomes possible for a King
O’Malley to be Minister for Home Affairs,
and a Joe Cook to be Minister for Defence.
Senator Pearce is, by general consent, the
ablest Defence Minister the Commonwealth
has known. But he was brought up to be
a carpenter, and instruction in the intricacies
of the art or game of War is no part of the
curriculum in the carpentering trade. Hence
the value to him of a Military Board of ex-
perts. So long as the existing system of
government prevails, it is desirable that the
Minister should appreciate his limitations,
and lean on his knowledgable advisers. And
that being so, it follows that the advisers
should be the best available.
The question arises: Is Colonel Wallace;
the best man available to help the Defence
Minister to lick Australia’s embryo army in-
to shape ? In other words: Is he more
likely to be a success at the job than the
man he has displaced? The soundest test
lies in a contrast between Legge’s record
and that of his successor. Legge has been
a battler for the compulsory-service principle
since the days when to mention such
a subject in club, importing, legal, or
other allegedly-polite circles, meant the
stirring up of curses. and the un-
loosing of a flood of wild talk about “dis-
loyalty to the Empire,” “white slavery,”
“un-English practices,” and so on. As pub-
lic opinion grew more educated, successive
Governments started to sit up and take
notice of the “Conscriptionists’ ” sug-
gestions. Universal service schemes were
called for. Legge formulated every one of
them, from the first, which was supplied to
the Deaicin Government when Ewing was
Defence Minister, to that which was under
consideration when Kitchener arrived
here. The only important detail in which the
Kitchener scheme, as finally adopted, dif-
fered from the Legge scheme, consisted in
raising the age at which adult training ended
from 20 to 25. As to Legge’s universal
service administration, the Minister for De-
fence had this to say in Melbourne when he
announced the reconstruction of the Board :
He recognises the valuable and self-sacrificing
work that has been done by Colonel Leoge as direc-
tor of operations. In giving effect to the change, he
wishes it to be distinctly recognised that he is
animated by no lack either of appreciation or of con-
fidence in that officer or his work. On account of
his past duties, Colonel Legge must necessarily, he
recognises, be more in touch with the intentions of
the Government as to the details of the scheme than
any other officer, and he feels sure that the various
members of the Board, who will be called upon to
carry out the duties belonging to their particular
branch, will be able at all times to rely upon his
advice and. assistance.
#*.'**
Colonel Wallack is an ex-Militia man
(Middlesex Regiment), who was appointed
Adjutant of the Forces in Tasmania shortly
after he arrived there from England.
Such enthusiasm as he possesses for the com-
pulsory service idea, is of recent growth.
And he has made no particular punch as an
administrator. Why should the admittedly
capable Legge be ousted from an office for
which, as the result of a lifetime’s experi-
ence, he is peculiarly fitted, in order to
make room for a more or less unknown quan-
tity? The answer is bound up mysteriously
in the “Minister’s absolute power” above-
mentioned, in Inspector-General Kirk-
patrick, in the genial pliability of Colonel
Wallack, and other things.
* * * *
No one questions the ability of Inspector-
General Kirkpatrick. His is probably
as active, hard-working and capable
an officer as Australia could have imported
to inspect the administration of its forces.
The trouble is, he is not content to inspect.
He yearns with the intense yearning of the
self-confident, energetic and ambitious man
to direct as well. For months it has been
understood in Victorian military circles that
Defence Minister Pearce had a “ghost”;
and there was a suspicion that the spectre
was Kirkpatrick. The recommendations of
the Military Board have been repeatedly
overridden. Thus the recent reduction in
the retiring age of officers —a policy which
is reasonable enough in the United Kingdom,
where there are plenty of senior officers, but
which has few points in Australia, where
senior officers are painfully scarce —was done
in the teeth of the Board’s opposition. The
Minister has been betraying a knowledge of
military details which has astounded his
intimates. The reasonable assumption is that,
though the voice was the voice of Pearce,
the hand that pulled the wires was connected
with the body of Kirkpatrick.
* * * *
If, as is pretty certain, the Defence Minis-
ter’s parting act was inspired by the Inspec-
tor-General, the position has some serious
aspects so far as Australia is concerned,
The completes! sympathy is known to exist
between the Inspector-General and Colonel
Wallack. Wallack is not what is known
as “a strong man.” Kirkpatrick is. In
other words, Colonel Wallack’B universal-
training administration will probably be, to
all intents and purposes, the Inspector-Gen-
eral’s administration. An official may be
an invaluable figure at the helm in England,
India, or wherever else he happens to : have
special knowledge, and yet be the very reverse
in a country whose local conditions, senti-
ments, conventions and views are strange to
him. The Inspector-General, who, by the
present look of things, will stand behind
Colonel Wallack, as masteriully as he has
recently been standing behind the Minister
who appointed Colonel Wallack to his
present position, has in the nature of
things little sympathy or acquaint-
ance with the Australian attitude.
He is gifted with the highest qualifications
to act generally as critic, adviser, in-
structor. or what not to the Australian
forces, inasmuch as they are modelled on
the British plan, and in Britain the Inspec-
tor-General is recognised as one who knows
his job. But when it comes to taking a
hand, directly or indirectly, in details of ad-
ministration, he is as likely as any other
comparatively recent importation to hash
things up.
A couple of instances will suffice. In or-
der to successfully carry out the junior por-
tion of the training, the co-operation of the
State Educational departments and the
teachers needs to be obtained. In mam-
cases, owing to the violent State Frights
prejudices that have been fomented for poli-
tical purposes by “Liberalism,” there is an
unwillingness on the part of the Educational
authorities to help the Federal Government
in any way whatever. That is only going to
be overcome by diplomacy, backed by know-
ledge of the representations that are most
likely to be effective with obstructionists, in-
differentists and others. It is a task at
which the brightest of oversea soldiers might
be expected to fail. On July 12, 1912, the
first lot of senior cadets is due to pass into
the adult forces. That is to say, in a year’s
time, 20,000 ex-cadets are to amalgamate
with the existing 25,000 militia —a process
which is to continue yearly till the 120,000
total recommended by Kitchener is reached.
It is evident that the initial difficulties of
this undertaking must be enormous. Not
only has an army of boys to be grafted on
to an army of men, but the breaking up of
many old units, and the creation of new r
ones, and the severing of all sorts of regi-
mental ties must be accomplished at the
same time, and without undue friction. Local
sentiment and prejudices, and personal and
regimental resentments and jealousies, will
need to be placated, or there will be trouble.
It is a job, if ever there was one, for an offi-
cer who has lived his life among the people
concerned.
Even if there were no delicate situation
inseparable from the institution of a
compulsory-service scheme in a country
like Australia, it would still be de-
sirable to lay down the principle at
the very beginning o 2 things that no In-
spector-General, howevifc: distinguished, shall
be suffered to take a hand in administrative
work. It is not his province. And though,
at times, his interference might prove useful,
yet there are circumstances, as in the case
under discussion, where it might easily be
disastrous. The importation of an incom-
petent Inspector-General is conceivable;
with precedents to support him in directing
matters outside his own sphere, he could
bring infinite trouble on the country.
THE REBELLION.
Randolph Bedford speaks a piece
A heavy range sloped to the sea —a great avenue
of eucalypts growing wondrously, and in contempt
of the fact that they were 10,000 miles from Aus-
tralia, the country of their origin. They half hid'
the wrecked mine buildings of the Argentella, or, as
it is called by the Corsican, Caprice d’lnglisi—“the
madness of the English”—by reason of a quarter Of
a million of London money having been spent to
equip a “mine” which yielded 100 tons or so of
galena, and no more. And here in Corsica grew the
eucalypts, tenacious of their Australian habit, and
flowering triumphantly in the winter they refused to
recognise. Here, within 20 miles of Calvi, one of
the birth-places of Christopher Columbus, and
10,000 miles from mine own land, I found a proof
that the rebellion which is to come is founded on
natural facts as much as on a growing national sen-
timent.
The weaker plants of the Northern Hemisphere
have no such strength or obstinacy. The wheat
must follow climate. January ends the harvest
of Australia. Egypt reaps in March; Syria,
Mexico and Persia in April ; Central Asia, Morocco,
China and Japan in May; California, Spain, Sicily
and Southern Europe reap in June; England, Ne-
braska and Northern Europe in July; Manitoba,
Holland, Denmark and Russia in August; the islands
of the North Sea in September; and so on. And
the fruits of the whole world come to us in our
summer, taking the line of least resistance, and just
strong enough to be adaptable. But the Australian
trees bloom on, and the Australian black swan breed
in their Australian season when transported to
cold, dark England, or the more temperate
Tyrrhenian sea. It can be cold in Cor-
sica as in Norway—yet there I saw the crim-
son-flowered gum putting forth his flame of bloom,
defying the tramontana that blew chillingly off the
hills of snow. And in Regent’s Park, London,
the black swan nests and broods in the winter. In
Australia this is not true of the imported oak. In
the Fitzroy Gardens, Melbourne, every .successive
winter finds the oak postponing the falling of the
leaf; assimilating itself to the Australian habit;
becoming gradually evergreen; holding its last
year’s half-dead leaves until it is almost ready for
breaking into the new.
Southern environment is strong enough to alter
types always for the better, improvingly. The red
deer in Maoriland, the rabbit in Australia, are
bigger and finer and more productive than in Scot-
land or England. Queenslanders know how com-
mon and authenticated the story that station horses
in the “boiling down” days fattened on the lean
meat in the tips; Maorilanders say that the horse
which draws the oyster trucks at the Bluff eats his
dozen or two whenever he can get them, although
he does not like them on the shell.
» * * *
Australia is the hardest, kindest, cruellest, and
most beneficent country of the earth. The real
Australian —not the little Australian-born person of
the cities —is the best type of man on earth, be-
cause he survives its recurrent and brutal unkind-
nesses. At the cost of a few million years of ex-
periment, Australia has produced the Flinders and
Mitchell grasses of the great rolling downs of Cen-
tral and Nor’-West Queensland— grasses which ma-
ture in the dry time into a self-made hay, which an
inch of rain at the wrong time will blacken and
rot. The geranium fodder-plant of Central Aus-
tralia has a seed shaped like an Archimidean screw;
when the country becomes a dust-heap, the winds
screw the seed home to wait for the rains in a re-
gion where 6in. and no more is the annual average.
On the plains the melon vine and the cucumber
dry and blow to dust, the soil cracks like slaking
lime, and the seeds fall into security to await their
season.
The great Australian quality is resilience. As the
country is, so are the real men of it capable of en-
durance to the verge of collapse, and then recovering
without effort. At 10 or 11 year intervals a four
years drought sweeps the continent. For scores
of thousands of square miles the country becomes
a vast dust-heap, whose poverty evicts even the
crows and the “gohannas.” And at the moment
when the land seems to be condemned, with the
death-warrant on its way to delivery, up comes the
brave south wind, with its wings dripping water,
and down from the north sweeps the depression of
the monsoon. An inch of rain consolidates the dust,
another inch makes mud; and although the drought-
smitten cattle have dug up all the grass roots, and
all seeds have apparently perished, yet oyer
the earth a sickly greenness springs with the third
shower. A few weeks later the dust-heap is a mea-
dow, a garden, a great and gracious park, and the


store cattle which have been hurriedly trucked back
from the crowded relief country wallow in luxury
until the yellow fat spreads over their ribs and casts
them for death. And all forms of life rush to re-
populate the new world —bird, butterfly, insect and
animal—everywhere, more than in any other coun-
try, the boundless egotism of self-preservation and
the colossal conceit of reproduction.
And this is a country that teaches its struggling
life new ways to live. The rifle fish earns his living
by the accuracy of his aim in shooting a grasshopper
with a drop of water, so stunning the prey and
bringing it within reach; the seed of =pear-grass is
taught to lie quietly in the dust in the dry time,
and to spring into energy of real movement, which
might almost be termed muscular action, as soon
as it is moistened —a fact which accounts for the
death of many sheep, whose wool-yolk moistens the
grass seed, and keeps it moving; and the ceratodus
fish on occasion climbs trees. Australia has taught
the Australian new ways, too, although he clings
slavishly to many superstitions utterly foreign to
the conditions of this country. Regard them —these
dull agricultural “pioneers.” Unable to see the wondrous
beauty of their new land, they imported gorse and
sweetbriar, to make it look like the old little patch-
work quilt country they had left, and so ruined
a few million acres in New South Wales; some
other homesick idiot introduced the prickly pear;
and yet other imbeciles brought the rabbit and
the thistle, and the sparrow, and the starling, and
the fox.
But, worse than any weed or vermin the nostalgia
of the thick-headed Englishman brought to Australia,
are their superstitions, which make the Australian
Sunday drearier than the Sunday of Glasgow; their
traditions, which retard the growth of an Australian
national sentiment; their stodginess in politics, lit-
erature or art. Their habits of living become farci-
cal when applied to Australian conditions. The
reason why the English like “Robbery Under Arms,”
and call it the “finest Australian novel,” is because
it is not Australian, but English. Boldrewood
never had the Australian outlook, never could think
Australianly. His British township is the English village
with the name of Boobybingie, instead of Gobbleham-
bury; his bushranger is left pulling his forelock to a
transplanted “squire.” Regard them—the dull, daily
papers that fatten on our pennies. Regard them —
many of our politicians: the men with never a word
for Australia, but only interminable gush for “Em-
pire” ; the shoddy seekers after foreign titles —pre-
pared to sell the best interests of their country at any
moment for the Windsor equivalent of the savages’
hoop-iron and three beads; the small sub-municipal
intelligences, which approach the, consideration of
national issues with the broad statesmanship of a
pound-keeper.
* * * *
It has long been the habit to believe that England
gave us our free institutions. The belief is false.
The English and Scotch and Irish who had come to
Australia, and who were aided by Americans and
Frenchmen and Italians and all the cream of the ad-
venturous men of the earth, did that; we had to
have a Eureka before fat-headed English officialism
saw that it had to deal with men who were better
than the men who governed them. To abolish eon-
victism in Australia, Australians had to turn back
a few shiploads of felonry that sought to be de-
barked at Sydney Cove. The superstition of Eng-
lish Government allowed us to sit quietly while
Derby in Downing-street disallowed Mcllwraith’s
annexation of New Guinea for Australia, and left the
Nor’-Western Pacific open to Germany. Yet it was
a beneficent stupidity; better the white German
than any piebald ally of England and natural foe of
Australia.
It is against the worship of imported habit and
belief that the Australian must rebel, as he rebels
against a fiscal system which makes him a digger
up and a shearer of raw material, a wood and water
joey for the manufacturing artisans of the earth.
Sectarianism —dead as a political force in the Old
World —fluorished like the rabbit under the condi-
tions of the new land, and for a time almost abso-
lutely banished political things out of politics. The
foolish habit of wearing North Pole clothing in an
Australian summer, of eating great masses of beef
and heavy pudding on Christmas Day, because the
Saxons did so to keep out the December cold —all
these things must we rebel against, and quickly
and decisively.
The Australian Sabbath is as dreary as the Sab-
bath at Stirling—where a Scotchman one Sunday led
me enthusiastically to the cemetery to be amused
and uplifted by rain and sleet falling on the graves.
The people own the libraries and art galleries, yet
for 20 years the great majority of the owners were
unable to see their own property on their one day
of leisure. A wave of cant has swept across the
Southern States, and slandering pulpiteers have fallen
again into their native obscurity; but only by reason
of the fact that they went too far. Indiscriminate,
ill-regulated gambling is an evil just as much as
drunkenness; but the only thing to be done with
gambling is to regulate it by State-owned totalisa-
tors; just as drunkenness is in part curable by pen-
alising the adulterators of liquor. But these drunken
teetotalers sodden with uncharitableness must
have the opposite effect to that they allege they
seek; although, apparently, they seek really that
audience which they cannot get by staying in their
churches. As a result the Southern States are dis-
figured with little associations of Pharisees —hypo-
critical busybodies, with a double component of
malice to fill in the place that in the ordinary
human is filled with tolerance. That kind of “good
man,” who is made of two buckets of sawdust and
a bucket of water, some envy, and a few tracts, is
very common in Australia. He cannot, unfortu-
nately, be deported; so the rebellion against the
minority he represents must be speedy and thorough.
* * * *
One of these killjoy organisations some time ago
proposed as a recommendation from its Purity
Department,” that the publication and exhibition of
picture studies in the nude, or of statues, should be
prohibited, as they were “generally from models of
our fallen sisters, and were a source of danger to
the young.” In a country that is destined by its
climate and its sunlight to produce people of Latin
temperament, in a country that must sing and must
write verse, and must paint and make statues,
simply because the joy of life is strong and craves
expression in one or other of these forms, the dirty,
Puritanical sentimentalism of the mid-Victorian
times should surely find no place. And yet it does.
The superstition of the indecency of the decent
human body is as usual in Australia as is that
horror for sitting down upon the mahoganv-framed,
horsehair-seated chair and the room that it lives in;
with the antimacassars which belong to the time when
men wore ringlets and cocoanut hair oil —with the
fat mahogany table, supported by one central and
swollen leg, and covered with a black and red table-
cloth and decorated with a mat of many-colored
wools; and thereupon seven pounds weight of the
AVor'd, brass-bound, and never opened.
It is the dull traditions of the Old World, the
superstition of the imported article and belief; the
attempts at perpetuation of the ridiculous social
castes, of the English village; the stodgy cookery
and the foolish black clothing and squeakful boots
of the British Methody, the dour Calvinism of Pais-
lev and Lancashire, and the cruel parochialism of the
Cornishman that prevent a triumphant national sen-
timent. A moment of national danger would give
the national sentiment a victory, but we cannot wait
for war or invasion, because Australia the best
country on earth—should be made the best country
on earth to live in without the unnecessary loss of a
da Let us rebel! The Australian has allowed himself
to be mastered by minorities, because he is not good
citizen enough to use the power he has. To make
Australia a land where wine may be drunk under
trees .and to the sound of music, instead of a place
where wine is mostly whisky, to be consumed in a
cellar, and music in a licensed house is an offence
under the Act ; to make Australia a place that re-
spects the good citizen who loves Australia, and re-
fuses to be legislated for by dreary men who bleat
“Empire,” it is only necessary to use, for the pur-
pose of making laws and moulding that public
opinion which is greater than all law, a little of
that energy in the duties of citizenship which the
average Australian of the cities puts into cricket or
the chase of the Cup winner. If he wishes to make
Australia a place of gladness, and music, and com-
fort and beauty—a place that knows no more the
Japophile, and the Anglo-maniac, and the Calvinist,
and the general crank, as noisy minorities pre-
tending to be majorities—let him make up his
mind in the matter and the thing is done.
From stupid conditions, only tolerated be-
cause they had their origin in the Old World, to
ways of life which mean the beauty, the joys and
the praise of life is not a long step—only a decisive
one. So let us rebel, decisively, this week.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A New Cure for a Broken Leg.
Accokding to the agenda paper for the re-
cent conference of the Associated Chambers
of Manufactures, the objects of the associa-
tion include the following :
To endeavor to secure, by conference or otherwise,
uniformity of laws and customs respecting or af-
fecting manufacture and production in the various
States.
The association has been in existence since
1903, and seven conferences have been held ;
but so far as securing “uniformity of laws
and customs respecting or affecting manu-
facture and production” is concerned, the re-
sults have been absolutely nil. It is obvious
that “conference” is useless to effect the de-
sired change, so it becomes necessary for
the Associated Chambers to push on with
“otherwise” methods. As the only method
in sight is offered by the Referenda, it might
have been thought that the conference would
have passed an enthusiastic resolution, re-
commending all manufactures to vote “Yes”
on April 26. Instead of doing so, how-
ever, the delegates made it apparent to
Hughes, M.H.R., who addressed them on the
subject, that he was dealing with a hostile
audience, and afterwards they carried the
following motion: —
That in the opinion of this Conference it is in
the interests of Australia that the electors of the
Commonwealth should vote “NO” to the questions
to be submitted in the Referenda in April next.
The fertile genius of the South Australian
Chamber was responsible for tabling this
motion, and the position of that body re-
quires a heap of explanation. A few
months ago there were bitter complaints on
the part of implement and machinery manu-
facturers of the central State that they were
unable to compete with Victorian makers,
owing to the South Australian Wages Board
having fixed wages on a more liberal scale
than the Victorian. Evidently the South
Australian Chamber of Manufactures is of
opinion that the only way to remove the
difficulty is to perpetuate the insane system
of misgovernment which gave rise to it, and
that the best way to cure a firoken leg is to
sit on the mule’s hind foot, and give him a
chance to break the other one.
.
The Shabby-Genteel Viewpoint.
The Anti-Labor papers, in the course of their
struggle against universal service, fathered
some weird objections, but the following,
which Sydney Telegraph quoted. approv-
ingly last week, was surely the limit :■ —
Quite a large number of boys have refused to reg-
ister, though refusal to register is punishable by a
heavy fine. It cannot be wondered at that many
boys refuse to register. You must remember that
the Governor-General’s son and the Admiral’s son
are equally liable for service with the coal-heaver s
offspring. The surroundings of the compulsory
training should be such as to make the boy take a
pride in it, instead of it being a' matter of scorn to
him.
It would read strangely in the most intoler-
ant of French royalist publications. In a
country like Australia, where practically all
except Domain and park loafers work for a
living, and in a journal which has lately
fulminated against “the wickedness and ab-
surdity of class distinctions,”. it is simply
stupefying. Though such a view is practi-
cally never heard in France, it would
more or less understandable there. . For
France only broke loose from Feudalism, a
century ago; it still possesses an inordin-
ately rich and proud landed aristocracy, and,
despite its aspirations after equality and fra-
ternity, it has a clearly defined niche for
each social unit. In such a country a sec-
tion of the inhabitants might be expected
to object to a system under which citizens
in every walk of life are forced into inti-
mate association, regardless of their up-
bringing and origin. Yet, whatever protests
do occur, they are so faint as to be inaud-
ible. There is nothing the Frenchman
dreads more than a charge of having shirked
his military duties —as witness the extraor-
dinary upheaval in the Comedie Franchise
recently, owing to a band of Anti-Semites
reminding Bernstein, the dramatist, that
he had been a deserter in the dim past.
Hilaire Belloc, M.P., after leaving the
English school where he was educated, and
before going to Oxford, crossed to France
and served his term in the ranks. In short,
to the Frenchman, rich . or poor, Jew or
Gentile, compulsory training is not “a mat-
ter of scorn.” And yet the surroundings
are, from the point of view of the veriest
bourgeois, simply awful. The French ‘picm~
piou,” or Thomas Atkins, sleeps, feeds
and dresses less luxuriously than the in-
mates of some Australian gaols. His daily
tasks comprise the most unattractive duties
of the rouseabout and the domestic help.
He cheerfully undergoes two years of this,
in many cases hundreds of miles from his
home, because his country asks it of him.
Australia does not, after the fashion of
France, require its young men to put in
two years’ uninterrupted service in an Ac-
tive or Regular Army, followed by 11 years
in a Reserve, and six in a Territorial force.
All it asks is that a certain number of
schoolboys shall, each year till they are 18,
put in 16 days’ training—consisting of
night drills, half-day drills, and a few whole-
day drills. Subsequently they will do a
further 16 days a year—of which eight days
must be spent in camp—till they reach 25.
And alleged Australians are to be
found who argue that this amounts to
an intolerable burden on our young
aristocrats, and that the fact that
' these lads may have to receive military in-
struction in the vicinity of “a coal-heaver’s
offspring,” practically justifies them in break-
ing the law, and doing that which, among
patriotic peoples, is regarded as an unfor-
givable sin. If there were not in this coun-
try a large number of intensely haughty
solicitors, commission agents, fluff-merchants
and so forth, who hold just this view, and
gravely advance it as a conclusive argu-
ment against compulsory service, it would
be a matter for diversion, not to say deri-
sion.

A Proposal to Sell a Vest Along with the
Coat and Trousers.
The Right Thinking Persons in Sydney
were quite sure on Friday that the Tiger had
broken loose at last. The main business
part of the city is built upon a narrow neck
of land —it has crowded as close as possible
to the principal wharves. Naturally, the
Harbor being very narrow thereabouts, a very
large section of the people who' want to get
to'the business part have made their homes
on the opposite side of the water. State
trams and trains pour tens of thousands of
people on to wharves on the North Shore
in the mornings; and in the evenings State
trams on the city side pour tens of thousands
of returning people on to the wharves on the
South Shore. The brief connecting link is, how-
ever, not supplied by the State: the concen-
trated traffic, gathered at great expense by
,the State, is handed over to Private Enter-
prise, represented by ferry companies. If
the State is justified in running trams,
it is equally justified in running ferries:
Private Enterprise has no more a mort-
gage over the water than it lias over
the land. In this case, the division
of service is absurd, because, as already men-
tioned, the State concentrates the passengers:
they are focussed by State lines which come
in from all directions. Tens of thousands
of them step off a State tram, and, having
been ferried across by Private Enterprise,
they step on to another State tram or train.
The Government proposes now to complete
the job ; in other words, to take its passen-
gers right through, instead of permitting
them to be pecked at en route. Other con-
siderations besides those already mentioned
have weighed with the Government. Circu-
lar Quay, where the trams disgorge their
passengers, has long been referred to as a
death-trap. Hundreds of thousands of
people have daily to cross the busiest tram-
lines in the city. In places the traffic is
so heavy that there are four parallel lines,
filled with moving trams, and men, women
and children have to thread their way be-
tween them. The Government proposes to
reduce this congestion by offering tram pas-
sengers facilities for escaping Circular Quay
altogether, and carrying them direct to State
ferries at the two points which jut out
farthest towards the North Shore. That the
State, which runs the trams and trains,
should undertake to carry its passengers right
through is a perfectly sound business propo-
rtion. On commercial grounds it is abso-
lutely unimpeachable. A man when he
wants a suit of clothes goes into a tailor's
or a clothier’s, and gets the whole suit. The
clothes-man doesn’t sell him a pair of trou-
sers, and tell him to go somewhere else to
buy a vest, and return for the coat after he
has been supplied with the vest at the other
shop. A tailor or clothier who worked on
those lines would be laughed at: yet those
ere the lines the State has.been working on.
In proposing to make a change, and supply
the vest, it simply proposes to do what every
good business man would want to do. But
that doesn’t end the matter. What it pro-
poses to do is to supply what is at best a
makeshift. If the foreshores of Circular
Quay are a death-trap, so is Sydney Cove
and the Harbor thereabouts. The hundreds '
of thousands who are ferried across are in
danger every day of their lives. The traffic
is perilously congested; and the addition of
new ferries will not reduce the congestion.
A man —or 500 men —will be just as dead
if drowned in a State-ferry disaster as if
the acicdent happened to a Private Enter-
prise ark. Nothing but a bridge or a tunnel
will permanently solve the problem. The
one justification for the ferry scheme which
the Government proposes is that_ it will re-
lieve the strain while the long job in con-
nection with the permanent solution is in
hand. If, however, the makeshift pushes the
real thing further into the distance, Sydney
would be better without it.

The Discovery of a Dreadful Conspiracy.
The Wangaratta Chronicle (Vic.) has
made an important discovery :
Under the amendment in Question No. 1 which
will give over to the Federal Government control of
“Corporations,” the Shire and Borough Councils
will pass under Federal authority, and it is only
reasonable to expect that when that happens the
Labor Ministry will proceed to carry out Labor
ideals for the management of municipalities. . . .
The fact is that the Labor party has for many years
striven to get control of the Shire and Borough
Councils, and the Councils have been, of set pur-
pose, included jin the Referenda.
The Municipal Association of Victoria has
taken the matter up, and has issued a warn-
ing to the effect that the Federal Parliament
could even wind up the present municipali-
ties, and create bodies “absolutely under the
control of the Federal Parliament.” But
the half of the awful thing has not been
told. It is all the fault <?f the malevolent
Hughes, of course. This impish person “of
set purpose,” used the word “corporations,
instead of “companies,” after turning up
Webster, the standard dictionary. Therein
he read thi^: —
Corporations—(4) The abdomen or belly, especi-
ally when protuberant. [“Sirrah, my corporation is
made up of good English fat Smollett] ,
So the dreadful proposal reads now really
like this: —
Legislative Power of the Parliament. —The Par-
liament shall, subject to this Constitution, have
power to make laws for the peace, order, and good-
government of the Commonwealth with respect to
(i) Trade and commerce.
(xx) Corporations, including the creation, dis-
solution, regulation and control of corpora-
tions —
which is to say, the creation, dissolution,
regulation and control of [for the rest see
Websteb]. This is certainly a matter that
the Wangaratta Chronicle and the Muni-
cipal Association of Victoria should look
into at once. It opens up awful possibili-
ties. The “regulation and control” — : the
dreadful Labor Government evidently has
its eye on some new and drastic medicine.
Perhaps it may even order Fat, M.L.C., to
eaf only one meal a day, on pain of six
months’ gaol. Hitherto this paper had sup-
posed that, in this Referendum, “corpora-
tions” only meant trading corporations. It
seems, however, that a subtle thrust is made
at one of our most intimate possessions—a
thing many of us hold sacred. For this dis-
covery we are indebted to the Wangaratta
Chronicle and the Municipal Association
of Victoria. But for these grand pioneers
in the realms of idiocy, one might never
have suspected the dreadful conspiracy—one
might never have supposed that the National
Parliament had sinister intentions on either
Little Pedlington or Little Mary.
Willie Watt, who is being boomed as a
great Anti-Referenda speaker by the (Melb.)
Argus and the Victorian Liberal League, has
discovered a new reason why the people
should vote “No” on April 20. What, he
asked, would be said L»y the people of Ire-
land when they were granted Home Rule,
if the British Parliament reserved to itself
the powers which the Australian Parliament
is asking for, particularly the power to con-
trol internal trade! There was no differ-
ence, he alleged, between that supposititious
action and the action of the Australian Par-
liament in trying to “take” these powers
fiom the States; and he challenged anyone
in the hall to point out the difference, if
there was any. Probably 00 per cent, of
his hearers knew exactly what the differ-
ence was; but, as the meeting had just
seen a woman removed from the hall for a
little good-natured interjection, nobody ac-
cepted the challenge, and Willie Watt
cried exultantly, “They don’t know!” and
looked as though he had scored a fine point.
The difference, which was not explained be-
cause nobody was given a chance, is this.
In the case of Ireland, a British Parlia-
ment, which is not elected wholly by the
people, would be taking the power from a
Parliament which is to be wholly elected by
the people; while in Australia the one and
only Parliament which is wholly elected
by the people on adult suffrage, is seeking
some of the powers at present held (and
mostly not used) by a number of Parlia-
ments, not one of which is wholly elected
on adult suffrage. Which is about as large
a difference as there could be.

Two persons charged with murder have been
acquitted by Melbourne juries within the
past week or so, and the two citizens they
were accused of murdering are as dead as
ever. In one case a young man, whose only
witnesses were two friendly damsels of shady
reputation, was charged with the death of
another young man in the Edinburgh Gar-
dens. The victim’s body was found lying
face-downwards in the grass. The left eye
was protruding. Death had been caused by
very violent injuries. Nobody but the ac-
cused and his girl-witnesses could be pro-
duced to say how the murdered man came to
be in the garden at all. It was suggested
that his pockets had been picked in the
streets, and that he had followed the party
of three to get his money back. The accused
young man’s story was that he had a stand-
up fight with the athletic young citizen.
That, however, didn’t offer any reasonable
explanation of the corpse’s shocking injuries,
and the Judge appeared not to be impressed
by it. Still the prisoner was acquitted. A
decent citizen was done to death by some-
one, and the doer escaped punishment. The
other case was that of Olive Moo die, who
was found not guilty of murdering her
mother with poison. In this case the evi-
dence for the prosecution was purely circum-
stantial, while for the defence the prisoner s
story suggested that the mother had poisoned
herself —in spite of the fact that the sick
woman had complained to the doctor of her
medicine burning her throat. The Judge
didn’t seem to think much of this story
either. Still, after one jury had disagreed,
another said “Not guilty.” So the cause of
the awful damage to Archibald Steane’s
left eye, and the reason for the presence of
arsenic in Mrs. Moodie’s stomach, are added
to the list of mysteries which the ineffective
police and detective force of Victoria haven’t
been able to clear up.

The Conscience Clause.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a movement on foot in Melbourne “to take
such steps as may be necessary to secure the intro-
duction of a conscience clause in our defence laws.’’
When flags are licking through the smoke.
And frantic bugles cry,
While mighty guns with giant stroke
Are shattering the sky,
I lie at home. God bless the laws
That granted me a conscience clause !
The foeman turns, and strips the land,
And fires the regal town.
A fevered Asiatic band
With bloody hands come down.
I rise in grief to bid them pause,
And quote my sacred conscience clause.
They shame my wife, and slay my child,
And give my home to fire.
So to a cavern in the wild
I hastily retire.
Alone, and nude, and famished here,
My comfort is a conscience clear!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

Successful lawyers and doctors fix their own emolu-
ments ; but the seaman (successful or otherwise)
who asks for a decent wage is “an agitator” and
“a menace to the ‘profession.’ ” In the old days,
when the shipmaster got commissions on freights,
it was reasonable to look upon him as a unit of the
“firm”; but in there times, when he has nothing
to do with freights, and attends mostly to pilotage
duties, he is merely a wage-earner. It is difficult
to get shipmasters to see this. The old fetich of
the “cloth” is still with them. And their pay
suffers in consequence.
I write feelingly. “Old Bill” has gone. God rest
his soul!
