“Curse o’ Moses” on the things left un-
said :
The Sydney Morning Herald complains that, in
his speech on the Referendum, Mr. Fisher never
once mentioned the Empire. This is exceedingly
sad, and makes us wonder whither this large conti-
nent is drifting. In point of fact, Mr. Fisher’s
speech was full of omissions. For nearly two hours
he addressed an enthusiastic Australian audience,
and in the whole course of his speech he made no
reference to the Athanasian controversy, the differential
calculus, Bickerton’s theory of partial impact, the
invasion of Europe by the Seljuk Turks, the manufac-
ture of raspberry jam, the claims of Maria Theresa,
the Copernican system, the discovery of America,
the signing of Magna Charta, the Chilian patriot
O’lliggins, the death of Julius Cjesar, the effects of
drought on pumpkins, the Old Red Sandstone period,
the French Revolution, the Adventures of Robinson
Crusoe, the Anglo-Japanese conspiracy, the state of
thermal activity in Iceland, the price of beer at
Broken Hill, or the possibility of deriving the
Federal land tax from Middle Harbor oysters. All
these pressing matters were quite ignored. Really,
a great statesman should be more careful. It is a
well-known rule of effective oratory that any person
attempting to discuss the Shakspearian sonnet
should, as far as possible, confine his remarks to the
manufacture of straw hats; and anyone desiring to
convince an audience that Cleopatra was the mother
of Moses should devote the whole time at his dis-
posal to the consideration of the properties of Homer’s
iliad, when used as a beverage. By inference, in speak-
ing on any Australian subject, the first and most ab-
solute necessity is to talk all the time about India,
England, Hong Kong and other remote places. Perhaps
Mr. Fisher will have learned this lesson by the time he
returns from helping George Rex to put on his
official hat.
♦
“Clarence O’Hagan” on Some Roosters
and a National Swindle:—
The Sydney press—with, of course, a set pur-
pose —made a great fuss because King O’Malley
referred to the head officials of his Department as
“roosters” and “boodlers.” I rise to second the
Picturesque One’s remarks on the “rooster” count.
I know the crowd well, and the term “roosters” fits
to the ankle-bones. For ten solid years three of
them have practically run the country, as far as
any permanent Civil servant can run it. These
three are all bigoted Sydney men —not Australians
at all, in the genuine sense. They entered the De-
partment loaded to the muzzle with the idea that
Sydney is N.S.W., and N.S.W. is Australia, and
they have acted up to the idea with great regu-
larity ever since. If ever the true story of the
disgraceful Yass-Canberra swindle comes to light,
these three gentlemen (personally they are gentle-
men) will be found prominently in the piece.
Forrest, a great Australian in many ways, be-
lieved in and fought for Dalgety; the Three Roost-
ers thwarted him. When Fuller was at their
head —a Canberra-mad Minister, if ever there was
one —the speed with which the Capital-site business
was pushed was something to marvel at. O’Malley
next came on the scene, and he was astounded to
find that, long before there was Parliamentary author-
isation —let alone national approval—of the Huge Job,
the Departmental “roosters,” aided and abetted by
Fuller, had got things cut and dried, and had
committed the Government in a dozen ways. Now,
O’Malley had always rightly contended that the
Capital question was a national question, to be
settled by the people themselves. Personally he
was strongly in favor of Dalgety—one of its
warmest advocates, indeed. The malign influence
of W. M. Hughes dragged the Fisher Government
into a foolish endorsement of the Fuller Capital-
site P.N., and reluctantly O’Malley went over
to the party view. The result, so far, is known.
“So far,” because when the next Big Drought
comes—and it’s getting quite close now —the Can-
swindle will be abandoned in a hurry. Even the
Three Roosters will want to stop away from it.
They worked for the dear, old Sydney interest, and
the Importing Press of their boyhood, and they
have their reward.
♦
“Bash” says it’s loaded: —
In my township, not 100 miles from Terang
(Vic.), a horse died from that deadliest and swiftest
of stock diseases, anthrax. The vet. who diagnosed
the case, following what, from personal experience
and hearsay, I believe to be the invariable custom,
cut an ear off the defunct neddy, packed it in cot-
ton-wool, squeezed it into a cardboard box, wrapped
the box in paper, addressed it to the Stock Depart-
ment, Melbourne, and posted, it in the ordinary way
—presumably for bacteriological examination. The
vet. and the Department ought to know the risk
of sending such a specimen promiscuously through
the general post; but apparently the G.P.O. people
are ignorant. An instance or two that have come
under my own observation may put them wise to the
ease with which human beings may acquire an-
thrax.
On the next farm but one to my own a beast which
had died of anthrax was being skinned (your true
cocky is thrifty at any cost!), and the owner’s son, a
lad of 19, scratched the side of one finger with
the point of the knife he was using. In an hour’s
time a pustule had formed, and though the father
harnessed up in haste, and had the boy under treat-
ment almost immediately, a second pustule broke
out further up the finger, and the patient was left
with the alternative of life with nine digits, or
death with ten. He chose wisely, and a prompt
amputation saved his life. In a second instance
a cattle-man scratched the back of one hand with
a piece of bone from an anthrax-carcase he was pre-
paring to destroy. Result, death in 12 hours —it
usually occurs, in any event, inside 36 hours from
the inoculation. A third case, also ending -‘fatally,
was the infection of a farm hand from a bifc upon
the ear inflicted by a fly, which had been dining on
the carcase of a steer that had died of the disease.
On that evidence I make oath and say that the
vets, who dump anthrax into the public mails, the
Stock Department which sanctions the practice, or
the postal authorities who permit it, are guilty of
culpable (I was going to say criminal) negligence.
They can fight it out amongst themselves.

So narrow was the margin between the
completion of the new Adelphi Theatre, Syd-
ney, and the opening ceremony on April 5,
that the last nail went in with a clatter as
Mayor Allen Taylor appeared before the foot-
lights to breathe a blessing on the building,
whereat the Mayor, mistaking the noise for
the extra appreciation of a big Dago at
the Council’s failure to break up the fish
ring, bowed with proper solemnity. After
that all went well. The theatre was de-
clared open, congratulations and cheers were
distributed to all concerned, including the
City Council, to which the property will re-
vert in 50 years, and Paddy’s Market, for
passing out to provide the site. Finally,
sandwiches and the usual choice of sauces.
In the evening the Marlow Dramatic Co. com-
menced its season in the theatre with “The
Bad Girl of the Family,” before a crowded
house. She is not a brand-new bad girl;
rather she is a conglomeration of old girl,
good, bad and indifferent. In these days
of doubtful axioms and proven paradoxes,
no man can say with certainty what consti-
tutes a bad or a good girl, and the writer
will venture no further opinion on Bess
Moore (Miss Nellie Ferguson) than that she
is a good actress. Robert Inman, Harry Diver,
J. P. O’Neill and John Cosgrove owned
the capable hands that the bulk of the work
was left in ; and Essie Clay and Beth Mur-
doch put their talents to the wheel to some
purpose. And it was a kind of wheel that
needed it. The Bad Girl will expose her
past till further notice.
m* * *
Year by year pantomime gets nearer and
nearer to musical comedy, inasmuch as they
both approximate more closely to vaudeville.
“Jack and the Beanstalk” —the show which
arrived from Melbourne last week, and was
produced at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, on Sat-
urday night—beats all records in the matter
of sparseness of plot. On the other hand,
the scenic effects are marvels of vastness,
brilliancy and color; the humor is mostly
humorous; the songs are the sort that the
habitual pantomime-frequenter best loves;
the patriotism (Australian, as opposed to
the British Empire League brand) is unim-
peachable ; and the star turns are dazzling.
“THE BAD GIRL OF THE FAMILY,"
AT THE ADELPHI.
It is in accordance with the spirit of
modern pantomime that the Pantzer troupe,
who are mere excrescences, so to speak, on
the face of the performance, should be its
best feature. These acrobats easily beat
anything in their line that has visited Aus-
tralia in the writer’s recollection. Neil Ken-
yon’s Dame Trott is a female of severely re-
strained absurdity. Evidently she is the
herald of a new order of Dames. She stands
midway between the old-time panto, mother —
who was a woman of disreputable habits and
rotten ethics, with the appearance of a
Water Police Court habitue —and a Dame
who shall be no more grotesque as to ap-
pearance, manner or sentiments than your
average “Liberal” lady-organiser. Jack
Cannot is an amusing King, who possesses a
better singing voice than most of the mon-
archs with whom this paper is accustomed to
foregather. Miss Sibyl Arundale is a de-
lightful, melodious and graceful little Jack,
and Miss Dorothy Firmin a sweetheart of
whom any boy might be proud. Miss Cora
Hamilton (solo dancer), the Doherty Sisters
(Ginger Maidens from the U.S.), Miss Marie
Eaton (Soprano) and W. A. Hassan (Cow)
are all excellent in their several ways.
“ THE BELLE
OF BRIT-
TANY,” AT
MELBOURNE
ROYAL.
The Beautiful
Chorus Boy.
A clergyman has advertised his intention
of delivering a series of sermons on "The
Woman in the Case,” now running at Syd-
ney Criterion. That looks like success. It
the cleric praises the play, the Wowser will
go in large numbers because he may ; if, con-
trariwise, the holy man damns it, the Wow-
ser will go in even greater numbers, because
he mustn’t. In either case, the Wowser is
urged to book early, since the Criterion is
suffering, just now, from a rush of those
unregenerate ones who go to stirring shows
regardless of the Church’s tips.
* ve * *
There are various things which this paper
thinks it superfluous to mention. Thus it
doesn’t, as a rule, allude to the fact that
there are fish in the sea, birds in the air, or
16oz. to the lb., if you watch the man while
he’s weighing. On the same principle it be-
gins to suspect that it is superfluous to say
that “Our Miss Gibbs” is at the Royal.
The lady last evening made her two hundred
and somethingth appearance; and when mat-
ters have gone that far it may be regarded as
a habit.
** * *
R. A. Roberts, the quick changer at Syd-
ney Tivoli, is more numerous than ever.
Last week he was five people in a Dick Tur-
pin sketch ; this week he is eight people in a
brisk playlet called “Ringing the Changes.”
Peggy Pryde, the joyous, bustling little
English comedienne, was billed to make her
reappearance on Saturday, after eight long
years; but illness kept her away. A vast
sigh went up from the crowded house when
the sorrow was announced. Conjurer Leip-
zig, Florence Challis, Alexander Prince and
Tom Dawson complete the first line of
amusement, which will be a very strong one
indeed when Peggy is well enough to take
her place in it.
* * **
The Royal Hawaiians opened a Sydney
season at the Y.M.C.A. Hall on Saturday,
and what the audience thought of the show
may be judged by the fact that 20 items
scored 15 encores. The programme contains
several English choruses, but the regal Ha-
waiians shine more dazzlingly in native chor-
uses, and in the items for the Hawaiian
equivalents of the guitar and mandolin.
Also, there is a native instrument, the uku-
lele, which hasn’t any equivalent, and is
handled with great skill. The co. consists of
eight sunburnt gentlemen and two ladies.
The gentlemen wear “hot” socks, and the
ladies wear large smiles —and other things.
The section which owns the socks includes a
bass soloist, James Kamakani, whose full,
deep voice was heard to most advantage in
“Wiliwiliwai.” The other large item
among the males is Ernest Kaai, the musical
director, who, besides singing a ballad, plays
a medley on the ukulele, on which weird
instrument he is understood to be a Kubelik
and a Percy Grainger rolled into one, and
baked brown. The Hawaiian instrumental
march “Hilo” was a particularly fine item.
The ancient Hula Dance, by Miss Anehila —
the lady who owns the larger of the two
smiles—is performed to the accompaniment
of a gourd and a bag of marbles, and is more
quaint than attractive. To a large extent
it consists of hip movements, which suggest
that the lady is in a tight place, and wants
to escape. The whole show is off the beaten
track, and may be recommended as a refresh-
ing change after a course of sodden Easter
cookery.
** * *
The thief, murderer, or other estimable
citizen who hankers to inaugurate another
“Mystery of a Hansom Cab,” and thereby lay
the foundation for further crowded houses at
West’s picture shows, while adequately re-
couping himself for his enterprise, has only
to waylay G. L. Peterson on his journey
from Wirths’ Circus these nights with the
cash takings. They are big and comforting.
The suggestion is offered free of cost by the
writer, he not having time to carry it into
effect owing to the enormous problem facing
him as to whether he should kidnap No. 1
Adelia sister, No. 2 Adelia sister, Miss May
Martin, or Miss Quira Mears. The main
thing restraining him is that to elope with
even one would deplete the ring of a gem,
and plunge the front chairs in mourning.
On the other hand, there would be plenty
of attractions left in the well-trained horses,
dogs, jugglers, trick jockeys, acrobats and
clowns, to say nothing of the Flying Jor-
dans. So far, Marco, the animal trainer,
hasn’t been chewed in his big iron enclosure
by a lion, tiger, wolf, or even billygoat, and
he reckons on getting through his perform-
ance every night during the Sydney season
in Prince Alfred Park without losing a but-
ton of his uniform. With a bit more prac-
tice Marco will have as strong a nerve as a
No-No politician.
** * *
Melbourne seems disposed to accept “The
Belle of Brittany,” at the Royal, as a hand-
some example of that kind of comic opera in
which the plot is starved to fatten one or
two of the character parts, and the music is
more remarkable for nimble orchestration
than alluring melody. All the same, Mel-
bourne is still in two minds about “The
Belle.” With the exception of Tom Walls,
who is a well-defined (and strictly modern)
joke throughout the proceedings, and Miss
Gilliam, the heroine of the “Butter” ditty, the
company doesn’t seem to lay enough empha-
sis on its remarks and actions. The per-
formance is too flippant. Miss O’Connor’s
manner of breaking off her engagement with
the cook from Paris is not more tender than
the chef’s way of bearing the blow. Half
a dozen times during the evening one wishes
that the people in possession of the stage
would take their foolishness more seriously.
Consequently, the pungent monologues of
Walls overwhelm the narrative, and reduce
the sentimental interest —which is desirable
even in comic opera—to a shadowy back-
ground. Not enough is made of the flimsy
but neatly-woven story, and the climax ar-
rives in a violent hurry, as though it were
running to catch a train.

For a fortnight only “The Waltz Dream”
is reviving its substantial German music
and serio-comic story at Her Majesty’s.
Melbourne remembers that the opera nearly
bursts into tears on account of Princess
Florence Young, whose husband damns the
royal dynasty, and breaks away from the
palace on his wedding night. It is the opera
wherein Roberts, the Prince Consort, chases
a waltz tune to a beer garden, and gets
sweetly entangled with the conductress of a
ladies’ orchestra. Their lingering kiss is
so long that it threatens to end in a royal
divorce; but the angel of the beer garden
has virtuous scruples about capturing a
prince consort, so she sympathises with ms

“In London Town” being about to depart
from The King’s, that playhouse is now pre-
paring for “Right is Might,’* by Edward
Peple, manufacturer of The Prince Lhap
and other toffee. H. R. Roberts has been
secured for the principal role, and he will be
supported by Miss Beatrice Day and other
persons of less distinction. In the mean-
time, it should be announced that the usual
concert will transpire at The King s on
Good Friday evening. The programme will
be weighted with songs and pictures, which
will try hard to be in keeping with the spirit
of the occasion.
VICTOR CHAMPION CONDUCTING
“ THE BELLE OF BRITT ANY."
At the Opera House the humorous juggling
of seven Perezoffs is the prevailing novelty;
but, apart from the supper party of dexter-
ous strangers, there are some re-arrived
couples who were not there last week.
Cooper and Lait came back on Saturday for
a final innings, and the Dartos danced in
with “Le Visiteur.” For the rest, the Col-
umbia Trio sing and frivol as before, the
costumes of Miss Spark shine with undimin-
ished gaudiness, and the Dogs are butting
the football for a few more farewell nights.
** * *
Last week West’s Melbourne Palace had
the usual batch of films dealing with heart-
throbs, like “The Greater Call,” or mercurial
stuff, like “Poor Sick Men.” A large pic-
ture de-luxe, by-the-bye, is announced to
flash at an early date. The subject is
“Henry VIII.,” as photoed by the Barker
Co. from Herbert Tree’s gorgeous produc-
tion at His Majesty’s, London. The item
exhibits Tree, Bourchier, Miss Violet Van-
brugh, and others, and spectacularly is de-
scribed as the attar of magnificence.
** * *
“L’Aiglon,” whom little Tillie Brune made
familiar to Australia, has been the subject
of a picture at the Prahran Lyric (Vic.).
The young man, played by an actor of poetic
exterior, is mostly shown as a romantic in-
valid, feeding on dreams of his father’s,
glories. The film is effective, and includes
a rich final scene, in which the boy dies with
a profusion of Napoleonic gestures. An-
other Lyric attraction of late has been
“In A Moment’s Folly”—a picture sugges-
tive of Bernstein’s “Thief,” watered down.
The curse of St. Kildam :
The highly disrespected Council of St. Kilda (Melb.
has decided that it “ wouldn’t like to see ” the affairs of
that marine suburb “conducted from Yass-Canberra,”
and therefore “would advise all the ratepayers to vote
against the Referendum.” The writer wishes to Heaven
that St. Kilda could be governed from anywhere other
than its Town Hall, and by anybody other than the pre-
sent crowd of councillors. He lives in a sideway turning
off one of the oldest streets, where the rents are pretty
high and the rates match the rents. The new houses in
this part have been occupied for a whole year, but the
road all that time has remained unmade, unlighted, un-
named and uncommonly dangerous. In fact, its condi-
tion is a disgrace to the jacks-in-offlce who are advising
the ratepayers “ to vote against the Referendum," what
ever that may mean !
“ Prahran ” is likely to be rubbed out of
Melbourne’s municipal and local records, and
“St. Kilda” along with it, the councils of those
two “cities” having decided to amalgamate and
call themselves South Yarrn, providing the citizens
are agreeable. And the, citizens, being in favor
of any progressive move that will substitute one
strong effective authority for numerous small,
squabbling and expensive bodies, are quite certain
to be agreeable.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Turtle”: I’m tired of all this talk about the
alleged want of patriotism on the part of Australian
theatregoers. Local audiences, and particularly thp
tightly-jammed gallery crowds, are most enthusiastic
over Australian performers. Asche drew the largest
houses ever known to our theatres; Amy Castles was
more rushed than Bel Sorel; Dolly Castles, as prin-
cipal panto-boy, would (like Florence Young in
“Mother Goose”) draw more people than any impor-
ted lead. Nellie Stewart packed the lonely Princess’s
everv night of her Melbourne season. The Austra-
lian'kiddies in the pantos, have always received (and
deserved) our best applause. Same applies to our
chorus-girls. The frigid reception given to the rather
stiff imports who deigned to appear with “The Gay
Gordons” recently should have taught a lesson ; but
the importing foolishness will no doubt continue
until we get rid of the old-fashioned managers, who,
instead of catering for an increasingly patriotic pub-
lic, are pandering to the weak-kneed Australians of
20 years ago.
“H.T.”: 1 think it’s about time someone got in
an uppercut on the music hysteria. I read the
other day that a “critic” thought one of Kubelik’s
scrapings sounded like a fountain playing in the
moonlight. This sort of thing ought to be knocked
out. Music is a mental game, like anagrams. The art is
in piecing the notes together to get an expression.
The higher the class of music the harder it is to piece
it together; that is why people who can understand
“Lohengrin” are so conceited. There is nothing ele-
vating or soulful in music. Musicians as a class are
the meanest and most cantankerous folk about. Surely
this proves the failure of music to make good. The
men who are doing things—the thinkers, writers and
scientists —-can seldom tell “Tommy Atkins” from
“God Save.” The sooner this fancy talk about a
piano-thumper’s “soul” is jumped on, the sooner we
will have him doing useful work. Many a musician
is a loss to the bricklaying trade.
The stage-struck condition of Melbourne’s “middle
classes” is strikingly apparent in the airs and graces
of the new Juvenile Opera Co. which J. C. William-
son has organised for touring purposes. The young-
sters, who played “The Geisha” in Melbourne last
week, and are now in S.A., appear to be on the
stage for their parents’ pleasure rather than profit.
Whereas panto, kiddies in general are wage-earners
working for the family, these juvenile opera nippers
look like suburban society amateurs revelling in the
importance of being professionals for a time. One
fancies that they think no small potatoes of them-
selves, in comparison with “common” children.
“Stargazer”: J. C. Williamson takes the juvenile
Comic op. industry in hand as an effective and
profitable means of providing recruits for the Royal
Comics, and in disregard of threatened legislation
to wipe the infant prodigy from public life. The
'juveniles got going with “The Geisha,” and suc-
ceeded in strengthening my conviction that operas
of this class are designed and builded for kids. The
utter childishness of the matter and the infantile
quality of the prattle are brought home to all by the
babes on the boards. Never for a moment is any-
thing intellectually a cut above the adolescent actor
of 12 and the immature actress of 10. But where Gil-
bert and Sullivan were interesting even in the hands
of babes and sucklings, plays of “The Geisha” sort
are a hollow mockery without an adult chorus to
google the front stalls, and I cannot picture them
running without legs.

COME, SEE AND HEAR!
At a previous pleasant Sunday a captain preached
in his shroud, with his head encased in an imitation
skull. The Army degenerates. It was once all blood
and fire. Lately it is only bones and ashes.
“Invercargill”: The “comedy” and “dramatic”
films at the flicker shows don’t appeal to the deaf
and dumb, for the reason that, by reading the lips
of the actors, such persons can follow the dialogue
as well as the action. It is a notorious fact that
professional cinematographic actors seldom follow
their “lines.” They keep close to the action indi-
cated in the scenario they are playing, but say any-
thing that comes into their heads. The ordinary
person is none the wiser; but recently in N’York
there was trouble when a school of deaf and dumb
children went to a show. The mutes lost interest
as soon as they discovered that the words spoken by
the actors had “nothing to do with the case.”
However, when one of the actresses in a Broncho Bill
comedy film began to tell one of the cowboys a
“high” story, the master in charge thought it time
to remonstrate. In the following film there were a
number of show girls in the back ground all telling
spicy tales while the hero and heroine occupied the
centre of the flicker, and as the interest of the deaf
and dumb kids was rapidly reviving, they were
hurried from the hall. To learn lip talk would add
a great interest to the average flickergraph show.
Motoring Gossip.
Cheap motor-cars, like all other cheap lines of
machinery, soon become absolutely valueless, as
their faults are so generally known to everybody,
and also that the market is flooded with them in
second-hand condition. High-grade cars of the
Wolseley or Renault type are always a saleable
asset, and are generally difficult to procure second-
hand, and to anyone starting motoring, to advise
them to buy the best is a safe proposition.*
“The Pessimist”: Sydney’s latest theatre, the
Adelphi, cost its owners, of whom T. E. Rofe is
principal, a good deal niore than was anticipated,
and if they get their money back in 50 years, when
the whole property automatically goes back into the
possession of the City Council, I’ll be pleasantly sur-
prised—if I’m not where I expect to be. It cost
over £30,000 to put up the building, which was
£lB,OOO in excess of the amount insisted upon by
the Council. The theatre will accommodate 3000
people, but it seems likely that cheap prices will
have to be charged practically all the time, and
unless I am very much mistaken the two circles will
be shunned to a large extent. The upper circle is
too high and the dress-circle seats are too uncom-
fortable. Authorities vouch for the statement that
the theatre is the coolest and most fire-proof in Syd-
ney, and the conveniences might serve as a general
example in a city where theatre-goers’ con-
veniences appear to be the last thing thought
of. But that is not everything. Neither is
cheap melodrama, which will probably be the
theatre’s sheet-anchor. George Marlow, the lessee,
who also leases the Princess’s (Melb.), says that he
has his chance now, and promises big attractions
from abroad; but “The Liver on the Doorknob” and
“The Fatal Titter” will have to carry the baby as a
general habit, and I’m glad that Marlow’s share of
the infant isn’t bigger than it is.
The Taits are borrowing several musical “attrac-
tions” from J. Bull. Leonard Borwick, a pianist,
disembarks a shortish head of hair some time in
June. About August the twelve Huddersfield Bell-
ringers are promised, and later bn Ben Davies and
Miss Esta d’Argo will bring their oratorio voices.
For next year the Tait attractions will include
Kirkby Lunn, a contralto with a successful past.
“D.”: A humorous feature of the “From Forest to
Fireside” film, now being shown all over the Com-
monwealth, is the “Editorial Room” of Lloyd’s
NEws. Message boys are seen tearing about with
wires; frantic persons hurl themselves at telephones ;
others manipulate the tape as though their lives de-
pended on their briskness. In the midst .of the up-
roar sits a fatuous-looking young man, self-consci-
ously gnawing a cigarette. In intervals between his
struggles to kep the latter alight, he writes with the
celerity of a stage-hero penning an appeal for im-
mediate assistance. The slips he hurls at waiting
minions, who project themselves like live shells
into the outer darkness. And Lloyd’s News is a
staid old Sabbath sheet, which never by any chance
finds out a thing for itself, but simply lifts the best
“stories” of its contemps., and passes them on to a
public avid for twice-told tales.
“Pat O’Maori”: I rise to howl an indignant vitu-
peration against the Wowserisation of the pictorial
representations of gore and galloping cut-throats.
Sydney theatres, which nightly draw their silver
from the exhibitions of horrible red Indians on the
murder track, and the weddings of half-naked savage
girls to poor whites, are turning down the films which
portray the native Australian bushranger. The
Wowser said the bushranger wasn’t good, and the
picture showmen at once agreed with him, and pro-
mised never to do it again. Rats! Also, pigs,
beetles and C. G. Wade. If berlud is bad for the patrons
of jiggergrams, why not do away with the foreign
horror first ? The fact is that the Wowser wail
suited the showmen’s pockets. It is easier and
cheaper to import the American gorescape than to
make the local one. If local pictures are to be made,
they will afford employment to quite a number of
Australian actors and actresses, and that is a very
horrible (and expensive) proposal. Of all the
haters of Australia the Wowser seems to come easily
first. He denounces the bushranger, and hasn’t a
word to say against the Indian horror with the dis-
rupted turkey’s tail round his savage cranium. Now,
which showman is game to adopt the motto: “Only
Australian Blood Spilt Here l”

“Mary McCommonwealth”: I am of opinion that
all women should be enrolled for the defence of Aus-
tralia. I am fairly good with the flat-iron myself,
and I know a number of sweet young friends who
could give a tolerably good account of their hat-
pins in the deadly breach; but I am convinced that
the sword is woman’s natural weapon, and that we
could more than hold our own with the implement
which our lords have discarded. The other evening
I witnessed the struggle between William Shak-
speare and a number of ladies under the leadership
of Miss Rose Seaton. The dispute was over “Two
Gentlemen of Verona,” and Shakspeare looked like
holding his own until our party drew their swords.
That settled the matter. Old Bill saw that the game
was up, and with a shudder retired to his place
among the shades. It is all very well for people in
their pride of trousers to speak slightingly of our
valor, but put swords in our hands and see what we
can do!
“Floater”: Privately the late Max Maxwell was
one of the most unmummerly of mummers. He
had certain artistic instincts, but these were never
expressed along lines of booze, belles, or Bohem-
ianism. He was well-conducted to the brink of
primness, honorable to the verge of Quixotry, and
extremely “saving.” Much of his little horde was
sunk in "his dramatic enterprise, but some of it went
in providing a home for two relatives (other than
his wife and child) whom he regularly supported
right up to his death.
George Wirth, writing from New York last
month, mentioned that he had despatched a first
consignment of new people for the Circus —13 of
them in all —and was off to London and Europe in
search of further turns. Somehow the vaunted
hustle and bustle of New York life has failed to
impress George W. “They call it a fast place,” he
writes, “but the only things fast about it are
the people who get out of the way of tram cars
and automobiles. Everything else is dead slow.”
“10.2.4.”: I see that W. E. Johnson, M.H.R., has
written and had produced in the Rockdale (N.S.W.)
Town Hall, a play called “The Prince of Petriana.”
Amazing the egotism of these politicians! Johnson
has no valid claim to pose as Prince of Petriana pre-
varicators ; he was beaten hands down by both
George Reid and Joseph Carruthers. It is possible,
of course, that some fool-comp, or other has set
“Petriana” for “petrifaction.” In that case I have
no quarrel with W. Elliott Johnson’s new title.
A ramshackle milkman of Woolloomooloo
was haled before Sydney Water Summons Court
last week, and charged with having carried two
gallons of water on his cart whilst delivering
milk. The water carrier, instead of the usual
painted sign, merely had his name chalked on the
cart, and, seeing the Law about to swoop,
hurriedly tried to rub out the inscription. On the
Law demanding a sample from one particular can,
the pump-juice seller upset it and allowed the
contents to flow down the gutter. And the fatu-
ous magistrate fined him £5 for carrying the water
and a like amount for refusing a sample from the
overturned can, though he was well aware that he
had the power to make the first fine £2O and the
second £10 —indeed he mentioned the fact him-
self. Instead of being tapped with even a £2O
fine the blurred chalk-sign milkman who rattles
round with water on his cart, and heaves his alleged
milk over the roadway when asked for a sample,
should stand shoulder to shoulder with the person
who has been caught with housebreaking tools in
his possession. The offence is much the same ;
the only difference is that the man with the
jemmy is only out to rob the householder of his
cash ; whereas the adulterator is out to steal both
cash and health. This paper begins to suspect
that the people who keep a loaded revolver for the
furtive burglar who never arrives might make a
lot better use of it.
Just sailed from Hobart, for London via
ports, s.s. Papanui, with 110,000 cases of apples,
a valuable general cargo and an assortment of
coronators. For a lucky “spec” the Papanui
beats the whales lately blown ashore on the
northern beaches of Tasmania. Twelve months
ago she was proudly displaying the flag of the
Shaw, Savill and Albion Co., the big traders
between Maoriland and Britain, Originally her
cost was between £120,000 and £150,000, for the
Shaw-Savill is one of those firms that don’t stint
things when they are fitting out their mail boats.
When, last year, she ran on an uncharted rock
off the Tas. coast her value for some days was 2d.
Finally, she reached Melbourne, w-as condemned
by the underwriters, put up for auction, and
bought for a mere ditty by a Melbourne syndi-
cate. After a row with the Melbourne Marine
Board the Papanui staggered over the sea to Naga-
saki. There she was repaired by cheap Jap labor,
and after being returned to Australia, she was sur-
veyed for a first-class passenger certificate.
Expert and envious critics put down the total
cost of refitting at under £30,000, or say £lOO,OOO
less than her cost price. If the P. and 0., the
Orient, and the N.D.L. Co.’s can make a good
thing by running apples, coronators, wool and ore
to British ports in vessels representing (say)
£lOO,OOO each, there must be a nice margin for the
Melbourne syndicate which furbished up the
Papanui for a third of the money.
“Isaac Gumboil, J.P.”: Appalled at the
woeful lack of decency among the bathers, I
quitted that scene of sin, shapes and sunshine for
a quieter stretch of sand, where I could sit and
meditate upon the evils of Federation and bad
drainage. Suddenly I became aware of an un-
pleasant-looking man hovering about me, with a
most disquieting look in his eye, and his hand
fumbling mysteriously in the breast of his coat.
I didn’t like it at all. Rising quietly, I hurried
off; and, finding another secluded spot, again
settled down, hoping this time to be safe from all
interruption. But not so. Something rustled
amongst the trees behind me. Turning quickly,
I saw a figure moving towards me through the
foliage. My nerves could stand no more. Seizing
my bag of buns, I fled towards the wharf. Board-
ing a ferry, I eventually reached the Quay in
safety and a cold sweat. Somewhat unnerved,
and exceedingly annoyed over my wasted morn-
ing, I wandered across to the Domain, there to
rest and recover myself ’midst the sunshine and
flowers. But was my persecution at an end ? No.
A sudden rustle in the grass beside me, and some-
one sprang upon me. I was aware of a red,
whiskery face looking into mine with the sordid,
hungry glare of a State Premier spearing Federal
encroachments with a tooth-pick. “Sign it ! ’ he
hoarsely snarled, shoving a paper into my face.
With that awful, ravenous stare holding my eyes,
I quakingly scrawled as he directed. Then, rudely
snatching the, pen from my hand, he began to
move away. Recovering myself somewhat, I
seized him and held him back. “Might I ask,” I
began angrily, “what mean you by” He
fixed me with an offensively elated stare. “Shut
up !” he said. “ Ye’re worth twenty-five bob to
OUR State, yeh fool !” and abruptly departed in
eager search of further spoils.
THE DEGENERATION OF BILL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On account of the low prices charged for ad-
mission picture shows are largely patronised by
chUciren, and might be made not only a source, of
amusement, but one of instruction and profit. But
what do we find? Nearly every entertainment has
Tome wholesale stabbing or shooting, and murders
are made as realistic as possible —the criminal, by
the way, invariably escaping justice. I know of one
bov, who has not yet .seen seven Rummers who now
threatens to stab his sister. —William Medd, in S. M.
Herald.
We never rightly grasped what Willie was
Until we let him see a picture show,
An inoffensive little child he seemed. . . .
The lights, the crowd, the music—l dunno
Exactly what stirred all the bad in Bill,
And plunged him headlong in Sin s undertow.
The first we knew he went and took to Beer.
He stayed out nightly till eleven. Bill
Was one of the boy-drunks whom Nicholls saw
Blind tight in fourteen Sydney pubs. He’d fill
Himself with alcohol, then hurry forth
And thieve, or rob a bank or burst a till.
I recollect as clearly as can be
The night we took the lad to “Sunny Spain—
A modern and instructive film.” We hoped
That it might do Bill’s morals good. In vain.
On getting home he briskly seized an axe
And brained our harmless housemaid, Mary Jane.
He gambled at the races recklessly:
His face grew furtive, hard, and heavy-browed;
He went to fights, and, when his man got licked,
Bill cursed the vanquished heartily and loud.
He also robbed a church or two, and forged ;
And finally he joined the Labor crowd.
Mayhap the Angel Child of melodrame
Would pass unscathed from a picture show:
The Kid of Adams might not be transformed
From wondrous Goodness into something Low.
I lay no universal doctrine down:
I only write of Bill, whereof I know.
N.S.W.
PARENT OF TEN.
HOORAY FOR GRIEF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Trevor plays the part of Mary Rolfe in “The
Woman in the Case.” “I have played it for over 300
nights in London and the English provinces,” she ex-
plined, “and I am certain the piece, will make an
enormous hit here. The women rave about it. I
have to cry all night, and I can tell you it takes
every ounce out of me.” —Evening paper.
Tears spoil a pretty woman’s looks,
And all her make-up smear;
But in sad plays and sodden books
Grief earns much cheese and beer.
Grief is the hobby—nothing more—
Of human crocodiles
Who over actors weep and roar,
But watch men starve with smiles.
Long, long ago, big shameless tears
Were shed by ev’ry gent;
They hall-marked in those bygone years
The man of sentiment.
Men blubbered at a fearful rate,
And to their loves did kneel;
But now that pose is out of date —
All men are made of steel.
Thus, nowadays, not brave enough
To cry in street or hall,
Men pay to hear some written guff
And snivel in a stall.
But not a woman cares a blow
Who sees her sob and sniff;
She loves to get her tears to flow
And damp her handkerchief.
It proves to all she has “a heart,”
And gives her much relief;
With food and drink she’d rather part
Than with the joy of ‘Grief.
Hooray for Grief, the luxury
And source Of revenue! . . .
Come, smiling wife, and hurry tea
For I nave seats for two!
N.S.W.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
Fat’s press and other claqueurs often sug-
gest that the real reason why some men go on
strike is that they may have more time to get
drunk. During the current trouble at Renrnark
(S. A.) a bull’s-eye answer has been given to that
yarn. Ordinarily the strikers haven’t got any
control over the beer-weapons, and couldn’t spike
them if they wanted to; but at Renrnark
the one and only pub is owned by the people
and managed by an elected committee, on
which, as it happens, the Labor people
are in the majority. As soon as the strike began
the committee met, reckoned up that there was
every prospect of a long struggle, and decided
that, in the interests of the community, the liar
should be shut. And as there wasn’t any Mr.
Bung depending upon it for fat profits, shut it
was, and shut it has remained.

Melbourne City Council’s unwillingness to
license the taxi-cabs for “rank” purposes and fix
their scale of charges—which unwillingnesssmells
of jobbery and corruption—was a matter of more
than local interest last week. The Melbourne
Cabmen’s Union had a row with the inter-State
steamship owners, and passengers landing at
Melbourne found the wharves cabless. Also, as
many of them as took a taxi in default of a cab
found, to their sorrow, that the motor-power
vehicle was unlicensed and horribly expensive.
Seemingly, the City Council’s policy is to keep
the taxi horribly expensive until the public wakes
up and does some violent kicking in the right
place.
* * * *
“ Curlie ” : Victor Daley’s “Ballad of
Eureka” (B. 19/1/T1) struck me as one of his best.
Having read it, and being only a couple of hundred
yards away from the Ballarat Old Cemetery,
where lie the remains of soldiers
and diggers, I took the poem
with me to the spot. The
soldiers’grave is ornamented by
a monument, erected in 1879 by
“The Government of Victoria,”
at the request of the citizens of
Ballarat, and kept in splendid
order by, I expect, State funds.
The diggers’ monument, insignifi-
cant in size, although more classic
in design, was the gift of a towns-
man of Geelong, James Leggat,
in 185 G ; and the monument and
grave presented in their state of
neglect a marked contrast to the
other. But that is to be changed
now; for James Oddie has capped
his other gifts by leaving a sum
to keep the plot in order for all
time. Whilst I was decipher-
ing the names on the stone and
noting Daley’s accuracy in regard
to details, Young Australia, in
the person of two happy-go-lucky
ragamuffins passed whistling
along the cemetery path. They
were evidently going rabbiting;
for one proudly carried his ferret
in a box slung from his shoulder,
whilst the other kept a watchful
eye on a pert-looking fox-terrier.
They either knew or cared
nothing (let us hope the former) about Eureka—
their country’s first and, up to the present, only
battlefield.
The Wellington (M.L.) Wharf Laborers’
Union lias been punched by a heavy legal decision
which will warn unions to be careful how they
handle defaulters. One Flowers joined in
190 G, at a time when preference to unionists
had not been granted, and, some time later,
having failed to keep up his payments, was
struck off the books. In March, 1908, pre-
ference was granted, and the employers of
Flowers were notified that his continued
employment meant a breach of the award.
So Flowers handed up 3s. 6d.—2s. 6d.
as a new entrance fee, and Is. as a month’s
subscription. The union closed on the
money, claimed it as part payment of
arrears, and left Flowers at work as a
unionist. In April, 1910, employers of
wharf labor were again given notice that
Flowers was a non-unionist. Flowers
then made his second move. He ap-
pealed to the Supreme Court for a man-
damus to compel the union to recognise him
as a member, an injunction restraining it
from representing him as a non-unionist
and £175 damages for loss of employment.
Justice Cooper said the legal equivalent of
“Right-o,” but sheared damages down to
£5O plus the old, reliable Costs. He
figured it that the 3s. Gd. handed up by
Flowers in 1908 was for new membership,
and should have been viewed in that light
by the union ; and as the plaintiff had not
been 12 months in arrears since that date,
he was ipso facto (also ipso jure and
the rest of the family) fully entitled to
the privileges of membership. So Flowers
comes out with flying petals. He doesn’t
have to pay up his old arrears, he is
enrolled as a new member, he hands his
bill for Horsehair to the union, and lands
hpme with £49 19s. in his pocket and two
drinks in Ins digestion. Meanwhile the
union will stay home o’ nights and think
out a less expensive way of catching its
defaulters.
“ Aeneas B.”: The sputter of excitement
caused in Melbourne by the appearance of
the harem skirt diverted attention from
another Collins-street attraction. A photo-
grapher, pushinga special line of wedding pictures,
exhibited a collection of these sad groups. In the
same window hung a card bearing the artless
legend—“ Wanted, a boy.”
The agitated Maorilander has broken out
in a fresh place, and when the mail left was shak-
ing his brolly before the highly-colored poster-
pictures of “ The Bad Girl of the Family” on the
Auckland hoardings. Nobody appears to have
felt any thrill concerning this young woman’s
lithographs until the director of technical educa-
tion, the headmaster of the Grammar School, the
principal of the Training College and the head-
master of the Normal School wrote to the Mayor
concerning the pictures :
In our opinion, they are a dishonor to womanhood, and
have a demoralising influence on the young people of this
city. We think the time has come when all such pictures
should be submitted to a public censor.
The thing which jangled the educational gentle-
men’s modesty the most depicted several girls in
nighties attacking a couple of policemen who had
entered their dormitory to arrest the Bad
Girl. Upon publication of the letter, inhabit-
ants who had looked at the picture hundreds
of times without seeing anything suggestive or
unusual in it, hurried to the nearest hoard-
ing to have another look ; newspapers gravelv
discussed the matter without venturing ail
opinion; the town clerk was interviewed and
declared that, had he been poster censor, the
pictures would have been passed without hesita-
tion ; and the Wowser was left flapping his ears
preparatory to another upward bound. A re-
markable person is the Maorilander—or some of
him ; a queer cuss who periodically throws his
morality on the counter presumably to prove that
it has not been pickled in sly grog.

“ Paroa ” : The Maoris have another
legend besides that given by “Konini” concern-
ing the famous fish, Felorus Jack. It was
given me on the authority of a Nelson (M.L.)
chief thus :
A northern rangatira on a visit to a South Island pa
became enamored of a dusky maiden, who was just as
willing. Unfortunately, father stepped in and ordered
the bloke away ; but he continued to hang round till all
his followers died or deserted and his canoe rotted to
pieces. At last the gods took pity on the lovers and gave
to each the shape and qualifications of a fish, where-
upon they shook the sand from their tails and
departed for many years of salt-water bliss. At last
the fish-lady died. The fish-gentleman, in his great grief,
decided that he would live alone in a cave in Pelortis
Sound, and in time became known to the pakeha as
Pelorus Jack.
I don’t vouch for the yarn myself; but my canni-
bal friend says he’ll wager te hogshead of beer it
te fac’.
* * • •
London papers announce that whiskers
are coming ih. Already the smartest young men
of the smartest set are exhibiting luxuriant face-
fur. No doubt this is man’s answer to the trouser-
ette. Coats go with the harem skirt, and the
advanced women openly declare that these are
only the beginning of a complete rational costume
for the sex. So, almost unconsciously, man falls
back upon his sacred whiskers as the one adorn-
ment that piratical woman cannot parody.
Heaven alone knows, though, how soon it will be
before the world is resounding to the shriek of the
still revolting sex. shaking Heaven with a new
shibboleth, “ Whiskers for women ! ”

THOUGHTS FOR GEORGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The majority of girls give little consideration to their
capacity for making their future husbands happy. Some-
thing more than love may be necessary to the comfort of
the home. —Daily paper.
When that my love forgetteth oft to shave,
The thorny prickles of his manly cheek
Fill me with longing his dear soul to save
From boarding-houses bleak.
The beef on which they foster him is tough,
The gravy hath much grease about the brim ;
How shall my fingers white delight to stuff
The juicy duck for him !
To brown the singing rooster, grill the chop
In days to come shall be my wifely pride ;
To build f lir things with beaten egg a-top,
Each like a veilbd bride.
My love is never exquisite in dress ;
I fain would minister to nil his wants.
Whiten his cloudy cuffs, and learn to press
Ilis rather baggy pants.
For him at steaming washtuhs would I stand,
Soothing the weekly stains of toil and care ;
Chastening his singlet and his collar-band,
Fit for my king to wear.
He shall have towels always in their place.
What time ho cometh from his daily toil ;
And water for the harvest of his face
Shall still be on the boil.
Beer shall he have, if he desireth beer ;
And if he howleth curses down the stairs,
His words shall fall as music on mine ear—
Sweet as the drone of prayers.
DrUnk and rebellious, I shall love him more ;
Solemn and dull, I shall not love him less ;
The raucous cadence of his midnight snore
Shall still have power to bless.
So shall the happy days to music run,
And gentle service shall be all my pride ;
And thus I wait in meekness to be won
And made mj’ George’s bride.
x.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

The calm of an eminently expensive Mel-
bourne restaurant was disturbed on a recent race
night. Dinner was in progress, nothing was
heard but the clamor of the lucky bookmakers
biting their soup, when the detached woman
entered the room. She was tall, quietly dressed,
and had all the lady-like affectations dear to
reporters. She looked round, then stood by one
table, gazing calmly at two diners. The diners
were male and female. Their attitude was in
marked contrast to the composed demeanor of the
spectator. They presented all the symptoms of
guilty wretches in the face of righteous retribu-
tion. The woman went on looking down at them.
She did not speak, she betrayed no feeling, and
the situation was painfully strained, when the
elegant minister for internal affairs came up
anxiously, and laid a hand on her shoulder. “My
dear, good lady,” he expostulated. The scrutineer
addressed him very sweetly, but in clear, pene-
trating tones. “ Please, do not interfere,’ she
said. “I am going. I merely wished to see how
my clothes looked on that woman with my hus-
band ! ” Then she turned and walked forth,
leaving two miserable wretches making ineffectual
attempts to hide themselves in oyster shells.
» * * *
Seemingly the “Little Mary ” grovel, for
the purpose of adding Coronation gifts to the
Queen’s hoard of superfluities, has been frost-
bitten all over the empire whereon the sun never
sets. A cable from London last week announced
that the date for emptying the collection-plate
had been postponed to the end of May, and the
name of Miriam had been added to the list of
those fortunate females who are related by wet-
baptism and dry-registration to King George’s
wife. The extra time and the extra name suggest
frantic efforts to raise a few more shillings. The
next cablegram on the subject will probably state
that Martha has been added to the list, because
Martha was “ the sister of Mary” and was much
concerned about many things. And how about
Maudie, Minnie and Mamie ? Can’t they be
called upon in the Queen’s name to push the
concern ?

The wreck of the Yongala makes the third
very bad disaster on the Queensland coast; the
other victims were the Quetta and the Gothen-
burg. Over 100 lives were lost with each of these
three. The trouble came to the Gothenburg on
February 24, 1875. Among those who were
drowned then were several well-known South Aus-
tralians returning from the Northern Territory—
Judge Wearing, ex-Premier Reynolds, Crown
Prosecutor Whitby, and others. About 30 of the
passengers were saved, mainly through the intre-
pidity of Jack Cleland and Fitzgerald, the former
of whom still lives in the Territory. The vessel
met such just such a storm as that which proved
fatal to the Yongala, which, by the way, derived
her name from a South Australian farming town
on the northern railway line. In the Gothenburg
wreck the then Resident of the Territory lost his
wife and six children.
A FOND REMEMBRANCE.
OLD MONEY (ying): “ I'm afraid Eve been
a brute to you sometimes, dear
YOUNG WIFE: “ Oh, never mind that, darling;
Fll always remember how very kind you
were when you left me."
Some shocking persons,
it seems, have been rude to chose
fine men who have taken on jobs
at the various Victorian im-
plement works where the
regular toilers are on strike.
Some of the rudest people,
it appears, have called
these fine men “scabs ”
and “blacklegs,” and
the Melbourne press
is horrified, and says
that something violent
ought to be done.
The Agk, the other
day, referring to the
execution of a French
hooligan, even hinted
that it would be a
good thing to treat
some of the Melbourne
strike sympathisers the
same way. lam going
to try an experiment
when the Jap invasion
comes along. When all
the other Australians go
on strike against them, I
will hurry to the daily
paper offices and tell them
that it is a glorious thing
to go over to the other side,
as it shows that I have
some of that grand independ-
ence of my forefathers ; and
I will add that I will not be
forced to join the Australian
army, even if it is fighting
on my behalf. Of course, the
daily won’t call me a “scab,”
or a blackleg, or a dastardly rebel,
or a traitor ; but it will shake my
hand, pat my curly hair, and re-
mark that I am a splendid example
of independent thought and a brave
fellow for refusing to be coerced into
fighting for my brothers.
The S. M. Herald lately published a letter
over the name of Caroline Grosvenor on “The
Shortage of Domestics ”: —
There is, however, luckily for Australia, another large
class of women in England from which it is possible, under
certain conditions, to obtain the much-needed help.
Many of the better-educated women of a superior class
are now forced to earn their living. These are thoroughly
trained in all branches of domestic work, and are quite
willing to undertake such work in the overseas dominions,
where no stigma attaches to honest work of any kind.
This is Australia’s salvation right enough. But
what a nasty thing to say about the Dear Old
Home—that it is a reproach there to do honest
work !

“The Shover”: Recently I attended a
Labor meeting in the Melbourne Town Hall. I
was late in arriving—just half an
hour before the proceedings were due
to start —but at length managed to
squeeze in between two pipes of the
grand organ. Next day, the Mel-
bourne press coldly remarked that
“ Mr. Fisher had no reason to be dis-
satisfied with the attendance.”
Another night I went to a “ m< nuer
meeting” of Laborites at the Ex-
hibition Building. To save space
chairs had been suppressed, and
four people were sardined where
at the Town Hall meeting only
one enthusiast had sat. Those on
the outskirts were so far from the
speakers that theycould hearnothing
The account given next day spnte
slightingly of a mere 5000 people !
Later on I attended the formation
of a branch of the “Commonwealth
Liberal Party.” There was a dog
on the door-step, and one or two
agents inside the large and gloomy
hall ; also a brace of reporters, and
a dispirited chairman. Next day’s
Aegus referred to the meeting as a
well-attended and satisfactory gather-
ing, indicative of the state of public feel-
ing. Somehow, I almost begin to suspect
these Melbourne journals of one-sidedness,
or misrepresentation, or defective eyesight,
or something.
Paris House, the expensive manger to
which Sydney’s young man goes when he feels
unusually affluent or unusually flighty, has taken
over the neighboring pub-property on the King-
Castlereagh street corner; and indue course, rio
doubt, the young man will be a devil of a fellow there.
In Svdnev’s early days the corner was put to quite a
different use. On it was the school and residence
of Mr. Cape, who whipped the three R’s into quite
half of the public men who flourished in Sydney a
generation ago. Later on The Three Tuns public-
house arrived, engineered by another distinguished
colonist, Richard Driver, for years a big man in
the city, and father of Dick Driver, a well-known
sporting solicitor. * *
The Law is slowly realising its responsi-
bilities to accused persons. The other day it
abolished the spiked and barred dock in Mel-
bourne’s Criminal Court, and substituted a sort of
wooden pew, not quite as luxurious as the benches
of the House of Fat, yet still mueh less uncom-
fortable than the press form or the jury-box.
When juries were instituted, the Law seems to
have suffered from the idea that they were as
antagonistic to society as the criminal, and placed
them in much the same sort of a den, and it
hasn’t improved their position since; but it has
gradually dawned on Authority that the criminal
really is necessary, and that if there weren’t any
criminals there would he a lot of officials out of a
job. Consequently it is laying itself out to be
affable to that animal. But jurymen still don’t
count; the Law thinks it could get along very
well without them.
SUE WANTED A QUICK RETURN.
“I'm not in exactly opulent circumstances, but
my life is insured for five thousand pounds."
“ Oh, yes, but you're very healthy, aren't
you?"
At Alfred Deakin’s State Frights meeting
in Adelaide the people who were running the
patter show had the gall to sing, as a prelude,
“The Song of Australia,” which S. Australia
regards as the Commonwealth National Anthem.
The printed programmes scattered through the
hall contained the words, in case anybody shouldn t
know what the rest were bellowing ; and when
the last “Australia” had been sung, Alfred got
up and kicked the whole spirit of the thing to
fragments. Obviously, if the State Frighters will
sing “ The Song of Australia ” at their meetings,
it ought to be in a revised version. For instance :
This is a land where feeble lies,
Stained with the good old Tory dyes,
Vain efforts make to harmonise—to harmonise.
On ev’ry stump and platform bright
Each “ Liberal ” and wild State Fright,
Like large mosquitoes, buzz and bite
Australia, Australia, Australia.
This is a land where drivel flows,
And where the mortgage swiftly grows,
Land of the Pawnshop and the Nose—our
Uncle’s Nose.
On hill and plain the evil sign
Is hanging over yours and mine,
To keep it there two “ Noes” entwine,
Australia, Australia, Australia.
This is a land from whose white throat
The hands of Death are not remote
You must not wound it with your vote, your
two-edged vote.
Heed not the fool nor his address,
Equip your nation for the stress
By marking down a double “ Yes,”
Australia! Australia, Australia!

The Oldest Inhabitant remembers another
Oldest Pub :
The White Hart Hotel, a landmark on the corner of
TSourke and Spring streets, Melbourne, the resort of Par-
is unentarians for over half a century, was offered at
auction sale a day or two ago. The origina l White
Hart dates back long anterior to the building of Pailia-
ment Houses, when scrub prevailed between the top of
Bourke-street and Newtown, as Oolhngwood was then
named. The original owner of the Spring-street frontage
from Little Collins-street to Bourke-street was Mr. r. L.
pqmnhell who was police magistrate at Maitland
(N.S.W.) at the time he purchased. In the first ’:>o’s the
occupier of the old inn was R. N. Coleman, who had for
next-door neighbor “Jimmy” Ellis, formerly of Cre-
morne Gardens, London, and afterwards manager
of the Cremorne Gardens, on the Yarra, for George
Goppin The Amphitheatre had not then been
built bv Mr. G. B. W. Lewis on the site now
occupied by the Princess’s Theatre. Next door to the mn
in Bourke-street was John Cray, “cab proprietor, though
Melbourne’s first hansom hadn’t then arrived to stand on
the rank in front of Scott’s Hotel. Next to Cray was a
saddler named Bullock, whose shop was very soon to be
occupied hv Henry Tolman Dwight, an asthmatic book-
seller who had an immense stock, chiefly second-hand.
Outside was a man in spectacles, who constantly cried :
“ Books, books ; buy, buy ! ” When Mr. Dwight died in
1872 his stock numbered 35,000 volumes, and Cohen and
Co. took 14 days to sell the'4soo lots.
“Ex-Papuan ” : “The expedition cost.the
lives of 17 natives, who perished either by starva-
tion or drowning.” Such is the news we get from
Thursday Island concerning the latest Papuan
expedition. Yet no white man died either from
too little food or too much water. If the captain
of a ship loses part of his crew by starvation or
drowning, he has to explain the matter before the
Admiralty or the Marine Board ; and what is
sauce for the water-fowl ought to be sauce
for the land-fowl. Among other things, we
should know the terms on which the natives went
on the expedition, and whether the relatives of
those who perished are to get any compensation
for the loss of their bread-winners, or are to be
just left to console themselves with the thought
that the white man neither starves nor drowns,
while 17 of his native mates and trustworthy ser-
vants go to their deaths.

“ Billy Beerseeker,” from the Cows :
The local dairy cookies, despite their inborn conser-
vatism and aversion to all change which they can’t bank
look with a kindly eve on the harem skirt. What appeals
to their practical minds is the utility of this latest craze
in feminine garments. It is a rational cow-yard dress ;
Arrayed in it, Jinny will be Jimmy’s equal, not only at
“ bailing up,” but at holding the milk-bucket between t he
knees, and in following the harrowing team in the culti-
vation plot. Jinny long ago annexed Jimmy’s headgear
for out-door exercise ; now she is being persuaded to go
after his leg-gear also. And from the divided skirt to
dungarees will be a short stride for the emancipated
limbs.

Melbourne Age talks truth on the liquor
question :
In Victoria the Sons of Temperance complain that their
young recruits are falling off. Yet public opinion in favor
of temperance is on the increase. ,
Popular sentiment was lately voiced by a keen-
witted Melbourne barmaid, who has been in the
business many years: “Don’t worry about the
Drink Evil. It is curing itself.” If Sons were
not taking to Temperance without signing any
pledge, there would be more force at the back of
the demand made by the latest local missioner—
one Dr. Henry—for stronger legislation. But,
as a matter of fact, Intemperance is the bottom
dog nowadays, and if the Law stayts to kick it
when it’s down. Temperance is liable to turn round
and take its part.

Cheering news item from Queensland :
In the Brisbane Oitv Summons Court to-day John
Chappel, laborer, was fined £2 for expectorating on the
footpath. Defendant was allowed 30 days to pay the
fine, in default 14 days’ gaol.
If everyone who spat on a Sydney or Melbourne
footpath were dealt with on similar lines,
McGowen and Murray would be able for a while
to spurn the pawnbroker, and the dissemination
of consumption and other loathsome and fatal
diseases would be decreased by about 50 per cent.
The N.S.W. and Vic. police authorities might
desist from their untiring pursuit of two-uppers,
and give this matter a moment’s thought.

THE CHILTERN “TIGER.”
Chiltern, Monday.—The tiger scare has
now subsided through the exertions of
Constable Carruthers. Barnawartha. The
tracks which helped to alarm the forest
worker were followed to Mr T. Doolan’s
estate, where a large dog—a cross be-
tween mastiff, staghound and pointer,
striped very much like a tiger—was dis-
covered. The huge animal is noted as
the best fox killer in the district. _

CIRCUMSTANCES ALTER FACES.
TRAVELLER: “ Can you tell me how far it is to Booblekyne?"
JACKY: “ Oh, yes, boss. Berry long way you walk. Him not so far you ride. Drive

“Sapphire Smith”: On sunny days the
fish that walks may often be seen taking his consti-
tutional on the mud flats beside the Cairns ' (Q.)
wharves. He varies from 4in. to Bin. in length;
tapers cigar-like from a very bluff head to the place
where he leaves off; is dark brown in color, al-
though I have a suspicion the complexion is alter-
able to accord with the surroundings; and has ab-
normally large eyes standing out like boot-buttons
from a steep frontal bone. Progress over the slush
is accomplished with the aid of two stiff-looking fins,
apparently reinforced with fine cartilage, set close up
to the head. At the least motion on the watcher’s
part, the beast executes a bewildering series of aban-
doned handsprings, finally landing in the nearest pot-
hole, and burying himself in the silt. I don’t know
what his endurance record is for staying out of the
wet, but probably he returns as soon as he finds he
is getting dry feet.
“Saladin” on a national habit: —
We had been talking of some costly “shouts,”
when someone recalled that Jack Gray, of Gray s
Find” (W.A.), set ’em up at the cost of £lOO the
day the stamps dropped on his first crushing. Inci-
dentally, it was stated that the same Jack, who
had handled over £60,000 from the Find, was back
on scratch again. A Bananalander, who told of the
royal “shouts” of the squatters in Central Queens-
land at the time the Canadian Borers were striking
artesian water, declared it to be an open question
which flowed the more freely—the water from the
bore at Barcaldine, or the liquor in the township.
Then there was the celebrated “shout” at Strike-
me-Blind, once a flourishing little township on the
W A Goldfields. There were two rag-and-bag
shanties in the place, doing a roaring business, while
awaiting the erection of permanent buildings on the
main street of the town site. Permission was given by
the warden that the new pubs should be opened on
New Year’s Day, but on the New Year s Eve, when
he arrived on a visit of inspection, a few of the
bovs put it to him that the occasion was worthy
of throwing onen the new bars, which were already
stocked, at once. At the thirteenth whisky he
agreed, and things hummed. For some time there
had been bad blood between the two pubkcans, and
some of the jokers set out to make the peace be-
tween them. Rollins, being told that Keegan spoke
very well of him, and was anxious to make it up,
promptly gathered all hands and marched to Kee-
gan’s pub. “Let the boys have some wine,” he
said smiting the counter; “and it’s champagne I
mean, Mr. Keegan.” And it was. Case after case
was split °P en - all han< l s mopping it up out of
mugs, and glasses, and pint pots, and Jack -Sheas,
and some of them out of bottles. Men who had
never previously tasted champagne—and who prob-
ably never have tasted it since—had a skinful that
night. In the thick of the guzzle Keegan sprang
on the counter and called for silence. “Boys,” he
announced. “I’m taking me frind the warden up to
Jerry Rollins’s, and I want yez all to come wid us,
and have wan more or so before the old year be
forgotten.” By daylight there wasn't a of
sparkle left in either pub, and a cyclist had to be
sent along the track to meet the teams, broach a
case of wine, and bring in a few bottles to fix up
the warden, so that he could preside over his Court.
All the auditors in the West could not have
straightened the accounts between Rollins and
Keegan, so they agreed to call it quits.

“Leo Lear” : Afraid I can’t elucidate for
“James Pannikin” (B. 9/3/’ll), the reason for the
sheep man’s sense of the beautiful being above that
of the gent who farms the horned animal; but such
an expert on bush hospitality as “J. Pan.” should
have discovered that gardens and hospitality usually
run together. Approach a station selection or humpy
that possesses a garden, and you need have no qualms
on enquiring within for tucker or a bunk. The
heart-rending job of running a garden without a water
supply no doubt accounts for many a barren home-
stead environment; still the bush home that has not
a geranium or honeysuckle on the premises is a
rarity. Incidentally, I would place these two an easy
first as the most common and widely-grown flowers
Outback.
“Curse o’ Moses”: “Waipuna’s” notion
(B. 2/3/’ll) that white clover will exercise a
check on the egg-depositing extravagances of the
blue-bottle buzzer may be all right north of Auck-
land ; but it is all wrong in the wilds of inland Nel-
son and Westland (M.L.). Nothing is of any use
against the pest except darkness. The sizzling hor-
rors will not operate to any extent after the light
goes out; but the man who dared to wear a button-
hole of white clover, in order to protect his hat
from insult, would be merely offering defiance to
tne enemy. The blowfly is a bird with whom it is
policy to keep on good terms. Treat him trucu-
lently, and nothing about your camp, from a cellu-
loid collar to a crowbar, is safe. Should you de-
cide on war, however, use burning sulphur. For
anyone who likes the scent it is admirable; but you
must catch the flies to administer it.
LEFT HIM IN THE LURCH.
WAYBACK PARSON. (consolingly): “ It’s very gratifying indeed, Mr. Grabbit, to hear you
speak so movingly of the demise of your dear wife. You must indeed miss her."
GRABBIT: “Miss ’er! Miss ’er ! Cast yer eye over them crops waitin' ter be cut, an'
them poddy calves s ingin' out ter be fed, an that fence wot wants mendin', an'
that wood'cap to be cut—an' 'er goin' orf of a sudden on top of it all! She might
’ave ’ung on till after Harvestin', anyhow."
“Kola”: “Stickpicker” (B. 20/311), in
his remarks about the carryings-on of a wasp and a
large spider, casts an unwarranted aspersion on the
lady wasp’s character. He says he has noticed her
occasionally “dragging the spider home, either very
weak or very sozzled.” But it was a different spider
each time. And she had previously stung him, which
accounts for his “sozzled” appearance. When she got
him home she packed him away, after depositing an
egg in his body. There, not quite dead, and yet
hardly alive, the spider will lie until the . egg is
hatched. The young wasp will then proceed to use
the spider as a lunch-basket, and eventually nothing
but the skin will be left. By that time the young
wasp will 'be able to go out into the world and hustle
for himself.
* * * *
“Leo Lear”: So all this howl against the
cocky petered out like a dry storm in a drought, and
the maligned citizen can hold up his head in the
land and open his mouth without being condemned as
a stray devil. Now that the attack has expended
itself, he can step out into the open, hoist his flag on
the roof of the silo, and to the applause of his own
separator, winnower, harvester, or eornsheller engine,
proclaim his virtues as under: —
(1) That he is an indispensable adjunct to the
prosperity of Australia. .
(2) That early in life he blesses himself with a
wife, and rears more young Australians per acre than
any of his traducers.
(3) That he puts in more solid graft per day than
the next man, and solid graft is a valuable asset in
opening a country.
(4) That he turns a desert into an orchard and a
sheep-walk into a wheat-field.
(5) That his freights pay for most things above .
axle-grease on the railways.
(6) That a life of hard beds, hard graft and hard
tucker is moulding him into a defender Australia
will be glad of in the day of trouble.
“Sleepy Dick”: Re par. in “Aboriginali-
ties” (B. 16/3/’ll) in which “Arsemo” asks for in-
formation concerning jungle fowl. The bird is pro-
bably the lowan, or mallee hen, as it is named by
most bushmen. Have seen one of their nests in the
Swan Hill mallee (Vic.) sft. high, about 30ft. in
diameter at the ground level, and conical in shape.
The nest is chiefly composed of sand, with a few de-
cayed leaves in the centre where the eggs are placed.
When pioneering, in 1894, I visited the nest in ques-
tion once a week for three months, and gathered on an
average four eggs per visit. The remarkable thing
about the nests is that there is no evidence of the
sand being taken from anywhere in the vicinity.
How do they get it there ?
“Jack Jay”: “Hamer” (B. 2/3/’ll) runs
foul of the theory of the scientist (wasn t it
Macmillan Brown?) who laid it down that the
Maori will be absorbed in the white race. Though
I had pretty well accepted the Mac-Brown theory
on personal experience, “Hamer’s” statements made
me sit up and put a wet towel round my head,
After thinking of 10 families with the touch of the

WHEN THE PUMPRING BLGOM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In summer—dusty, dried-up, good, old, blazing
summer-time—
Oh, see the fatted pumpkins, blooming in their
summer prime!
The pigs may pine for skim-milk, and the cows may
nose for grass
Where frequent, fatted grasshoppers the care-free
moments pass ;
But there’s a good time coming when, in autumn,
once again
The bulging, bloated pumpkins show they have not
bloomed in vain.
What though the spring-cart come to grief upon its
rocky trip
For tucker from the township—let the blanky tucker
rip !
Although the larder's getting bare, and presently we
see
Our bacon’s running out; although in winter-time,
maybe,
The weekly butcher’s bushed, or blocked by roaring
creeks aflood,
We see good tucker clear ahead when all the pump-
kins bud.
When, later in the season, there is little left to eat,
Since father’s sold the goat and some financier owns
the wheat,
And prime, unpoisoned rabbit’s scarce, and ’pos-
sum’s overdone ;
When all the roosters silently have vanished one by
one,
And every blessed pullet has refused to play the
game,
Ripe pumpkin, always on the spot, comes forward
just the same.
Ah, when the kindly pumpkins bloom, we see new
hope ahead ;
With pumpkin-chops and pumpkin-steaks the house-
hold will be fed,
And pumpkin-soup there soon will be, and pumpkin-
pie all right,
’Twill send us whistling to our work and welcome us
at night.
We have no time for cursing, now; there is no need
for gloom. ... ;
There’s hope ahead of us, in tons, when all the
pumpkins bloom 1

TURN AS THE RIVERS IN THE SOUTH.
[For The Bulletin.]
Here, In a land of drought and snn,
Where shrunken creeks and rivers crawl,
And never through the gullies run
Those fountains immemorial,
With thirsty longing I recall
The old days when we used to stand
And watch the white-flecked, hurrying fall
Of the good rivers of Maoriland.
Waiho, Waimate, Waitaha, Waitaki,
Havelock, Wilberforce, Hunter, Hook,
Tekapo, Taipo, Twain, Pukaki,
Catlins, Craigieburn, Copeland, Cook.
Was it not good, a journey done,
Along the tussock flats to sprawl,
And see the river shingle spun
Under the haze meridianal?
Or in a gorge where green cliffs wall
The bush-fringed banks on either hand,
To loiter in a shaded hall
Of the good rivers of Maoriland?
Otekaiko, Otepopo, Otematata,
Taieri, Shotover, Ilaast, Ohau,
Waimakariri, VVainui, Waimata,
Poulter, Wedderlmrn, Potts, Wsirau
Of all the thousand streams, there’s none,
Spring-fed or snow-fed, great or small,
East coast or west, that has not won
Me to be evermore its thrall.
O mountain valleys and birches tall!
O shining ti-tree and silver sand !
My heart is weary for each and all
Of the good rivers of Maoriland.
Clinton, Clarence, Karangarua,
Kowhai, Molvneux, Cass, Kaihu,
Mawliera, Maitai, Maraewhenua,
Ivyeburn, Manorburn, Manawatu.
Old friend, you know them, every one.
Do the old names make you understand
How to an exile comes the call
Of the Good Rivers of Maoriland?
Saltwater, Clearwater, Grey, Ahaura.
Deepstream, Silverstrcam, Clyde, Waiau,
Lowburn, Lindisburn, Rees, Mataura,
Gimmerburn, Poolburn, Teremakau.
MAORILAND.

An inside view of big-game hunting, as
shown in a letter from Henry Tarlton, formerly of
S. Aus., but now of Central Africa, brother of
the Tarlton who “ran” Roosevelt’s expedition there:—
We went away again last September after rhino,
buffalo, and hippo, but we only brought back two
young rhino for three months’ strenuous work. I
sold the small one for £150, and still have the big
fellow. He is said to be the largest yet caught,
and took 15 of us to handle him after I got the
rope on ... He gave a lot of trouble for about
four days, but soon got tame with those lie knew.
He broke an Indian’s leg coming through Fort
Hell —just to show how it was done . . . He is quite
tame now, and goes out grazing all day with his
koy, following like a dog . . . Leslie’s friendship
with Roosevelt has been productive of several new
clients to murder game. This big-game hunting is
a poor affair. The game does not get even a
sporting chance. All the “sportsmen” use different
calibre of cannon, and most have a white hunter
behind to take the brunt of a charge, if it should
come.

“H.S.M.”: Was travelling with a friend
recently, when, just outside Scone (N.S.W.), some-
thing began to squeak, and we pulled up beside a cot-
tage to investigate. A crowd of three small young-
sters immediately collected, and looked on while my
friend poked about under the car. “Give us the
oil can,” he sang out; “there’s something wrong
with her inside.” I got the can, but it was empty ;
so I said to the oldest child, a girl of about 10
years: “Would you, like a good girl, run and ask
your mother if she could give us a little oil?” She
ran away, and returned breathlessly to say: “Please
mother hasn’t any oil, but she says if you wait a
minute she will give you some salts!”
“Penitent Form”: I wish to announce my
conversion. For years I simply laughed at The
Bulletin’s attitude towards Loans and Borrowing.
I took the side of the Herald and Telegraph. I
assiduously practised borrowing on my own account.
The following are some of my loans: —
120 half-crowns (because I left my money in my
other trousers’ pocket).
3978 shillings and sprats (for a feed).
9586 thrums (towards a feed).
20,000 browns (for tram fares).
3000 profane books.
8742 pipes of tobacco.
1,798,430 matches.
587 umbrellas.
50,000 Bulletins.
6999 drinks (stuck up in back country).
3 drinks (stuck up in Sydney).
13 bottles whisky (borrowed when barmen’s backs
were turned).
1 bottle limejuice (mistake).
But never again. I didn’t know the monster I was
playing with till the Telegraph, the whilom cham-
pion of loans, opened my eyes. On March 4, talking
about the wild animals of Egypt, it said •
In the various parts of the Anglo-Egyptian Soudan,
where loans are excessively abundant, they ap-
pear to be an unmitigated nuisance, doing much
damage yearly to domestic animals and to hoofed
game; they also kill many human beings; but, as
is the case with lives lost through crocodiles, the
native headmen seem adverse to reporting these
deaths, and the authorities are not generally in-
formed of the lives lost under these circumstances.
This dreadful intelligence has caused a reaction. I
have already handed back the bottle of limejuice to
Mr. Bung. I wish I could return some of the drinks
I borrowed west of the Darling on condition that
Bung himself had to swallow them.
