“Les”: Have heard of Australians who
make pets of snakes, but have come across only one
instance of it myself. With a mate, I was travel-
ling the Northern Rivers (N.S.W.) selling jewellery
ana spectacles, and one afternoon approached a
homestead, in front of which was a luxuriant pas-
sion-fruit vine. The banquet to which we invited
ourselves was abruptly terminated by a large carpet
snake. My mate promptly rushed for a sapling.
Just as he was about to use it, an old, wrinkled
woman ran out screaming: “For God’s sake don’t
kill Emily; she’s bin with us fer three years.” We
made apologies, and were invited inside for a bite.
The old lady told us that her husband was dead,
that her sons were not at home much, that she had
made pets of carpet snakes, and that they were “real
company.” When the tea was poured out, I was
going to sit down on an old-fashioned arm-chair,
when the hostess loosed another shriek. “Look
out,” she cried; “don’t sit on pore Flossie.” I
promise you I didn’t, for Flo was the living image
of Em. Next instant I had slammed the lid on the
box of samples (which we had opened in hope of
trade), Jim had dived for his bowler hat, and we
stampeded for the open. Imagine the horror of
James when more snake started to climb out of his
hat. He let out one blood-curdling yell, and dropped
in his tracks. I don’t know how we got away
from that place, but four miles along the road we
heard a horse galloping behind us, and presently a
sawny, freckled youth pulled up and demanded his
mother’s snake, which he accused us of stealing.
The faithful chronicle of “Phil Random”:
“I’ll be leaving at the end of the week, Mr.
Grady,” said the youth to the cocky, ’way down on
the Murray.
“Oh, you’ll be lavin’, will you? An’ where might
you be makin’ for ?” said Grady, reproachfully.
“Well, I’m going up to Queensland,” said the
youth.
“Queensland, eh? Queensland, is it? Then ye’U
repint it, me bhoy. That’s the fust place I set me
fut whin 1 kem to Australia. And it’s there you’ll
have the flies be day and the iniskaties be night.
They’ll go in your tay and on your mait —an’ down
your troat, whollop. An’ it’s there ye’ll see the
wild horse buck. The divil a thing he’ll do at all,
whin yez straddle him, but chuck ’imself heels over
head, and slither out iv the saddle an’ bridle widout
ever disturbin’ a buckle or a sthrap. Yes, an’ it’s
there ye’ll see the straight-haired native wid the
goold ring on his finger an’ the girl tatooed on his
arm—and the divil a thing at all he does for a livin’
but toss the two pinnies, tails uppards, an’ cry ‘Bar
’em.’ And whin yez go down to the crake in the
mornin’ for a dhrink o’ water, the alligator ’ll
schnap the head off you. Queensland, is ut?”
And even after that Grady had to find another help.
“A William-Cann”
“Jimmie Pannikin” inquires (B. 9/3/’ll) why
successful cattlemen live simply, whereas sheepmen,
so soon as their bank balances disclose a bulge,
proceed to “enlarge and beautify their gardens” and
entertain “Pannikin.” My view, which is hereby
presented to my old travel-companion for what it is
worth, is that constant association with sheep has
a malign effect on the brain.
But why cite the cattle-man as typifying the
simple liver? A far more Spartan character is the
ovster-raneher. Years ago I shared a mollusc-farm
with a person who lied to me that he was “Pan-
nikin.” We came by the estate in a queer way.
This pseudo “Pannikin” found the money—l saw
him. Plagiarising the sorry hero of “A Message from
Mars,” 1 remarked firmly, significantly, but withal
quietly, “Halves.” “Pannikin’s” reply was unprint-
able. We decided, after a more or less heated dis-
cussion, to put the plunder into a shell-fish run.
Let me here digress, in order to point out that
there are features about this industry which render
it superior, as a business proposition, to any that I
have engaged in. The Land Tax is impotent to harm
it. Droughts it simply does not know. No herd of
shearers, avid for a living-wage, crowd hungrily
round the oyster baron. Extravagantly-salaried
boundary-riders are unnecessary ; also rabbit-poisoners
and their outfits, pear-eradieators, horses, machinery,
and the myriad other costly details which harass the
bucolic person, whether Out Back or Right In. The
only implements required are —(1) a bathing cos-
tume and (2) a few old rakes, which may be com-
mandered from the nearest Mongol soil tiller.
Behold, then, “Pannikin” and his fidus Achates
Gann, settled in their new home. Things looked
bright ahead. Away beneath the limpid waters
stretched our estate, alive with the luscious fodder
which was destined, as “Pannikin” put it, to bring
“profit to the primary producer and ptomaine-poison-
iiig to Peer and Pug promisculously.” It seemed as
though we were in a fair way to become Bivalve
Emperors. Did we attempt to live like sheep-men?
Did wfe even build ourselves such a dwelling as might
satisfy a cattle king? No; we built nothing. On
the whole of that vast property not so much as a
lean-to shed was to be seen. Our homestead was the
local pub. It was also our club, our theatre, our
banquetting hall and our salon. The back yard was
our stadium; the verandah our debating hall. How,
after several halcyon weeks, I discovered that “Pan-
nikin” was privily hypothecating the stock and in-
vesting the proceeds in beer; how he simultaneously
found that I had mortgaged our land, or, rather,
mud, in seven different places for a similar purpose;
how our genial host essayed to foreclose on the run
in a last desperate effort to connect with our board-
money ; how he was frustrated by a horde of credi-
tors, whose claims, if not wholly sound, were, at
least, less shadowy than his; above all, how James
and I fled the scene in disorder, are matters I pro-
pose to handle at some future date. My present ob-
ject is solely to insist that, as regards stern,
Diogenes-like simplicity of life, even the cattle-raiser
must give place to the hardy pioneer who dares to
blaze the track in the oyster-raising line. I have
been there and I know.

“Manuka” : I wish to get my stroke in, anil
disagree with “Kowhai” (B. 5/l/’ll) and “Uncle
Jim” (B. 9/2/’ll) on the toiling ability of Jack
Maori. Years of experience of him satisfies me that
he is a good worker. He excels as a bushman; and
he provides the champion shearers of Hawkes Bay and
the boss timber cutters of North Auckland. But
while I disagree with “Uncle Jim” about the men,
I extend the glad hand to him in regard to the
chastity of the women. Any Aboliar who wishes to
disagree with us, is welcome to free board and lodg-
ing in my camp for a month to get a first-hand view
of things. I think he would be eating his lie for
breakfast on the 29tli day.
“FROG WINE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When saddled are the trusty nags,
An’ squared the ration bill,
Good-bye said sadly to the booze,
To Fred, Jack, Ned an’ Bill,
With ’ead grog-thick an’ burstin’ ’ot,
With breath ter kill a dog,
Ye camp beside a stinkin’ ’ole
An’ drink the wine called “frog.”
Good things must ’ave an end, fer now
Ye’ve done a big cheque in
Ye swelter in the lonely bush
An’ scratch like ’ell fer tin ;
Gone square-faced gin an’ o.p. rum
Which set the town agog,
But on a stagnant billabong
The booze is wine called “frog.”
It’s blazin’ ’ot an’ damper time,
Since dawn ye’ve cursed an toiled.
Ye guess ye need a drop o’ tea,
The billy must be boiled;
Ye push the reeds an’ grass aside
An’ dip beneath a log—
Green slime, mud, insects, rancid froth;
This is the wine called “frog.”
Ye’ve grafted like a bullock team
With shovel, dish an’ pick. ...
Great Scott, ye’ve struck a patch at last;
No more ye’ll ask fer tick!
Ye load yer crocks with bags o’ tin
An’ slowly pub ward jog;
Ye’ve got a sandy-desert thirst,
But scorn the wine called “frog.”
The frogs may croak,
The drought is broke,
Ye’ll give the bush the sack,
Fer now ye pine,
Fer drink divine,
An’ leave the last dry track;
The door’s ajar,
Ye reach the bar,
An’ order up the grog;
Ye’ll sip straight rum
Till Kingdom come,
But no more wine called “frog.”
VICTORIA.

Trade Mark Case.
A WARNING.
It having come under our notice that various
substitutes are sometimes sold and passed off
as 0.T., notice is hereby given that this practice
is a breach of the Trade Marks Act, and con-
sequently illegal, as O.T. is a Registered Trade
Name, and applies only to the beverage made by
this Company.
After this date all persons who so continue to
pass off other drinks as O.T. will render them-
selves liable to proceedings under the “Common-
wealth Trades Marks Act.”
March 1. 11)11.
O.T. LIMITED,
F. Malvin Quinn,

ITEMS
BOWEN, SPEAKER OF MAOIR-
LAND'S LEGISLATIVE COUNCIL.
“10-2-4,” on the latest prodigy :
Re Jacky Baldwin, of Mount Colah (N.S.W.), who was
lost in the bush the other day. I have been waiting 1 ex-
pectantly for a psean from “ Jimmie Pannikin,” “ A
William-Cann ” or one of the other congenital Abo-liars
who whoop about the endurance powers of our black
brothers. In their silence, I beg to supply a brief
panegyric myself. Here was a three-year-old white
and carefully-nurtured baby who went for 72 hours
quite without food. At an early stage he jetti-
soned his hat—and it was well-nigh hot enough, on one
of those days, to have grilled a chop by sunlight. During
most of one night rain poured. After walking 10 miles,
the astounding infant was discovered, doggedly keeping
himself alive by drinking from a bush puddle. Though
weak in the legs he was right enough in the head, and
within three hours his swollen tongue subsided, and he
was able to give a coherent account of his adventures.
How many aboriginal babies would have survived? For
my part, I am satisfied that this Baldwin infant is Arthur
Adams’s only and original Kid in masquerade. I have
never met Adams’s notorious offspring, but find it im-
possible to believe any other on earth can be so wonder-
ful.
Hans Irvine, M.H.R. for Grampians
(Vic.), who is stumping his electorate against the
Referenda proposals, threatens to sell his property
at half what it cost him if the people say “Yes” on
April 26, and get out of this accursed country.
The awful threat—which sounds something like a
draper’s bargain-sale advt. —sets one thinking
over what the accursed country has done for
Irvine. He started his career with F. W. Niven,
of Ballarat, where he learnt his trade as a litho-
printer. After serving his time he spent a few
years on a station down Bombala way ; in those
days he was a reckless young man who would ride
anything with a tail on it. Later he returned to
Ballarat, and his father bought for a few hundred
pounds a partnership for him in Niven’s business,
which then became F. W. Niven and Co. Irvine
travelled for the firm, and amongst his customers
was the late Joseph Best, of Great Western, to
whom he sold a few wine-labels occasionally.
Incidentally he realised what a fine property
Best’s vineyard was ; so he sold out of Niven’s
for more thousands than he had paid hundreds
to go in, bought the vine-leaf property, and '
settled under his own fig tree. The wine j
business boomed, and so did Irvine. Then 1
he became a mining magnate (as a side ' -,
line), and, later on, M.H.R. (also as a side |
line). And there’s scarcely a thing in the world 1
that he knows less about than his last side line.
Died the other day at North Shore, Syd-
ney, aged 79. J. W. Brooks, who was in Ballarat
when the Big Thing happened. In those far
back days he was distinguished as an amateur
actor; and. after Eureka, he took part in a per-
formance got up for the benefit of Peter Lalor.
The beneficiary brought the one arm which the
unpleasantness had left him, and returned thanks
to a crowded house.
Fleetwood Fuller, the new Governor of
Victoria, is a nephew of ex-Chancellor of the
Exchequer Hicks Beach (Lord St. Aldwyn). His
father was a devotee of coaching, hunting and
cricket until he grew too old for such vigorous
amusements. Both father and son are Christ
Church (Oxford) graduates. The father was 10
years in the Commons, and the son had been in
the same Chamber just over the same period when
he was offered the rubber-stamp billet in Aus-
tralia.
Captain William Paxton, who for years
was Port Phillip’s best-known “mud pilot,” has
finished his contract. He took to the sea early,
serving his apprenticeship aboard an East India-
man in the roving ’4o’s; but turned his back on
the British flag soon after to sail beneath the
Chilian, Spanish and United States colors in turn.
After a varied career, he settled down in Mel-
bourne, and for 30 years poked up and down the
Yarra, piloting ships of all nations. He retired
into private life some years back, and was 85
when he was beached on the Golden Shore.
Died at Mackay (Q.) the other day, Daniel
Connor —without the O’ —one of the earliest of
Central Bananaland’s pioneers. He made north
in 1857, took up Princhester station, and had a
hand in much other pioneering. He was the
second to ship wool from Rockhampton, then no
town to speak of; the Archers were the first.
Later on he welcomed A. C. Gregory when that
explorer poked in to make inquiries about the lost
or strayed Leichhardt. His own name goes down
to posterity and the day after fixed to a mountain
range and a fair-sized northern river.
“ Wilhelm,” from the Inside :
The Timks (London) was recently sold to “The Times
Publishing Company.” There is nothing in the file at
Somerset House concerning “The Times Publishing Co.,
Ltd.,” to show who are the real owners of the paper.
The shares are held by nominees, and none of the actual
proprietors are directors. As a fact, the owners of the
Times are the Harmsworths, one Kennedy Jones, and Sir
John Ellerman, of the Ellerman line of steamers. Eller-
man has the biggest number of shares. His father was
Johann Herman Ellerman, of Berlin. Kennedy Jones has
had a queer career. He started life as private secretary
to T. P. O’Connor, M.P. At the age of 46 he is capable
of buying and selling his old employer several times over,
being a director of Associated Newspapers, Ltd., and a
millionaire.
“Gwy”: Longden, the Australian engineer,
whose death in abject poverty in N’York was
mentioned in last week’s Bulletin, starved him-
self for the sake of Science, it seems. He must
have made money after leaving Australia, because
not many years ago he was worth £20,000. He
spent it all on an electrical invention, from which
he expected fame and fortune. At the last it
became with him a despairing struggle and possi-
bly a monomania. Finally, while his apparatus
was lying in a safe deposit vault, he was dying in
a garret. It is a cruel world for inventors—and
the inventor’s cruellest tyrant is usually the in-
ventor himself.
Major C. A. Wilson, for whom the Last
Post sounded a week ago, was born at Penrith
(N.S.W.) in 1838, and began to battle for the
necessary biscuit in a Sydney shipping office. He
was a pioneer member of the First Regiment of
N.S.W. Rifles (the chrysalis of the First Aus-
tralian Infantry Regiment), which was formed in
1854 ; and in 1860, when the corps was re-organ-
ised into the First Battalion Volunteer Rifles, he
got an ensign’s commission, became a captain in
the early ’7o’s, and retired a major in 1895. Inci-
dentally he was one of the first choristers of St.
Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney, and was something
uncommonly large and whiskery in the Druids’
lodge.
Cann, M.H.R., is a brother of “Jack”
Cann, Speaker of the N.S.W. Assembly. He is
comparatively a recent arrival in Australia, and
was on strike from the Illawarra coal mines when
the last Federal general election took place.
Thinking, probably, that an election contest—
and apparently a hopeless one—was as good a
way of putting in time as any, he mounted a
bicycle and took the field. He worked his way
from town to town, and in each place the Labor
men fra,nked him on to the next. He defeated
the Fusionist, Bowden, by a large majority, to the
great surprise of everyone—including himself.
He is no orator, as Joe Cook is and Brutus was ;
but “a plain, blunt man,’’who looks after the
interests of his constituents, and takes the big
Australia side against the little Australians.

L. L. Bell, Inspector of Natives in Papua,
who accompanied Staniforth Smith on the expe-
dition that had a lot of luck not to be simply a
funeral, is a son of George Bell, of Melbourne
Argus. He was formerly shipping-clerk in
Mellwraith, McEacharn and Co.’s, and is one of
those adaptable, gritty Australians who will make
niches for themselves anywhere with a fair chance.

Hazleton, the Irish-party delegates who are about
to travel through the Commonwealth and Maori-
land, hunting the oof-bird for the Home Rule
pot, will start at Wellington (M.L.). The trio is
expected to reach there by the direct liner
Rotorua on May 1, and will conduct 12
meetings before tapping Patrick O'Stralia in the
old place.
Dr. J. A. Beattie, who for quarter of a
century has soothed the way of the unfortunates
suffering from incurable cancer in Liverpool
Asylum (N.S.W.), takes a well-earned holiday,
and departs shortly for Europe, where he will
combine personal pleasure with the general good
—the latter mainly by a study of the latest dis-
coveries in regard to inoperative cancer, the most
dreaded phase of the dreadful disease.
A Maoriland family, the Harringtons,
drops in for a nice dry bit by the death of one
Patrick McGuire, of N’Yark. Patrick left
Galway some 50 years ago, got work on the rail-
ways, seized his opportunities by their forelocks,
and led them into his paddock to the alleged
amount of $7,000,000. John Harrington, of Den-
niston ; Barclay Harrington, of Hawera; and
Mrs. A. M. Gow, lately of a Wellington hotel,
but now of the Royal Hyde Park Hotel, Sydney,
cut most of the little lot up among them.
Alfred Tennyson Dickens, the rather un-
poetic-looking son of a famous father, is being
accepted in Britain as something of a national
hero. Since he arrived from Australia under the
clever business direction of one of the Taits, he
has been requested to dine at the expense of
numerous corporations, and in almost every pro-
vincial town he visited to lecture on his pa he has
been feted, receptioned or addressed by the mayor
and councillors. Now he has been invited to the
United States, and has been expressly “com-
manded ”to eat with President Taft. And this
is the Alf Dickens who was wont to complain that
a famous father was a heavy handicap for a man
of only second-rate ability 1

The casual Australian has been resting of
late, but one of her dropped into publicity re-
cently at Lang Lang (Vic.). A woman left the
Melbourne-to-Warragul train at that station for
some refreshment, and casually took her seat
again, leaving her baby in the waiting-room. She
got three or four stations away before she missed
anything. Possibly a new hat had captured her
attention.
Maoriland has just lost a link with the
past by the death, at 84, of Mrs. Sarah Jane
Williams, of Wellington. Her mother was a
sister of Kendall, the missionary to the Maoris,
who landed in 1814. Her father, Thomas Flor-
ence, surveyed the greater part of Auckland town
and once owned most of the present enormously-
valuable Queen-street, which he swapped with the
old Provincial Government for 30,000 acres of poor
country land.

“ THE MUSICIAN."
J. S. Watkins’ a portrait of Josef Kretsehmann, of
Sydney, just bought by the Sydney Art Gallery.
In N.S.W. Art Society’s current exlr-
bition is shown a portrait of Josef Kretsehmann,
painted by J. S. Watkins and entitled “The
Musician.” The canvas, which has been bought
by the trustees of the N.S.W. National Gallery,
might well have been labelled “Father Christ-
mas,” for the kindly Professor, adored by
children, beloved of his confreres, and ever ready
to give his last away, is Sydney’s Father Christ-
mas. wholly in appearance and largely in fact.
He belongs to a type of enthusiasts essentially
German, and in his old age—he verges on 75 —
works from morn to sunset all the year round
teaching music. Among his former pupils are
numbered not only many eminent amateurs but
several who have attained much distinction on
the concert platform. “ Ach ! ” exclaims this
unworldly veteran, “Vat beautiful schildren and
vat lofely minds you find among der poor ! How
aggreeable it is to teach dot leetle girl und her
brother 1 They schildren of poor storeman—he
keeps eight schildren on two-pound-five efery
week. Und dot leetle poy rnit der bright eyes—
his fader dead und his mutter work very hard to
keep her family.” And the old Professor looks
the part to the life; he is Father Christmas all
the year round.
Queensland is just now in the curious
position of having as sole Commissioner of Rail-
ways a man who didn’t have anything at all to do
with railways until he was past 65 years old.
Thallon was getting ancient, and the Bananaland
railways were growing bigger ; so the Government
a while ago appointed Auditor-General King to
be Assistant-Commissioner, the idea being that
this ex-Customs officer should look after the coni-
mercialsideof theshow. Then, within a few months,
Thallon died, leaving the new-ehum Kingthesole
Commissioner. Thallon had things pretty well
organised, however ; so for a couple of months,
while the Government is considering the situa-
tion. nothing dreadful is likely to happen—as the
result of Thallon’s departure, anyhow.
H. M. THE HOUSEHOLDER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As a householder, the King personally interested him
self on Sunday night in seeing that an accurate census
return of persons at Buckingham Palace was compiled.—
Argus (4/4/77).
Upon the back-door steps he stands,
An empty milk-jug in his hands,
And scans the stars, and breathes “ More rain ! ”
And mildly goes inside again,
And gently locks the kitchen-door,
And searches then for burglars ; for
A model householder is he . ..
That is the sort of King for me !
When burglar-drill is done he goes
To sneak a snack on stockinged toes ;
And then, remembering Tortoise-shell,
He seeks the parlor couch, for well
He knows fat Torty’s little ways.
Some kings and princes never laze,
Of household duties never tire.
That kind of King I do admire!
Within the cupboard soon he gropes
(He knows the right housekeeping ropes),
And rakes out something nice to eat,
The remnants of a favored sweet,
Cold meat, a slice of fat and lean,
A cup of coffee for his queen,
And—yes, three fingers of the best
For him, the King who's earned a rest!
Hail him, the King whose Torty-cat
Exits into the garden at
Ten-thirty sharp ! Hail him, the one
Who goes to bunk, his duty done,
Before the chimes of midnight sound !
A King whom household cares surround
I can applaud like anything !
Hail him, our own Householder-King !
VICTORIA.

COLUMN
For the first nine months of 1909-10 the
commonwealth net Customs revenue was £8,738 143 •
for the past nine months it was £9,856,463, an in-
crease of £1,118,320, which is about 10 times
greater than the anticipated growth. The con-
tinued great prosperity of the country and the ex-
ceeding plentifulness of money is no doubt largely
responsible for the big increase in imports, with the
consequent big increase in import duties. In many
• cases the cost of production in Australia has in-
creased very much, and, as the duty has not gone
up in proportion, the foreigner is able to undersell
the Australian producer. This shows over again the
need to put the tariff and wages boards, etc., under
the one authority. If the duty races ahead of the
rates of pay fixed by the wages boards, the manu-
facturer gets an unfair advantage over the work-
thf, wages rates, etc., race ahead of the
tariff then the workman gets an unfair advantage
over the manufacturer, and presently the orders go
°J tl , le c ? untl- y- The tariff is, and must be, in
the hands of the Commonwealth; and the purpose
of the coming Industrial Referendum is to put the
regulation of wages in the same hands.
. s - Wales on the nine months is £328,307 ahead
of the first three quarters of the previous year—
£10,549,101, as against £10,220,794. The rail-
ways continue to have a booming revenue; they are
£521,357 ahead, and tramways are £125,152 ahead,
together more than wiping out the shortage in the
subsidy which the Commonwealth pays the State.
When McGowen came into office, after the October
elections, he found that the Estimates had been
tramed (as a matter of fact, more than a fourth of
the year had passed), and he practically took them
over. They provided for a shrinkage of £1,200,000
in the revenue, due, of course, to the reduction in
the subsidy paid to the State by the Commonwealth.
As things are shaping there won’t be half that
shrinkage; so, if the expenditure had not been
bumped up, McGowen might have had a surplus of
perhaps three-quarters of a million at the end of the
year. Things stand this way at present:—
9 mos. 1909-10. 9 mos. 1910-11.
Revenue.. .. £10,220,794 £10,549,101
Expenditure .. 9,976,046 10,665,348
Surplus .. £244,748 Deficit £116^247
It is plain from this that the McGowen Government
is pouring out money fast. But as long as it
doesn t show a deficit it is all right. A tricky
financial crowd would defer expenditure, and thereby
manufacture a surplus, which it would wear as a
sort ot halo; after which it would proceed to spend
the money. In plain words, the manufacture of
surpluses is mostly a trick, and the McGowen Gov-
ernment hasn’t learned it. Unfortunately it has
learned the other trick of running to the pawnshop.
It has so far spent £2,842,164 of Cohen’s money
this year, plus £294,581 on account of railway
# sa’® 0r a total of £3,136,745 —an increase
ot it. 473,703 on the figures for the corresponding
quarters of the previous year. Inevitablv there
will be trouble by-and-bye. A handful of people
cant pour out £3,136,745 of loan money in nine
months without producing a boom, a burst of artifi-
cial prosperity; and when the borrowing stops, and
some tens of thousands of men are thrown out of
work, there is bound to be trouble.
The Victorian revenue shows an increase of
£52/,278; the total reaches £6,618,51'5. Railways
show an increase of £269,462. Expenditure re-
turns are, as usual, not forthcoming. S. Australia
shows the remarkable proportionate increase of
£321,532 ; total for nine months, £3,065,076.
The swift growth of S. Australia’s revenue is, how-
ever, partly due to land sales; and land-sale money
isn’t really revenue at all. For years there was
hardly any income from this source; with the open-
ing up of new lands, once supposed to be desert, the
total for the three quarters ran up to £193,265, as
against £95,978 for the corresponding fragment of
last year. The one fly in S. Australia’s butter is
the weak wheat market. Parcels were sold down
to 3s. the other day—and that means Is. less than
the farmers lately lined their pockets with. Some
at least of this shrinkage is due to the large in-
crease in ocean freights. Some time ago S. Aus-
tralia inquired into the local wheat-buying arrange-
ments. With more justification it might now in-
quire whether there is a shipping ring—whose
existence may indeed be taken for granted -and
what its effect has been on freights, and, conse-
quently, on the prices that shippers can afford to
pay this end.
Queensland for the nine months shows up this
way:—
1909-10. 1910-11.
Revenue .. £3,530,217 £3,866,839
Expenditure .. 3,168,773 3,275.453
Loans .. .. 1,140,712 1,455,219
The bricks heaved at N. S. Wales for its borrowing
habits can be taken as picked up again, and heaved
some more at Queensland. Westralia has had a
revenue of £2,808,901, and an expenditure of
£2,626,839; so that nominally it has a surplus of
£182,062. But when a State charges up meals pto-
vided for immigrants to loan account, as Westralia
was doing lately, and is doing still for all this
paper knows, the surplus is plainly bogus.

Depositors’ balances in this purely State Bank have
moved as follows:
1901 ..£6,647,289 1906 ..£9,322,923
1902 .. 7,100,108 1907 .. 11,128,495
1903 .. .7,018,425 1908 .. 12,118,772
1904 .. 7,952,885 1909 .. 13,303,421
1905 .. 8,883,651 1910 .. 15.190,820
The increase during the past few years has been very
remarkable, and proves the wonderful prosperity of
N. S. Wales. And in that connection it has to be
remembered, too, that there is another vigorous
Savings Bank in existence. The immigration agent
couldn’t be provided with a stronger magnet than
these very extraordinary figures. Still, the very
rapid growth of deposits may have been partly due,
curiously enough, to the increased facilities offered
for the withdrawal of money. The old objection
was that, if a small saver wanted a small amount
quickly, and especially if he was away from home,
he couldn’t get it; and in the larger centres money-
lending middlemen made a fine thing out of giving
the would-be withdrawer ready cash. Nowadays,
however, the _ depositor can not only withdraw on
demand at his own branch any amount up to his
total credit, but he may on satisfactory identifica-
tion, withdraw £2 in any one day at any other
branch or post-office agency in the State. Moreover,
ls now an arrangement for reciprocity between
the Government Savings Banks of all the States. A
system of transfer has also been established between
this bank and the United Kingdom. All these things
have no doubt added greatly and deservedly to the
popularity of the bank.
The bank has no capital in the ordinary way apart
from the £93,834 of reserves; but it has the entire
resources of the State behind it. It is, therefore,
absolutely the strongest institution in Austral-
asia. Of the deposits and other available moneys,
£10,961,739 has been lent to the State of N.S.W.
on the security of funded stock and Treasury bills,
and another £1,441,707 is on call at the Treasury.
Four-fifths of the funds being in these gilt-edged
Government securities, the earnings are naturally
comparatively small. On the mean funds —which, of
course, include till money—they averaged £3 9s.
p.c.; interest to depositors absorbed a shade over
£2 15s. 3d. and expenses a shade over 9s. sid.,
leaving 4s. 3d. as profit, to provide for contingen-
cies. The unsatisfactory feature is the very small
amount of the savings which is being used to finance
other strugglers—-or perhaps the same strugglers.
There certainly is an Advances Department, a crea-
tion of the past two or three years, but only
£798,050 of the Savings Bank money is being em-
ployed in that business. Probably, especially in the
earlier days of the Advances Department, there was
a good deal of red-tape about the administration,
and men who might have been customers of the State
bank had the money they were in need of pushed
into their hands by private banks. This paper isn’t
going to heave even a clod at the private banks of
Australia, which are, on the whole, institutions that
the country can be proud of; but, at the same time,
their object in life is to make dividends for their
shareholders. This State Bank has no shareholders
to make dividends for ; and the one object of the Ad-
vances Department is to forward the interests of
the borrowers, while, of course, safeguarding the prin-
-01 jf. a cus t° m er has borrowed for 30 years
and has undertaken to pay in instalments spread over
that period, and if he then has the luck to strike a
good season or two, the bank will allow him to re-
duce the debt straight away—which is more than the
private mortgagee will do if the security is good and
improving. He wants the debt reduced when the
borrower looks shakier ; when the latter is firmer on
his feet the mortgage is all the better property, and
therefore, to be hung on to. And in other respects
the Advances Department shows that its job in life
is to fatten the borrower and not the lender.
The Advances Department was started on the debris
of the business of the old Advances to Settlers’ Board,
which wasn’t run on banking lines, and was looked
on as a good deal of a charity. (It operated mostly
in the days of drought, distress, and seed wheat
‘ advances,” and its clients were those who weren’t
in a position to borrow money from anybody else.)
Nominally, the old Advances to Settlers’ Board had
made profits; but these and much more have been
absorbed by bad debts. This shows how the whole
business has fared:—
Nominal
Reserves.

During the past year a further £634 was written
off on account of losses made on the old Settlers’
Board advances; on the other hand, and by way of
a pleasant change, £1292 was recovered on account
of old advances written off. When the bank took
over the Settlers’ Board business the advances
totalled £401,741; the total has been reduced to
£135,317; and the amount of interest and instal-
ments in arrear on December 31 was only £2276.
The present bank is, therefpre, winding up the old
business with infinitely more success than at one
time seemed at all probable!. And at the same time
it is building up, though slowly, a new business on
a much improved basis.

People are beginning to find out that Australia can
do a thing or two besides grow wheat and watch
sheep grow wool. The importer had it all his own
way in Sydney for very many years; but he will get
it less and less his way the more the manufacturing
interests grow. This co. is among those that will
profit. At one time it had to be content with odd
jobs, mostly of cleaning and scraping, done for the
cargo-carriers; now it begins to do much more im-
portant engineering jobs, and the outlook is vastly
improved. No doubt it is on the latter account
that, lately, pref. shareholders have been asking to
have their shares converted into ordinaries. They
evidently reckon that the prospects are good enough
to make the preference unnecessary; and they want
to join in any larger dividend that may be going.
(They at present stop at 6 p.c.) Pref. shares will,
therefore, presently disappear, and all will rank
alike. The management writes off something against
depreciation, but the amount isn’t mentioned. Be-
sides its share capital it has £148,300 of debenture
money—and that, of course, means a first bite out
of revenue whether things are good or bad. Very
much depends upon the amount of revenue written
off against depreciation. The principal balance-
sheet items have changed in 10 years as follows:
1900. 1910.
Property, plant and stocks.. ..£327,758 £373,653
Cash and other assets .. .. 85,823 55,478
Total assets £413,581 £429,131
Less liabilities 175,660 169,012
Net assets £237,921 £260,119
Nominally, therefore, £22,198 has been added to
the assets in 10 years, so that the surplus over the
dividends has averaged £2219 a year, which is
nothing at all tremendous for a co. with a capital
of £200,000 and £170,000 of other people’s money
to safeguard. But, as remarked before, it may be
that the management has been paying for a good
many things out of revenue, and writing the cost
off against depreciation; though, on the other hand,,
it may not have been writing off enough. It is a
case of trusting the management entirely.

In addition to the usual dividend credited to share-
holders, a bonus of 2s. 6d. per share is this year
declared payable on the maturity of the shares. As
usual in such cases, the shares are £5O when they
reach maturity; and then, as usual, the capital is
withdrawable. For 3s. lOd. paid per month for 15
years (=:£34 10s.) a subscriber gets £5O; or, for
£l7 18s. lid. paid now, £5O is forthcoming in 21
years. This is guaranteed. Any surplus after pay-
ing interest to depositors and other creditors, paying
Working expenses, and providing for bad and doubt-
ful debts, goes to the credit of Profit and Loss ; and
when the surplus is regarded as more than sufficient
to guarantee the fixed interest, a bonus is allotted,
as has been done for the past year. The bonus on
this occasion amounts to £432. There is no capital
to guarantee the promised interest; the only guar-
antee fund is provided by the reserves. Hence the
wisdom of keeping them large. Moreover, they have
to do other work. The total capital (all withdraw-
able by degrees) was £47,377 on December 31. The
deposits totalled £62,645. These deposits are in-
vested in the business—that is to say, in shrinkable
securities; they are practically all advances on
building-society terms. Whether they shrink or not
the society has to pay the depositors 20s. in the £,
and the reserves have to do most of the guaranteeing
of that also. However, the risks are not large. Pro-
perty in and about Launceston is not subject to
violent fluctuations; and a moderate fluctuation
shouldn’t do much damage to a building society
which gets safer and safer with every fortnightly or
monthly payment made by a borrower. The greatest
danger is when there is a sudden boom demand for a
large sum ; if a slump happens then, the society is
liable to be left with a large block of depreciated
assets. The Equitable isn’t troubled with these
sudden demands. Things, on the contrary, move in
a very leisurely way:—
„ . , 1901. 1907. 1908. 1909. 1910.
Capital ..£50,883 £44,404 £44,490 £47,794 £47,377
Deposits .. 83,677 59,044 58,777 58,844 62 645
Mortgages .. 140,453 111,975 109,332 112,540 114,’582
Expenses of management last year were £1293, or
about £1 2s. 6d. p.c. of advances. It isn’t much
use trying to compare this with other figures, be-
cause different societies charge different items to
management; some, for instance, have large repairs
to pay for, and some deduct rates, etc., from income,
while others show their income gross, and add taxes
to expenses of management. The figure in this case
appears to be exceedingly moderate, and the manage-
rnent generally prudent; while, if no big return is
offered, a shade over 5 p.c. is available with very
little risk indeed.

and Loss, and written off, to cover general depre-

money is no doubt spent in renewals and possibly in
extensions. A certain amount of profit can legiti-
mately be spent in this way in extensions; it is
really a set-off against the depreciated value of the
rest of the undertaking. Great progress has been

The liabilities consist chiefly of 4 p.c. debentures
payable in 1915; and, though it is very cheap
money, the directors are evidently saving up to get
out of pawn.

Profits are shown after “providing for wear and tear
and depreciation” ; but the amount of the provision
is unspecified. “Mining property, including mineral
lands, buildings, railway, rolling-stock, pits, tun-
nels, etc.,” stood on June 30 at £100,642. The
corresponding item on December 31 was £97,986.
That looks as though £ 2656 has been written off
out of profits. But it may have been either more or
less. It may have been more because some addi-
tional plant may have been bought during the half-
year ; and it may have been less, because some may
have been sold. The former is much the more pro-
bable. A comparison of the principal figures now
and eight years ago shows up as follows: —
1902. 1910.
Liabilities £1,473 £13,810
Mining and fixed property 83,901 97,986
Liquid assets .. . 91,567 52,047
From that it would look as though the co. hadn’t
got any forrarder in the eight years, inasmuch as
liquid assets have decreased while liabilities have
increased. Blit there is no telling how much has
been put into fixed assets. The liquid assets —prac-
tically all cash and book debts (£21,419) —are
still very substantial, and reach almost to the total
of the capital (£100,000). Apart from these few
things, nothing of any consequence is to be got out
of the published accounts. More is to be got out
of a survey of the past. For very many years the
dividends have swung between 35 and 10 p.c.,
and for the past 10 years have averaged about 17
p.c. The co. is not in the coal vend, though it is
certain that the threatened bursting of the vend
would provoke a bitter competition, which wouldn’t
do this co. any good. Besides its mine, it owns
very extensive surface properties and a tramway,
and it enjoys a large revenue from both of these
sources.

The original goodwill was £57,500; the total capi-
tal was £60,000. The co. thus started 22 years ago
with £57,500 of paper and £2500 of substantial
assets. As shown above, it has now £33,147 of sub-
stantial assets and £26,853 of paper. Moreover, no
less than £15,136, or almost one-half of the sub-
stantial assets (chiefly deposits), has been added in
the past five years. The great activity in the city
and suburban property market has, of course, helped
the co. enormously; but even apart from that, the
larger the snowball of substantial assets grows, the
faster it grows. The interest on the present accumu-
lation means about £1250 a year; and, even if no
other additions were made to reserves out of profits,
that sum put by yearly, and invested with interest
accretions at 34 p.e., would prodi co the required
£26,000 odd in 15 years. When that happens,
the co. will, of course, have 20s. in the £ of cash or
its equivalent, as well as the business.

The Freehold Assets Co. (Melbourne), which
in 1907 succeeded the Freehold Assets Realisation
Co., whose job was to act as undertaker to the Free-
hold Investment and Banking Co.: —
Reserves.
Taken over from old c 0... .. £14,119
To Re-
Profits. Dividends. serves.
Jan., 1908.. £1,484 Nil £1484 15,603
Written off reserves, £2957 .. . 12,646
Dec., 1908.. 6,540 7 p.c. = £5197 1343 13,989
Dec., 1909.. 7,284 8 „ ~ 5939 1345 15,334
Dec., 1910.. 11,327 10$,, ~ 7795 3532 18,866
The few remarks at the top of the table sufficiently
explain the history and occupation of the eo. The
Freehold Investment and Banking Co. had a short
life and a wild one. It was born in the debauch of
1892, and soon accumulated liabilities of about '
£1,300,000. When the smash came an arrangement
had to be made with the creditors, who were just
about as much to blame as the shareholders, and
new deposit receipts were issued. These were the
“A” series, A “B” series followed, representing
unpaid interest. After the mess had been* cleaned
up to some extent, it was decided to finish the job
on a sudden and wholesale scale. The old share-
holders were dropped out of the calculation, for the
remaining assets were only equal (according to the
valuation) to the paying of 7s. 7d. in the £ of the
remaining liabilities. Still, even that was better
than had' sometimes been expected, seeing that some
creditors had sold their deposit receipts to the under-
taker for as little as ss. in the £. As some of the
assets were not expected to be very productive, even
on the 7s. 7d.-in-the- £ basis, and as, moreover, it
was intended to get rid of them as fast as possible,
the capital of the present co. was fixed at £74,245,
equal to 3s. in the £ ; 4s. was represented by de-
bentures, issued to the old creditors; the other 7d.
was treated as the nucleus of a reserve fund. The
co. has had a very good time, on the whole; and,
besides paying a good dividend and adding materially
to the reserve, has accumulated a contingent re-
serve of £14,355. This, branded as a realisation
suspense account, is the profit expected to be made
on transactions not yet completed. At the end of
1908 the co. had in hand properties of the book-
value of £76,438. This has, in the intervening two
years, been reduced to £66,133. The more or less
liquid assets showed up as follows at December 31,
1909 and 1910 respectively:—
Cash and loans at short call.. .. £29,328 £28,825
Debentures, bank stock and deposit
receipts 24,444 36,755
Advances (balances, etc.) .. .. 55,089 51,109
Shares in other cos 11,721 13,344
Trifles 560 118
Total .. £121,142 £130,151
The liabilities, chiefly debentures, total £83,991;
so the surplus of more or less liquid assets is
-£46,160; and, besides that, the co. has £66,133
of unsold property, mostly freehold. Whether that
will fetch 20s. in the £ this paper doesn’t know.
Evidently what has been sold so far has averaged a
good deal more than that, the balances on completed
realisations during the past three years having been:
—l9OB, £2872 ; 1909, £1512; 1910, £5963. Rents,
interests and dividends last year totalled £17,662,
which was equal to 9 p.c. gross on the average of
the assets. Out of that interest and exes, have to
be paid, and amortisation provided to extinguish the
leasehold asset; but that the whole assets bring in 9
p.c. suggests that there certainly isn’t much over-
valuation. On the contrary, it looks as though some
at least of the property is worth more than the books
say it is. If so, it will mean more surpluses later
on.

A stone quarry is a wasting asset; but in this case
the waste is so small that it is said to be almost
imperceptible. Moreover, what there is of it is un-
derstood to be allowed for apart from the sums writ-
ten off for depreciation and shown above. In other
words —or so it was officially stated some time ago
—'wear and tear at the rate of 2d. per ton is in-
cluded in the cost of quarrying. Altogether, then,
it may be taken for granted that the profit shown,
after deducting the sums Written off, is true profit
—which it isn’t in the case of 99 mines out of a
hundred. The Co. has had a very good time these
last few years, mainly, no doubt, because of the
general prosperity, the rapid growth of Sydney arid
suburbs, the extension of roads, the increase of rateS
and the consequent patching of old roads, etc. In
the dull times some years ago things were very
sick with the Co., aiid £6BOO was written off capi-
tal as lost Or hot represented by assets. Recently,
as the table shows, this amourit was restored, and
about the same time another £6BOO was called.
The capital is now £40,800. In the past five
years this change has occurred with regard to more
or less liquid assets: —
1905. 1910.
Assets other than quarries and
fixed plant .. .. .. £3817 £8409
Liabilities.. 7814 464
Deficiency ' £3997 Surplus £7945
The position has thus been improved to the extent
of £ 11,942 in five years; but of that amount,
£6BOO is represented by new capital called up. The
business itself has paid the dividends, bought a new
property at Guildford ( £1441, book value), added
considerably to plant and machinery, increased the
quarry land and equipment at Prospect (over £4OOO
has been spent here), and put £5142 into the co.’s
stocking, or cigar-box, or whatever it keeps its loose
change in. The record is extremely satisfactory;
but this paper doesn’t know how long it will con-
tinue. The goods the co supplies can’t be regarded
as necessaries, like bread or beer; and in poor times
building almost ceases, and we put up with almost
any old thing in the form of a. road.
* * * *
“0.K.”: One of the commercial sins of the Mel-
bourne City Council is the Corporation Baths —a
business which represents an annual loss to the rate-
payers of £2OOO to £3OOO. The loss is not directly
shown in the council’s accounts, wherein the baths
are tangled up with the markets and other matters;
but the deficiency is an annual event, nevertheless.
Under certain circumstances, the fact that the city
was losing money on the business of washing its
citizens, wouldn’t be very terrible; there are some
necessary municipal jobs—road-making and smell-
chasing, for instance—which one doesn’t expect to
be directly payable. But in the case of the baths,
the loss is wholly, or almost wholly, caused by some
side-shows that are not wanted at all. Also, the
big monopolised building includes a Turkish bath,
heated by a wrong system of steam-pipes, and patro-
nised mainly by parties of stout Hebrew ladies, who
are attracted by the bargain sale system of manage-
ment. In Melbourne there are but two Turkish baths
nowadays; and tpe city bath, which was opened
whilst the larger establishment was being rebuilt,
has reduced its rate to six tickets for 10s. This re-
duction doesn’t benefit the citizens as a whole; it
benefits merely a few stout parties, who, in 99 cases
out of 100 (or in 999 cases out of 1000), are well
able to afford the old rate. Really the ratepayers
are paying for some of the Turkish baths which the
Rachels and other fat citizens and citizenesses take;
and, incidentally, they are no doubt making it very
uncomfortable for the principal Turkish bath boss,
who hasn’t got any ratepayers to subsidise him.

Taking spelter at £23 per ton —latest
middle price £23 5s. —-the Zinc Corporation at
Broken Hill figures the last three months’ work this
way:—
Jan. Feb. March.
Income .. .. £20,101 £27,292 £38,916
Working costs .. 11,733 12,861 14,866
£8,368 £14,431 £24,049
Cost of tailings .. 5,922 6,783 8,392
Net profit .. £2,446 £7,648 £15,657
Deduct £4OO a month for London exes., and the
‘ final estimated result is arrived at. Admittedly, the
£15,657 for March can’t be kept up ; the co. is now
changing over to the Metals Separation process, and
when that has been installed lower-grade stuff will
be handled. Still it is an amazing performance, see-
ing that it is all done on material which up to a
very few years ago was reckoned waste. And even
after the Zinc Corporation had started, success
seemed so uncertain only about three years ago that
a 20 p.c. preference had to be offered for the last lot
of money raised on shares.

Some of the Tolwong (N.S.W.) people
still hang on to hope. Armstrong is now to make a
thorough investigation of the whole business. If he
gives any encouragement, they will go ahead; if he
doesn’t, the thing will be allowed to blow itself out.
* * * *
The plot thickens at Bullfinch. When
Lionel Robinson’s best man, Frank Powell, wanted
to have a look at the big mine the other day, he was
told that it couldn’t be done; and now a report is
to hand that Fred Morgan’s objection to working the
property to suit the Board in London has reached
breaking strain. Just what the trouble is between
Morgan and the bosses, or why it was considered
advisable not to let Powell go below, no one seems
to know; but the market, which is generally a
pretty good guide as to how things are looking
underground, has lately been booted, from piUar to
post, and more kicks are said to* be in store.*
Dvason, Bonwick and Greenway, whom
the Westralian police couldn’t get within a mile oi
convicting of conspiracy over the Chaffinch business,
have each put in a claim for £lOOO as compensation.
* * *
Kalgurli (W.A.) lias decided to take a
null The strain of working the mine honestly and
finding ore of sufficient value to maintain present re-
turns, has begun to tell, and in order to avoid a
serious breakdown the grade is to be brought down
and the output reduced. In making this recom-
mendation to the London directors, the extra-careiul
manager, Black, says: “It is difficult to make any
definite forecast of probable returns, but the next
few months’ operations should indicate the working
grade of ore from the stopes and the fresh bodies of
ore opening up.” Beading between the lines, this
seems to indicate that values in the present working
levels are unreliable, and that the bottom of the
mine looks sick. #
At the Oroya Exploration meeting in
London lately, held with the object of inducing
shareholders to put up £lOO,OOO more cash to de-
velop five mines, because the market was not favor-
able for floating them at once, chairman H. C.
Hoover was in great form. The Youanme mine in
W.A. covered 4000 ft. along the proved ore-body. At
the 80ft. level (down to which a wild cat might fall
from the windlass without hurting its spine), the
reef was 1156 ft. long, sft. 6in. wide, and worth 50s.
a ton. This body for evfery 100 ft. in depth would
yield a profit of £60,000. At the 170 ft. level, the
shoot was 480 ft. long and worth 505., showing a
profit of £40,000. They wanted to open out at
300 ft., and if the ore body did not lose its character
(sounds like the lapse of a servant girl) the mine
was worth over £300,000! (Prolonged applause.)
The Meekatharra was another phenomenal property.
Above the 160 ft. level (the wild cat might crack his
spine or break his legs falling this far) there were
47,000 tons of ore, which would give a profit of
£42,000; and every 100 ft. of extension in depth
would mean another £30,000. If the 250 ft. level
were as good as the 160 ft., the profit above that
depth would be £72,000! (Applause.) Hoover
then transported his shareholders to the Babilonia
and Los Angeles mines in Mexico, where the revolu-
tion is coming from. The Babilonia was worth
£45,000, and the Los Angeles £20,000 per 100 ft.
of depth. Another wave of the hand, and the Oroya
illusionist took his spellbound hearers to the Maikop
oil-field in Russia, where — But, anyhow, the total
profit was £252,000 (on paper), and with an ex-
penditure of £25,000 another £120,000 could
easily be earned, or £372,000 in all. After all
this there was only one dissentient, a deaf man, and
the shareholders uproariously agreed to subscribe for
200,000 new shares at 10s., making the capital of
the Oroya Exploration Co. £250,000. Nobody
thought of asking what the directors meant to do
with the balance of ,1:75,000 of new capital. Pos-
sibly it will be deposited with the Birkbeck Bank.

Cross (W.A.) wire, to the effect that the Young
Bullfinch, Birthday Gift and Rowan’s Find had shut
down for want of funds. Several infuriated sup-
porters of these extremely doubtful properties in-
vaded the paper offices and cursed the sender of the
telegram for wiring such a cock-and-bull yarn about
concerns that had plenty of cash, and were still
spending it. Judging by the way developments are
shaping in the three properties, and the expenditure
that is being, incurred in their upkeep, it won’t be
long before the state of affairs reported by the of-
fending correspondent will come to pass. Already
several Bullfinch concerns have given up hope, and
are looking for other shows on which to Spend the
remnants of their capital.
R. B. Pettigrew has had his say with re-
gard to the row which the Westralian shareholders
have been making over the way in which the affairs
of the Pine Hill are being conducted. Pettigrew
swears that, if his advice had been followed by the
Board, everything would have been all right. He
also mentions the fact that the co. has still a bit of
cash in the bank, and that the trouble most to be
feared is want of water for crushing purposes. On
the other hand, there are some Job’s comforters,, who
say that the shoot has snuffed out, and that the
directors haven’t mustered up enough courage to
break the sad news to shareholders.
“McG.” : That doleful tale of the chairman
at Gwalia Consolidated (W.A.) meeting but bears out
writer’s remarks in The Bulletin in January or
February, 1908, regarding this Wiluna show’s re-
quirements. Any practical miner could thus early
—aye, two years before—-foresee the ultimate end
of the shortsighted policy of chopping out and
treating the shallow, though somewhat lengthy and
patchy, body of oxidised ore. Often enough at that
period monthly returns left profits; yet no attempt
was made to grapple with the antimonial sulphide
problem. To-day, minus cash or credit, and with
the curses of scrip-holders as an accompaniment,
they’re going to experiment and explore—or, per-
haps, they are just starting to think of doing so.
It’s part of the old, old tale in mining. Local
management may have desired correct working, but
a hungry London directorate desired a show of pro-
fits, and hang the future. And the future —together
with the chances of what looks like a legitimate
mining proposition—appears to be hanged fairly
well.
* * * *
Mt. Morgan is gradually improving its
smelting practice. Last month’s output was worth
£76,004, which makes the total for three months
£230,762. The feature of the return for March is
the larger quantity (6699 tons) of basic ore used
from Many Peaks. This increased proportion of
basic ore, containing only a few grains of gold per
ton, naturally reduces the average extraction; at
present it is 2.99 p.c. copper and 6.76dwt. gold.
As time goes on the average will fall still a little
further; but there should be a set-off in the shape
of much reduced costs. Meantime a few shares are
still dropped' on to the market, particularly in
London, “at best,” which seems to suggest that
there are a few bears about.
* * * *
After three months’ hard trying, Gt. Fitz-
roy (Q.) has put through 3679 tons of crude ore by
the flotation process, for a yield of 531 tons concen-
trates, containing 77 tons copper and 170 oz. gold.
Taking copper at £5O per ton and gold at £4 per
oz., the metals recovered are worth between 245.
and 255. per ton of crude ore; but even this low
value is subject to slight furnace losses. It is fairly
clear that 8., M. and Co.’s troubles are not over yet.
The flotation process is getting out less than 80 p.c.
of the copper and only 52 p.c. of the gold ; and the
stuff is only worth 2| p.c. copper in the first place.
Last month the whole output was valued at £11,464,
while the working expenses ran to £14,874.

At Hamnden, Cloncurry, they have com-
menced sintering, and the plant is running satisfac-
torily. The smelters will be ready to start almost
immediately. An order for converters has also been
placed with the Austral Otis Co. The capacity of
these will be about twice that of the Mount Elliott
P lant - ,
Corbould, of Mount Elliott, has gone
south. He has done some great hustling since he
was put in charge of Elliott. “Reminds me,” said
a miner, talking of him lately, “of one night I was
going home, and there was an old fellow with a dog
just ahead of me. The old chap was blithered, and
the dog was frisking around at a great rate. Coming
across an old bag, the dog tore round and round
with it. The inebriated one gazed at him in disgust.
Then he went up to him. ‘Paddy,’ he said, ‘you’re
too energetic for North Queensland. You’d suit
Corbould.’ ” # #
Corries, of Brisbane, have put up a for-
feitable £lOOO to pay for an option over the Blair
Athol coal property. The price is £165,000, of
which £65,000 is to be in cash; and the option-
holders must speak on or before June 30.

South Glanmire and Monklaad. Gympie,
crushed 560 tons for 2170 z.—a poor return that is
excused by the mullocky nature of some of the
stopes and the poverty of the Inglewood formation.
Prospecting continu. ■< for the Power reef, which re-
cently helped No. 2 S. Great Eastern to clean up
850 tons of loz. stone. S. Glanmire would feel
quite happy again if it could get that; but the best
things at Gympie generally live near a slide or cross-
course, and do not extend far.
* * * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, crushed
367 tons for £2173, an average of £5 18s. sd.
The last previous lot of 240 tons averaged £8 38. bd.
per ton; but variable results are to be expected
from a shew like the Mexican, with its erratic ore
shoots. . .
The Tasmania gold mine has got fS’fpO
from 4963 tons ore and large quantities of by-
products. At some mines this would be good ; but
the Tasmania is one of the few that treat both by
cyaniding and chlorination; and this elaborate
method helps to account for high costs. The deepest
levels show a considerable improvement, particularly
the crosscut at the 1250 ft. level, which, after pene-
trating 43ft., has exposed a lode 4ft. wide, worth
over loz. The 1370 ft. level has been driven 338 ft.,
with Oft. of stone assaying 17dwt. at the end. the
last 40ft. has shown consistently good prospects. A
rise from the deepest level (1370 ft.) is up 88ft., the
face showing 6ft. of stone, averaging 16dwt. If the
crushings can only be made to average 15dwt., or
70s. per ton on 4000 tons a month, there will be a
substantial profit.

THE AUSTRALASIAN WIRELESS CO., LIMITED.
The persistent, though somewhat monotonous,
regularity with which Australasian Wireless, Ltd.,
has secured the Commonwealth and M.L Govern-
ment business and shipping cos’, ship stations in
the face of the keenest interested opposition, nas
culminated in the Wireless float—the Australasian
Wireless Co., Ltd., £65,000. In other words, the
little preliminary co., after a short, but highly suc-
cessful career, blossoms into a bigger co. to carry
out the contracts secured, and conduct and develop
the message-traffic business arranged.
The £65,000 capital is divided into 50,000 £1
ordinaries and 15,000 6 p.c. cumulative prefs., ear-
marked towards purchase of patent rights. Of the
50,000 the public is offered 30,000 at os. on appli-
cation, and ss. on allotment, and ss. monthly, if
required. The remainder—2o,ooo ordinary shares
.—are allowed the vendor co. (the little Australasian
Wireless, Ltd.) as purchase consideration for con-
tracts secured, agreements entered into, and plant,
machinery and stock at factory in Underwood-street,
Sydney.
In a concern such as this, management is every-
thing ; so the Board is the old Board strengthened.
Old Board members, Hugh R. Denison (director
Australasian Tobacco Co., Ltd. the tobacco com-
pany that runs the allied tobacco companies in-
terests) and William Macleod and Walthcr Staerker.
To these are added E. Owen Cox (Birt and Co.,
Ltd.), and Sam Hordern (Anthony Hordern and
Sons). The whole of the working capital has been
underwritten. The share list opens on April 12,
closes on Thursday, April 20, but list will be closed
as soon as the full number of shares are applied for.
Applications may be sent through any broker, or to
the Secretary, J. H. Forrest, 129 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Where does the co. come in as to profits? It has
been pretty decidedly laid down by Commonwealth
and M.L. Parliaments that wireless is a postal mono-
poly—-excepting as to some small advisory business
on the naval side of the Defence Department. This
monopoly is a cast-iron one as regards . shore
stations; but as both Fisher and Ward promise an
Act making it compulsory for steamers to be fitted
by wireless, it widens the field of ship-station work-
ing. In short, the business is cut up between the
Government that runs the shore stations and the com-
pany that runs the installations on the ships. In
other words, the revenue from a message costing 55.,
either to or from a ship, will go 2s. 6d. into the
postal revenue and 2s. 6d. to the credit of the
ship’s wireless station, which explains the notifica-
tion in prospectus that arrangements have been made
for the installation and equipment of steamers
belonging to Union S.S. Co., A.U.S.N. Co., Hud-
dart Parker and Co., and Howard Smith’s. Natur-
ally these shipping companies come in on the
adoption of the company’s system by the Common-
wealth and Maoriland Governments. Then there are
the profits on Government installations, of which the
company already has on hand £35,000 worth.,
This, of course, is only a beginning.
The Company’s system deserves a word. It is
the Telefunken quenched singing spark. It has
been adopted officially by the Governments of Russia,
Austria, Germany, Spain, Portugal, Holland, Bel-
gium, Denmark, Norway, Sweden, Mexico, Brazil,
Argentine, Chili, Peru, Uruguay, and China, and
used almost exclusively by the Navies of these
Powers. The British and Indian Governments have
ordered Telefunken Field Wireless Outfits for Army
purposes, and the United States have recently in-
stalled with this system 20 of its long-distance
Naval Stations, and all of its new warships. This
system speaks in all languages and all codes, just
like the telegraph.
The “Patent Rights” bogey seems to have
been successfully laid, as the Telefunken patent
rights have never been successfully assailed, although
working alongside every system, in all countries, and
in all 'waters! Even the little 300-mile station on
the top of the Hotel Australia has sung nightly
unmolested, to such purpose that the Wellington
Postal authorities heard it talking from a little
tov station in the Wellington Post Office, whereupon
Ward took the system for Maoriland.

Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings of
flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply to the Australian Agents,
W. & J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. Hurst-
house and Co., of Wellington, the agents in New
Zealand.*

The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “Wilfley.” It is continuous, and
consists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged
in a tank of slimes or material to be Altered. The
drum is covered with a filter cloth supported on
grooves on the drum, which are connected by means
of holes to the interior to allow the liquid to be
withdrawn. Through the trunnions at the ends of
the drum the vacuum pipe passes to the interior.
This pipe connects with the interior of the drum,
through which the liquid and air are withdrawn by
means of a vacuum pump. The filtered slime is re-
moved by a Simple apparatus placed at or below the
level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly the
cake is removed, the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp
ready to receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the
sucking in of air, and consequent loss of vacuum
between the time the cake is removed and a fresh
one is forming. The residual liquid in the slime
cake is removed by distributing water over the sur-
face of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the fact that
the filter is circular in form, it will be seen that it
is continuous in operation, and has no complication
of valves to get out of order; furthermore, work is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manu-
facturing rights of this filter.* [2

Society. Its moderate premium rates, and its
promptness and liberality in the settlement of its
claims have gained for it a favorable and firm foot-
ing throughout the kingdom. The annual accounts
