Serving tea to one’s visitors
in the evening is an idea which
is catching on all over the world.
And men seem to appreciate it
just as much as the ladies do.
The next time you have visitors,
without saying anything to them
about it, have some tea freshly
made and offer it around, and you
will be surprised to see how many
will take it in preference to
anything else you may offer
them.
Of course, to be thoroughly
appreciated, you should use the
finest tea you can lay hands on,
which is Robur “ Special ” grade.
It's dainty aroma and rich good-
ness is most pleasing, and will
make your friends think —even
if they don’t say it —“ what a
lovely cup of tea.”
Quite recently it won the Gold
Medal at the Paris Exhibition;
it must be good tea to do that,
don’t you think 1
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 10, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
The launch of the first Australian-con-
structed warship Warrego was connected, by
official inference, with the Last Trump. The
invitation list disclosed the names of well-
known persons who are notoriously dead.
Not content with this intrusion into the
murky waters of Charon’s Ferry service, the
deceased were labelled amongst those who
had accepted the invitation to be present.
The Society of Ghosts ought to protest
against this infringement of their copyright
in Hades.
Mr. Share revised the invitation lists at
Admiralty and Government Houses. Not
before they had become monumental effigies
of official stupidity. Captain Leslie Wilson
had a memory. Mr. Robertson Kerr-Clark
always invited the living, on behalf of Gov-
ernor Rawson. Chamberlain Richard Nevill
a Silt-bound Australian edition of
v\ ho s Who.” Captain Ralph Yerney
casts an agreeable manner over the sorry
work of rejecting the socially importunate
who are aggressively alive. The desire to be
present at Governor Chelmsford’s Easter
party is a prevailing disease in dress circles
this week.
“All Nations’ Fair” was not a Beauty
competition. Occasionally a pretty girl
sprinkled the solid worth of serious suburban
matrons. The Wretch says it was so oc-
casional that male creatures explored the
Town Hall organ pipes, the visitors’ book,
and the dolleries stall before they could find
anything good-looking enough to “keep the
change.” Nevertheless, financial success
went on its way rejoicing, and the chink of
coin falling into the treasury box echoed in
Queen Victoria Markets.
The Puritans in the doll stall were
amongst the best-groomed girls in the charity
fete. Mrs. Michaelis was one of the notably-
responsible persons present. Miss Molly
Levy, who seems to have grown up sud-
denly, was probably the youngest damsel, in
dove grey, with white muslin fixings. The
Greek stall (Mrs. Ross responsible) put its
faith into hurried cream draperies, with the
Greek key pattern in gold soutache. The
attendants wore their heads in the orthodox
gilt straps. In their coiffures they were
distinctly early-Athenians. The China
ladies (Mrs. Meacle was stroke oar) showed
a fine contempt for the head-greasing com-
petition peculiar to the Chinese belle of the
well-oiled locks. China had to bear up un-
der modern Australian coiffures. An inde-
terminate skirt, under a tunic with a mild
Pekin flavor in its upper cut, gave an eva-
sive hint that China was present.
The Northern Suburbs promptly retired to
the kitchen. Things inanimate at this
stall —pots, pans, kettles, new and strange
devices for not boiling milk over on to the
stove made a strong appeal to every woman
ot homing instincts. Mrs. Treflg—with an
accent over the final e, and a husband in
the State Ministry—is this kind of woman.
Her pound cake has a serious reputation for
solid merit. Her way with a goffering iron
and a .muslin frill is said to exterminate
competition. She might have acted as
interpreter last week. Even the Northern
Suburbs, which live in the kitchen (the do-
mestic help being almost extinct in the wilds
of North Sydney), met its Waterloo. This
was a round iron vessel, with two bodies,
and large holes in its head. Will Mrs. Treflg
please explain its secret habits?
Akenehi (Miss Conor-O’Brien), whose
brilliant pen illuminated this page for nearly
10 years, dismissed herself a few weeks ago.
Her approaching marriage this week
with Mr. William Macleod was her sole ex-
cuse. Newspaper women of every shade of
politics gave Akenehi a farewell tea and
strawberry party at the Australia last
week. A silver chain-bag was the votive
offering to the departing writer of the
graphic phrase.
Whether the Sheffield Choir will find time
to unpack its voices when it strikes this city
in June is not known to the casual be-
holder of their entertainment list. The
resourceful Southwell must dive into de-
tails of this kind. The Philharmonic So-
ciety means to arrest the Sheffield Glees on
their arrival in Sydney, and will give the
visitors the largest welcome the Town Hall
can hold. A conversazione that is already
taking steps to turn itself into a ball is
ear-marked for the occasion.
Montreal is the possessor of a hall that'
can squeeze 17,000 people into two per-
formances. It cheerfully accomplished this
herring-barrel feat as a mark of its appre-
ciation of the Sheffield Choir combine of
Earls and girls.
Melbourne, with its Exhibition Building,
and Ballarat with its Coliseum, reach con-
cert pitch in the housing of Australians who
are determined to go to musical events
Sydney’s Town Hall, with 4000 people, or
thereabouts, jambed up together in agitated
tempers, with other persons’ boots scraping
their chair rails, is too small for special at-
tractions.
Sydney ’Varsity has evolved a woman
student who threatens to be an authority on
Australian mineralogy. Blessed be the
name of Smith! This bright and shining
light has found favor in the learned eyes of
the Senate. To Catherine Smith is award-
ed, therefore, the John Coutts and Deas-
Thomson scholarships. Post-graduate re-
search is her chief offence.. Miss Smith, in
consequence of scholarship trimmings, will
continue _to soak her grey matter in un-
tamed minerals. She takes a cheerful view
of their “optical properties.”
The man who prospects for minerals is
generally supposed to be the person who
wants optical properties. Perhaps Cather-
ine Scholarship Smith will invent some new
and reliable method of growing gold mines
in the back yards of the deserving poor.
This is her only hope of convincing male
cynics that a woman knows the technical
difference between a brass poker and a gold
muff-chain.
Australian manufacturers’ week has in-
cited a Sydney girl to deal severely with the
blouse. Most women need no introduction
to the vicious conduct of a blouse that bucks
at the belt. They lie in wait and stab it
ivith a safety pin. They claw it with a
sharp-tongued buckle. Every resource
known to the ingenuity of woman has at-
tacked the blouse in its weakest moments.
Miss Jeanne Roth, has, continuing the fight,
invented a new blouse-grip. Its prowess will
be displayed in shop windows next week.
Jeanne is the daughter of a Swiss father
(Mr. J. J. Roth) and a French mother.
Australian-born', she has the mixed nation-
ality useful to any girl who tackles the
old-time “rising-up” nuisance. I have tried
this invention on a young and innocent
blouse. It is still struggling to find out
what hits it in the (back when it tries to
rise out of its belt limit.
Miss Connie Simpson, who has taken her
Greek cameo profile to London music-halls,
writes to deny that she is still “Corona.”
Connie has knocked “Corona” into a departed
stage memory. As Constance Craven she
was playing and singing under Alfred Butt’s
management, at the Palace, when she sent
this inky item to meet your eye. Craven
is said, by Fog-bound managers, to adjust
itself better than Corona to the thick-headed
British suspicion of foreign sounds.
Constance Craven records meeting Maie
Sydney Reynolds in Piccadillly, “looking
very sweet and dainty.” She was then just
off to India. Constance also digresses into
harem skirts, and asserts that London (Feb.
28) was mad on two subjects—“ The Choco-
late _ Soldier,” and a new dancer, Sahary
Dzeli. This Oriental enchantress gives the
well-worn Salome dance one or two fresh
features. She substitutes a stained knife
for the gruesome head of Maud Allan’s first
frenzied leap into London Society.
Mrs. Nigel Gresley died recently in Lon-
don, at the age of 83. In the ’7o’s she was
known as one of Tasmania’s “leading host-
esses.” A brilliant musician in her youth,
Mrs. Gresley kept her musical faculties al-
most to the last. This is in sharp contrast
to the average modern matron. Her atti-
tude towards the family piano is to assault it
with a feather duster in the morning, and
hire it to a professional pianist in the after-
noon. Payment in kind —fruit salad and
weak tea —is going out of fashion.
Miss Christine Kong Sing will presently
add her artistic pin-feathers to the great
flight to London. She leaves Sydney
early in May. Miss Bess Norris (Mrs.
Tait), a friend of Christine’s student-days
in Melbourne, has achieved the golden art
of getting good prices for her miniatures.
She will hold out the brush of welcome to
Australian-born Kong Sing. Her objective is
to snare the elusive art of miniature paint-
ing in its native haunts.
Dolores has had to delay her return to
Australia, owing to the prevalence of a
Maoriland rush for her concerts.
George Alexander, like most managers in
a big way of business, is aggressive qn the
topic of amateur theatricals. As a passport
to the golden gates of stage-land—alleged to
be inlaid with diamond tiaras —he considers
a long course of amateur theatricals fatal to
success. Ella Crone, of this State, shows
every symptom of escaping this blight. She
has come, apparently unscathed, through an
epidemic of amateur charity performances.
Her stage presence is good. Her intelli-
gence is acutely dramatic. Miss Crone will
have left Sydney by the Orvieto before this
reaches you. She intends to pursue the
mummer’s art in London stage schools.
Miss Madge Hellmrich escaped the kitchen
tea which generally inundates, with sauce-
pan lids, the bride-elect who has a large
circle of admiring girl friends. A swan-
song concert, to commemorate her marriage,
was the farewell offering of Girl-urbia.
Longstaff threatens an immediate descent
on this city, and may be here before the
ink dries on this letter.
The Frank Coffee American tour is likely
to begin when Sydney achieves its nearest
attempt at mid-winter weather. Mr. Coffee
was an American citizen, who once believed
he would rather be run over and killed by
a New York street car than live happy
ever after amongst Australian wattles. He
has changed his mind, and cut his wisdom
teeth. Years bring these treasures to the
mouth of middle age—to take liberties with
Carlyle, who forgot to add whether' they
could be left in a glass of water overnight.
The naturalised Australian, Frank Coffee, is
as patriotic as the home-grown bean. He
has developed into a good Commonwealth
citizen, and finds life at Chatswood much
more alluring than death on a New York
tram line.
Mrs. John Toohey, of Wahroonga, has en-
tered into possession of the Greenoaks Cot-
tage.
The Jim Osbornes have taken Uig
Lodge, the Beatons’ house at Point Piper.
They will abide there during the race-week
festivities, and for some time after. The
Wills-Allens have had the place for the last
couple of years, but Mr. Wills-Alien, sen.,
died in it a couple of weeks ago, and so they
are going elsewhere. Mr. Osborne, by the
way, ever in the forefront of fashion, is de-
liberately growing whiskers. The “best
people” of the male sex are taking enthusi-
astically to these adornments in London.
Mrs. Markell, one of Sydney’s most fas-
tidious frockers, is proving the charm of a
blue and emerald combine. Certain shades
of green and soft azure tints of blue were
the first choice in Nature’s original color-
box —witness the atmospheric garb of Aus-
tralia on a fine day. Seen recently in a
street costume, Mrs. Markell had laid blue
and green under tribute to her charms. Hat,
frock, parasol and jewellery were a fine
blend of the two colors, which, in partner-
ship, w r ould have sent our grandmothers into
hysterics.
The harem skirt is now officially known
as the jupe-culotte. According to the Il-
lustrated London News, it has recently
taken an airing in Regent-street. M. Poiret
is, so far, the only famous French dress-
maker who has a good word for the jupe-
culotte. He believes it will lead to an era
of ankle-bangles, handsome shoes, and high-
stepping soles. Worth has d —d it with
faint praise. He regards the “harem” as
the only logical sequence of the hobble skirt
—otherwise, as a passing folly.
The pros and cons of “dressing for the
theatre” are getting much press discussion in
London. There the snobs who sit among
the nobs in the most expensive seats have
always put on their war-paint, under the pre-
tence that it is their everyday dinner garb.
In London, the custom of “dressing the
house” is, or was, maintained by the system
of “papering” it, for the free passes to pri-
vate boxes, Stalls, or grand circle, bore the
printed order—-“ Evening dress indispensa-
ble.” In Paris and New York patrons go as
they please to theatres, and the deadheads
are not more beautifully arrayed than those
who buy their seats. Australia’s big cities
thus far have been slavishly English in their
dress-circle customs, and here, even more
than in the great British Fog, the women
have delighted to go to the play as though
they were going' to a dance—the men being
bound more or less to follow suit. Natur-
ally, the mummers plead for the continuance
of this feminine rule. It pleases their
vanity. It pleases, also, the management
that part of the “house” should provide
something of a show for the rest of the
house. Louis Nethersole, who acted as
business manager for Margaret Anglin in
Australia, rejoiced over the smart appear-
ance of a first night crowd in Melbourne or
Sydney, as compared with a fashionable
audience in America. Yet it is doubtful
whether the war-paint regulation does more
good than harm to theatrical business. The
London papers have almost decided that
many people are staying away from theatres
because of the trouble of changing their
clothes for the sole purpose of going to see
a play. In this country there is more
temptation for the women to flaunt abroad
in airy finery, but the tax upon the com-
mon-sense of the men is almost as severe as
in the Cold Country, and they are likely to
insist upon a revolt against the rule of
“Evening dress indispensable.”
“Have you forgotten, father dear, all the
wonderful stories with which you used to
enchant our long country walks, when my
tiny legs were unwilling, but when I forgot
to complain of fatigue, asking you instead :
Et alors, petit pere, what did the Princess
do?’ ” Thus “Martouche,” in the brief pre-
face to “Phrynette and London.”
The woman who can write a book without a
preface, or make a remark without a post-
script, is on the high road to fame. Marthe
Troly-Curtin, author of “Phrynette,” has not
escaped the' preface, but she is picturesque
and abbreviated. Dymock’s are responsible
for the arrival of “Phrynette” in the middle
of this letter. She is worth quoting.
“Amongst the things I jotted down in my
note-book,” says “Phyrnette” of France, “is,
that skating Englishwomen seem to have a
fondness for feathery hats and anachronistic
streamers... .My aunt is busy making hor-
rid little crochet things for a bazaar. She
asked me if I could help her. I had to con-
fess I could not. She looked surprised.
‘Why, all my er —under —clothing, I
trimmed myself with crochet as a girl.’ I had
thought as much. I could not imagine Aunt
Barbara’s lingerie of transparent batiste,
trimmed with frothy Valenciennes.”
Roycroft and other Sydney libraries are
circulating Elinor Glyn’s last book, “His
Hour.” “Women will have it, and give it
up with reluctance,” said one librarian who
is steeping his aching brow in cold tea-
leaves. He has to keep calm. “Why don’t
men go to church?” and “Why don’t you
get more copies of ‘His Hour’?” are smash-
ing up the mental outlook of every other
young and susceptible librarian. The Glyn
maelstrom of whirligig emotions ds drawing
the Young Person to worship at the shrine
of the Greek god.
Elinor Glyn found her opening situation
for “His Hour” at the feet of the Sphinx.
Tamara, the heroine, is obviously English,
in spite of her Russian name. She visited
the Sphinx—in search of a new sensation —
at 2 o’clock in the morning. Elinor Glyn
knows all the romantic ropes that tug even
a tourist resort into a daring defiance of the
narrow ethics of Mrs. Grundy. Her clever,
vulgar style is full -of sharp epigrams. Her
saving grace is an unshaken desire to hit
Mrs. Grundy in her line of least resistance.
She introduces the inevitable —a beautiful
bounder of princely rank, in this manner:
“At first I was angry,” he says, “when I saw
your camel against the sky.... I like to
have her (the Sphinx) all to myself. But
afterwards, when I watched you, I saw you
meant no harm; you aren’t a tourist, and
so you did not matter.” Tamara, though
a nicely-brought-up young woman, takes
this impertinence in good part. The Sphinx
is chaperon. Prince Milaelavski leaves the
impression, on the merely Australian reader,
that his beauty would have been spoiled in
any Commonwealth school or college by a
comprehensive thrashing at least once in
every day.
Many passages in “His Hour” make the
same appeal to the Young Person of to-day
that Ouida made to the schoolgirls of 20
years ago. Ouida was devoured in prim
boarding-schools, by the uncertain light of
secreted candle-ends. Elinor Glyn is swal-
lowed in public. Times have changed for
the better. Novels of a certain type-’ show
less improvement in their false heroics and
fancy-dress emotions.
“An Australian Night” will be given to
Madame Boult on April 21. As hon. sec.
of the Australian Dramatic Authors’ Asso-
ciation, this compliment is intended as a
strong hint that the said authors appreciate
her interest in the now going concern. St.
James’s Hall, Phillip-street, will be top-
dressed in palms for the Boult entertain-
ment.
Wandering Australians who scratch the
snow at St. Moritz for Swiss glacial-period
amusements, send me joyous, frost-bitten
post-cards. They don’t seem to remember
that we have heard of St. Moritz before to-
day. The late George Robertson, of Colac,
an Australian, really started the festive
Moritz on its prosperous career in the tobog-
ganing trade.
Carine Cadby, who shakes an informative
pen on Alpine sports, is very sound on the
reformative effect of ski-ing for “catty” wo-
men. Tobogganing is also said to be a
good nerve cure. For the neurasthenic pets of
modern society, however, ski-ing is particu-
larly commended. Husbandettes might
also try it. It gives courage. One of its
charms is that it is such a sociable sport.
The girl who sprains her ankle to enlist the
sympathy of a handsome young man needn’t
play those pranks at a ski-ing party. The
active beau is too busily enployed pulling
his broken nose out of an adjacent snow-
drift to worry about good-looking ankles
that belong to other people. The woman
tvho can’t learn to take care of her feet is
safer in a St. Moritz hotel drawing-room.
She can put them on the fender.
From “the man Outback” came a cheque
for St. Margaret’s Hospital for Women.
Matron Abbott forwards a consignment of
thanks per safe conduct of the penny post.
Yours, with a broad nib,

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, April 10, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Two separate garden-parties sunned themselves
on the clipped lawns of Stonington last week. On
Monday, beneath a blue sky, the bulk of the
motoring multitude ran out and shook hands,
l’he Carmichael lady, in a petunia-colored gown,
stood under an awning for this wearisome part of
the function. Niece Ena, in a white muslin
frock, and with her hair up, helped in the polite
pump-handling, and Governor Carmichael smiled
and bowed like clockwork. Secretary Victor Hood
and Aide Vaux directed the stream of guests to the
vice-regal pressure, whence it flowed on to the ter-
races and lawns. Stonington is an ideal place for
carden parties. The house ODens its hospitable
doors to the men guests, while the women trail
round the flowers and wait for afternoon tea in
the usual marquee. This time the Pierrots had a
circus tent all to themselves on the upper terrace,
where roses nod in the wind when there happens
to be any.
The continued-in-our-next party on Tuesday
afternoon was devoted mostly to artistic people,
though the Zoological Society was also repre-
sented. The Carmichaels showed some origin-
ality in the drawing up of the two guest-lists.
No ordinary garden could possibly hold all the
people who figure on the vice-regal visitors’ list.
Former magnates got out of the difficulty by
inviting people alphabetically. The arrangement
did not bring any joy to the people who were
recorded in the M to Z ledger, because the A to L
division got first call every time. Mrs. Acid
laughed at Mrs. Muchcash, and the air of
superiority Miss Lukewarm assumed when she
met poor little Miss Zero was unbearable.
On Tuesday, Federal members had their innings
with the Art section. Prime Minister Fisher and
Mrs. Fisher drank their tea at the Governor’s
table along with the “Jack ’’ Thompsons and the
Peter Mcßrides. The Mcßrides brought with
them a 17-year-old daughter with her own curls
pinned up in the Early Victorian style, which is
just in again. The hostess wore intricate braid-
ings all over her mauve-colored gown. Mrs.
Fisher wpre a black gown low in the neck, accord-
ing to the latest fashion ukase, and a turban hat
of royal blue with a band of gold.
The .Cloyne Maddens were absent in a
bunch, the day being the anniversary of one of
their many family bereavements. The Frank
Madden two were present, however, dressed in a
droopy, l artistic way. A girl who drew the atten-
tion of all was Roma Clarke, daughter of W. J. T.
Clarke, of Mount Schanck, in South Australia,
where the Carmichaels stayed during their Mount
Gambier wanderings. She’ was quite the best-
dressed girl on the lawn, in a lovely white frock.
Artist Longstaff held a reception of his own on
the lower terrace. The Abrahams pair were also
on hand, and several studios full of V.A.S. mem r
hers wandered amiably at large. Ambrose Patter-
son had his n«Jw little wife with him, under a
granny hat. Author Paul Wentz cut his
part in the social stroll as short as pos-
sible,'and drifted away. Some artistic souls
perched the stove-pipe hat of convention on top
of their artistically tangled hair, and thereby
achieved a weird effect which almost stampeded
their friends. The University crowd was also in-
cluded in the Tuesday party. Serious professors
gave up an hour or two to butterfly fluttering,
and seemed to enjoy themselves in a placid, re-
flective way. Mrs. “Queen’s” Sugden wore a
dress all browns and autumn tones.
The music, supplied by a band and a string
orchestra, was intensely Scawtch. Also, two
kilted warriors pranced nimbly through a sword
dance.
The Arts and Crafts gathered tearfully for
their third annual meeting on Friday night.
Lady Carmichael, as president, was in the
chair for the very last time. The Governor and
Aide Vaux accompanied the capable lady and
listened attentively to the remarks she made to
the admiring band of craftswomen. She quoted
the cjmical words of some unobserver that “art
in Melbourne has been, so far, in a tub” (it
sounded like “pub”), and declared that, if that
was so, the Arts and Crafts Society was at any
rate pulling it out.
The room was cosy with hatted and furred
dames, and the function had the friendly atmo-
sphere of a come-in-and-hring-your-work after-
noon. This despite a plentiful sprinkling of men
—mostly of the decorative (or decorating) species.
Architect Walter Buller made one of the throng,
and I noticed the aesthetic furnishing Stockfeldt!
Auguste Fischer (who only sees through the glow
of stained-glass windows) and the artistic Black-
well were also about. Mrs. President, with two
big blooming cabbage roses in her black dress,
sailed confidently ahead, and fairly pelted the
house with practical suggestions. “Specialise in
the prizes,” said she. “ Last exhibition we had a
model nursery for a house. Give a prize for a
library or a dining-room ; only make the onlooker
realisetheproportions.” Thensheranon to describe
an ideal living room in the house of author George
Macdonald, and advanced the suggestion that the
society should take possession of almost-completed
houses, “such as are springing up all round,” and
design all the decorations, including the garden.
“ Commandeer one room for A. and C. exhibits,”
advised the clear-eyed enthusiast 1 , “until the
work is finished.” Another striking suggestion
was that the society should gather together a loan
collection of portraits from the people who have
good things hanging on their walls, and like them
to be admired.
The Art's and Crafts Society is branching out.
An exhibition will be held in Launceston during
the first week in May. The whole Commonwealth
is invited to send exhibits. Victorians may send
through secretary Mac George, who has her office
in the Collins-street rooms.
The Osborne-Atherton wedding was a theatri-
cally spectacular affair. The beautiful bride was
attended by a group of beautiful bridesmaids.
(Which seems to show that the bride isn’t of a
jealous disposition. It takes a lot of good-nature
and courage to invite comparison.) St. John’s
ivy-covered church in Bast St. Kilda provided the
setting. “She” wore crystal-white satin, thus
bravely pitting her brilliant complexion against
daring white, instead of softening ivory. The
dress was a long-trained affair covered with
crystal beads and fringes on Brussels net.
The veil, which was pleated round her face like a
Butch cap, was adorned with orange blossom.
Then came the phalanx of bridesmaids—Molly
arid Muriel Atherton, Gwladys Chirnside, Nan
O’Hara and Gladys Box —in pink satin. Their
frocks kept the women guests whispering for a
quarter of an hour. “Fishtail backs !” was ex-
citedly telegraphed all round the sacred edifice.
They also had the newest kimona coats of net and
lace, and their dainty bonnets of pink tulle and
silver lace glimmered in the stained-glass sunlight.
Everybody was at the wedding, or else came on
from the other orange-blossom affair a mile or two
away. Most of the motoring people “did ’’ the
two—the reception parts, at any rate. The re-
ception occurred at Illawarra, in Toorak, the
Atherton house, which had been redecorated for
the occasion. All Toorak was in attendance.
Ruby Madden-Osborne was over for the affair,
also Mr. and Mrs. Hope Osborne and the rest of
the clan. The ballroom was hung with native
clematis, and the breakfast was overhung with
pink roses. Mrs. Atherton wore a smoke-grey
dress in tulle and satin, with some red Jacqueminot
roses for color. The bride put on black velvet for
travelling, with a coat and furs of musquash that
a thoughtful bridegroom had provided. Her
granny motoring hat, which accompanied, was of
royal blue and black.
At time of writing, speculation js rife as to
whether the Buchanan-Cumming or the Brookes-
Emmerton wedding will reach the loftiest top
note. Each is to be a veritable scream. But as
the bridegroom and the best man at the former
wedding are to be dressed for the part in their
respective war paint, and as the ceremony is to be
witnessed by the G.-G., it is likely it will win by
half a tone. There seems to be no just cause or
impediment why Norman Brookes, the tennis hero,
should not also sport his insignia of Greatness; and
his best man could be beautiful in a blazer too.
The Emmerton heiress set Australia at naught as
regards her trousseau, for she and her parents
tripped off to Europe to collect it. The bridal
veil and gown are of valuable Venetian lace. A
court train of velvet will be held up by a small
Staughton girl, whose mamma had her court train
upheld on her wedding day by the Emmerton
chick. A diamond necklace, given by her parents,
will glitter upon the bride’s neck.
The future Mrs. Buchanan will not be out-
classed in the lace scheme, as her all-white will be
enriched with old Wragge lace. Not old rag,
but precious fabric which has been in the Wragge
family for years, they say. (N.B.—One of the
Gumming brothers took unto himself a very
pretty Wragge for better or worse, and ’tis she
who is responsible for the decoration above-
mentioned.) Lauehlan Mackinnon, the third, will
keep the gallant captain in countenance by
attending to the bridal train in all the bravery of
kilt and sporran.
Said that a marriage is forthcoming between a
western district girleen and the successful peti-
tioner in a famous divorce case. No sooner had
the poor man disembarrassed himself of the
respondent than Rumor found many “second
tries” for him. One effort of the old lady’s
imagination was to assign him as a candidate for the
hand from which he had got legal release. There
was nothing whatever in that yarn; but Rumor
seems to be telling the truth this last time.
The other Wednesday the City newsboys ten-
dered a goodbye concert to their President, the
Carmichael lady, at their rooms in Coromandel
place. A couple of hundred young voices which
nightly quicken the pulse of the city bv calling,
“ ’Orrible Tragedee /” cheered their kindly
patroness as she entered the hall, and later the
owners of the voices received from her hands the
picnic prizes, including suits of clothes and strong
boots. The clothes were examined and the boots
tried_ on at the back of the hall with much
rustling of brown paper. Then, in their turn,
theswarm of small hosts presented the lady with a
fountain pen. While struggling through'a pretty
speech of thanks, the recipient was encouraged to
proceed by a shout of “ That's all right, Mum ! ”
from a hoarse-voiced lad at the back. In addition
to the Pathe Freres pictures of cowboys and
things, IJnie Russel] and some other clever people
contributed a good programme of music. Among
the visitors on the platform were Vera Clarke,
the vice-president, and Colonel Reay, of the
’Erald, the evening sheet in which the boys are
at least commercially interested.
Paris Nesbit, the handsome K.C. who had a
howling match with a Port Melbourne audience
on Saturday, had just got back to Adelaide from
the remunerative boundary dispute when he was
induced to return to Melbourne by the “ Liberal ”
side to speak against the Referenda proposals.
Nesbit never uses notes. He ruminates in
some quiet spot for an hour or two, and mar-
shals whatever arguments he has. Then he rises
prepared for the fray, and, when the right
moment comes, starts the oratorical procession.
When he isn’t allowed to proceed without inter-
ruption he asks his audience if they would rather
be bigger fools than they look, or look bigger
fools than they are, and sits down angrily
on his hat. He is always liable to break out in
unexpected places. Years ago his front name used
to be spelt with two s’s; but he made up his mind
that that was wrong, and so got the S. Australian
Court to prune one of them off. He used to occa-
sionally bring out a paper to uplift Adelaide; and
once he wrote in it an article to maintain his view
that The Bulletin is one of the half-dozen most
really and truly religious papers in the English-
speaking world. In fact, I think his idea was
that there were no more than the half-dozen alto-
gether. I’m sure you’ll be sorry to hear that he
didn’t even mention the S. M. Herald.
Ye Olde Englyshe Fayre (blame the invitation
card for the spelling), in aid of the Alfred Hos-
pital, is to be officially opened on Saturday after-
noon, April 15, in the vast, barnlike Exhibition.
The Carmichaels are to do the little job in polite
burgling. Immediately afterwards, sassiety
matrons and more or less sweet girls will start to
hold up the public in the interest of Charity.
Mrs. McCubbin turned the opening of the
McCubbin-cum-Patterson picture show into an
affair of drawing-room cosiness. There was an
unusually large number of males on hand for the
opening ceremony, including several of the picture-
buying variety. Laurie Abrahams, who has lent
a lot of his McCubbin paintings to the show, was
present, of course. Hr. Myer, too, was among
the inveterate art hunters; whilst Bernard Hall
and other Feltonians prowled with interest.
Monty Brown (who shortly exhibits), Mrs. May
Vale Gilfillan, Mrs. Heardon and Dr. Wood like-
wise helped to obstruct the view. A little girl,
who ambled round the room in a fierce little toque
of bright yellow, perched on the side of
her bronze locks, was a startling sight even
for an Art show. Beatrice Day wandered
about in a simple white frock, with a bunch
of violets tucked into her waist belt. The
McCubbin hostess, in a high-waisted grey
velvet frock and a black picture hat, looked as
if she had just walked out of a frame. Young
McCubbins hovered round, handed pr ogrammes,
and generally looked after the comfort of the
drifting visitor. Sheila and Kathleen, the two
little McCubbin maids whose portraits adorn the
walls, were there in the flesh. Mrs. Abrahams
bought a picture of Lou MeCubbin’s, called “The
Stone Crusher.” The young man is an ardent
student, and those who know what is what in the
world of art are confident that he will do bie
things.
Ambrose Patterson had a corner full of his
most recent work, including some drawings and
several glimpses of Port Phillip beaches. “ Beau-
maris” is a picture looked at keenly by the
Gallery ; also a fine picture of detail called “On
Change.” Pretty Mrs. Ambrose, a young Eng-
lish girl who came out to Australia to marry her
artist just a year ago, helped in the entertaining.
Quite a party of old Paris chums gathered round
her, and talked gaily of old times.
I suppose Prime Minister Fisher, now on the
high seas, will take himself to Edinburgh
after the Coronation, if it be only to pose against
the much-photographed Wall. Fisher has been
farewelled to so many bagpipes that he owes it to
his friends to visit the place. It has become
almost a proverb that no Scotchman goes back to
his own country for good. They do revisit it,
but only to show themselves and remember how
poo-er they were before they got out. Then they
come away again.
Mrs. Secretary Sheppard, whose husband at-
tends Prime Minister Fisher, had her own batch
of good-byes, and gathered a crop of bouquets
from her window of the departing Adelaide
express. She takes away with her a trunk or so
of Australian-made frocks.
The Dudley children have been gathered
together, well wrapped up and shipped to
England. Doctor Dunhill, who did, some ’itis
chasing in the Dudley family, instead of going in
the later Morea, as he originally intended, handed
his august patients over to another medicine man,
grabbed his professional bag and a suit case, and
rushed for the liner with the olive branches. Ifi
all probability a rush made in courtesy to the
Countess, who felt qualms about entrusting her
family during a long, wobbly voyage to the care
of nurses, governesses and maids.
The manager of a tall hotel in this city said he
felt like a Salt Lake elder when the census papers
happened along. He was merely head of the
house according tp the regulations ; but flurried
guests who didn’t understand their papers marked
him down as “ Father ” and “Husband.” It took
himself and a worried collector several solid hours
to straighten,out the papers and his own repu-
tation.
Mollie Carpw has taken a 12 months’ engage-
ment from J. C. Williamson to play Nellie Wil-
son’s parts. Miss Mollie has stacks of confidence
and other valuable goods. When she interviewed
the J.C. W. management it requested her to sing,
and proposed to call in music and accompanist.
The little lady flirted her bright head and said
that neither was necessary, and forthwith sat
down and sang and played music. Then the
astounded managers engaged her on the spot.
Also her best girl pal got an engagement too, so
that the fair Mollie would not be lonely.
“Flat” life in the city affords one some queer
roof-glimpses. A Gothic thatch, festooned with
“the wash,” sets me wondering how the washer-
lady managed to negotiate the Alpine slopes
without the usual icepick and rope. Looking out of
another window at the back of a pile of buildings
which present a dignified front to a fashionable
street andareinhabited principally by medical men,
I observe the spiral fire-escape which offers the hur-
ried butcher, baker and milk-ob the only way of
getting at their high-level customers. Marianne,
spick and span in the regulation uniform, looks
from her breezy steel watch-tower at a bread-
laden knight clattering gaily up in a sort of aerial
dance. It is idyllic to see her leaning out like
the Blessed Damozel and taking the offerings
from the panting devotee.
Melbourne people are promised anew moun-
tain-air resort not too far from town. About four
miles from the Warburton railway station there
is an earth pimple which rises fully three-quarters
of a mile above the station level. A winding-road
about eight miles long would make the summit
accessible to coaches and open the route to habi-
tation, so the Public Works Department talks of
making it.
The way Australian political visitors are being
quartered on the British nobility during Corona-
tion time reminds one of the way most of the big
church conventions are run. Staunch adherents
get strange, visiting clergymen dumped upon
them ; they take whatever angel has been allotted
them, and treat him as kindly as possible. Any
private thoughts they have in the matter are
buried deep in their churchy hearts, or hissed at
intimate friends behind the Sabbath belltopper.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
There are signs that we are awakening from our
summer lethargy. Some dances threaten, and on Saiur-
dy the North Adelaide Golf Club is to smite its first ball
to an accompaniment of afternoon tea. Last Saturday
the S.A.J.C. Autumn meeting transpired at Morphett-
vil'le. Sartorially, it was an off day. Frockers hesi-
tated, for the most part, between remnants from
last winter and belated muslins. The most profit-
able happening of the day was Brownloek’s win
in the Hurdles, which gave his supporters £27 10s.
for £1. There was the usual rush and scramble at train
and tote ; if a paternal Government won’t increase our
betting facilities, it might at least protect us from
trampled toes and battered headgear on the road.
By the way, are jockeys superstitious? All the “ boys”
sported crfipe armlets for late President Baker, and it’s
not everyone who’d care to start in a hurdle scramble so
bedizened.
Secretary von Doussa looks like establishing a re-
cord for Onkaparinga. "Gates and booths have sold
for £1058—top price to date—and everything on wheels
has long since been chartered for the day. The Club has
made some necessary innovations and alterations, which
include a motor enclosure and an improved entrance.
Our Admiral Ex. doesn’t let the grass grow under his
feet. He’d no sooner returned from the South E ist (and
made nice remarks anent its need of a decent harbor)
than he set out for Moonta and Wallaroo. Wberebi’
hangs a tale. Kadina was to have been included in the
itinerary. But the City Fathers had no official knowledge
of the visit till too late —that is to say, till a local mag-
nate had commandeered vice-rovalty. The mayoral
dignity felt hurt, and said so. Telegrams flew ’twixt
Government House and an almost tearful Council. The
Upshot was that his Ex. has. been politely invited to
officially visit Kadina in October, and not before.
Patrons of “The Dandies ” lam-nt the illness of Mrs.
Claude Dampier. As Ireha de Vere the little lady has
danced and smiled her way to wide popularity. She and
her husband contribute to much of the success of the
show, and appear to enjoy it themselves as much as
anyone.
Talking of sickness, doctors and lodge-patients are at
variance, and apparently the Adelaide Hospital is em-
broiled. Anyway, Grand Mast- r Solomon, of the
A.N.A., threatens to establish a hospital of their own for
their own benefit.
The Grosser Kurfurst further depleted our population
to the tune of medico Bollen and his wife. When he’s
not ricking his neck looking at processions, the doctor
will turn his attention to the latest in x-rays and operat-
ing apparatus. There’s some talk of a big hospital near
Woodville, which is within the Bollen range of practice.
After a perfect orgy of farewells, the Mayor Cohens
make their final bow on Friday.
At present parading these streets are two individuals
with wild beards and long, lank hair stretching wpll
below their waists. They affect mustard-colored suits,
and are here as the exponents of a doctrine which
preaches, among other things, absolute immortality.
Most people would prefer a shorter life and a less extra-
ordinary appearance.
The Society of Arts’ annual show is responsible for the
exhibition of some good work. Hans Heysen deals hap.
pily with gum trees and oxen. He gets the spirit of the
bush excellently on to his canvas, and his work is suffi-
cient answer to those who blab of “monotony” and
“lack of character” in our scenery. President Gill’s
contributions include a “Portrait of a Lady,’,’ which is
worth looking at. There are, by the way, more portraits
and figure studies than usual, and if some of them deserve
more credit for technique than beauty, it’s neverthe-
less a phase of work to encourage. A head of an
old fisherman, by Rose Maepherson, calls for com-
ment, and the same lady carries all before her iii the still-
life class. The Ashtons, between them, cover a wide
range from seascape to suburbia. A couple of Victorian
scenes from Edward Davies and a head by Elsie Wells
also deserve the nod of approval.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

This Woman Spent Weeks
in Agony Until She Tried
the Tonic Treatment.
• o
Many who are tortured with neuralgia will read
with interest the statement of Mrs. J. Adcock, of
Bunburv, W.A., who was cured of a very severe
case by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Her experience
is only one of many, as these pills have cured a
host of cases, and those who have used them tor
neuralgia have gone on sound medical lines.
Neuralgia is a nervous disorder, and experience
has proved that there is no better nerve tonic than
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Mrs. Adcock, whose
husband has a store on White’s Road, lives at the
corner of Railway Parade and Carey Road, She
“ We were living in Derby, and the| climate
there affected my nerves and brought on neuralgia.
I suffered dreadfully for weeks, and in spite of all
the remedies I tried I could not find any relief.
The pain was present all the time. I could not
sleep or eat. and fell away in looks. I would try
applications of hot salt and hot bran, and anything
that I might get a little ease from. Every nerve
of my face gave me pain. It would throb up to
my temples and affect the back of my eyes. 1
could not see to read or sew. At night I could not
sleep. 1 would rise and walk about quite frantic.
It was in cold weather, and I dreaded to go out-
side; the nagging, shooting pains always felt
worse, and I would often have to bandage my face
to avoid a chill. I don’t know how I got through
the day’s duties, but they had to be done. I tried
many remedies all in vain, then I read in a news-
paper about Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and Mr.
Adcock got them for me out of our store. I hey
gradually toned up my system, and drove the
neuralgic pains away. I am always glad to re
commend this tonic, for I have never had an
attack since, my health remaining capital, i
took three boxes altogether.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by chemists
and storekeepers, or sent by mail, post paid on
receipt of price, 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d
by the Dr. Williams Medicine Co., of Australasia
Ltd., Sydney.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
When Alfred Deakin spoke in Adelaide he
dwelt upon the probable awful consequences to
the railways if the people voted “Yes” with
regard to Federal Arbitration. “ Everybody,” he
said, “will ask: “ Who owns the railways?’”
And, as he asked “Who owns the railways?”
Deakin turned to his chairman. His name was
Cohen.
“Vic.”: Justice Higgins’s recent lecture
on the Growth of Constitutions throws up the
windows and invites us to look back. The first
Victorian Parliament sat in St. Patrick’s Hall,
from 1851 to 1856-. This St. Patrick’s Hall Par-
liament was very one-sided. In theory the Par-
liament was half nominee and half representa-
tives ; but the second half represented sheep and
cattle more than men. The Speaker was Dr.
James Frederick Palmer, duly qualified, but re-
duced to gingerbeer making. He was an ex-
mayor of Melbourne, and in 1856 became Presi-
dent of the Legislative Council. An invaluable
contemporary picture, by Strutt, shows the St.
Patrick’s Hall Parliament. O’Shanassy loomed
very large in it in every sense ; he followed Went-
worth’s course in turning from Radical to Tory.
On the other hand, McCulloch, afterwards the
Radical, was picked by Governor Latrobe as a
fitting Tory Nominee.
Notable how Decayed Fish and “ Liberal-
ism ” walk ever hand in hand, so to speak. The
following from the Australasian has been re-
printed in at least four English dailies in the last
two months :
Membership of Parliament (in Australia) is deemed a
reasonable and natural ambition for any person, how-
ever uncouth and uninformed. Knowledge, experience
and strength of character are, indeed, by many people
deemed disqualifications, for they unfit men to content
themselves with the ignoble role of a cog in the caucus
wheel. The position is regarded as a “ billet ” pure and
simple—a billet requiring the suppression of one’s indi-
viduality, it is true, but well paid, and still conferring
some conventional distinction.
And then the Australasian— the paper which
represents that the country it lives in is run by
uncouth people, lacking in knowledge, experience
and character—wonders why Britons are shy
about immigrating here.
APPLES OF OBLIVION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Tory lay on his last lone bed ;
The tears were wept, and the prayers were said,
And back he fell on the pillow—dead.
A floating cloud of inflated lies,
His soul soared up through the starry skies
Till it reached the Gardens of Paradise,
Where angel-guardians hailed him : “ Wait!
Here Apples hang, of the store of Fate,
Which each must taste ei i he pass the Gate :
“So swallow Oblivion’s fruit, and thus
Forget the Earth and become as Us ! ”
But the Soul outspluttered : “ Prepos-ter-ous!!
“ Shall 1, a provincial Soul, wot fights
For States, forget—in a few swift bites —
All thoughts o’ States an’ their damaged Rights?
“An’ threats that ‘the Constitootion fails’?
An’ the Sydney Morning Blowfly’s tales
Of ‘Gross Affronts’ upon Noo South Wales?
“ An’ th’ ‘ Federal Pin-pricks,’ sharp and sore ?
An’ Bruce Smith, wild with his yells of ‘ War !’?
Why, ivot on earth is a Heaven for t
“ I spurn your ‘ Bliss’! I refuse your Prize 1
I prefer my crusted Provincial lies
To all the Happles o’ Paradise ! ”
He turned, as one who had chosen well,
And toddled down to the brawls in Hell;
While out from realms where the Great Bear plods
Rang the endless mirth of the Great High Gods.

AUGUSTUS BLOWFLY.
IT IS POSSIBLE TO REDUCE FLESH
WITHOUT DIETING OR EXERCISE.
It is not only possible but being done everywhere.
Exercise, unless carefully professionally superin-
tended, may easily do a deal of harm to the over-
burdened body. The stout man or woman who is
determined to get thin is only too likely to over-
strain the muscles by ill-advised exercise, and so
cause troubles that may develop into something
far worse than too much flesh. The same with
dieting. Starvation is certainly effective, but it is
sure to weaken the patient constitutionally, while
at the same time it is constant torture.
Fortunately neither dieting nor exercising is
required or desirable if one uses the right remedy
for reducing fat, and, strange to say, that remedy
is a simple, wholesome home mixture the ingre-
dients of which are obtainable at any chemist’s at
a small cost. This mixture is as follows : One half-
ounce of Marmola, one ounce of fluid extract of
Glycyrrhiza B.P., one ounce of pure Glycerine
B.P., and Peppermint Water to make six ounces ;
atid the proper amount to take is two teaspoonfuls
after meals. This mixture takes the fat off rapidly
but naturally, so that no wrinkles are formed and
no harm is done to the stomach, as is too frequently
the case with “ patent ” or "secret” remedies.
In order to meet a constantly repeated demand,
the above prescription has been condensed in the
form of a compact and perfect tablet known as
Marmola Prescription Tablets, and those readers
who prefer them to the liquid form can obtain same
of any chemist, 3s. Gd. per box, or post free from
The “Marmola Co., 7 Barrack Street, Sydney,
N.S.W.*
The tremendous indignation of Wade
(V.C.), Wood, Garland, Solemn Lee and the
Sydney daily press at the alleged “gagging” of
Holman recalls the famous scenes in the N.S.W.
House when Wood was putting the Coercion Act
through. Then the gentlemen who are now
shedding crocodile-tears over the silence of
Holman made no bones of moving the gag at
every stage of a bill which was without parallel in
N.S.W. legislation since the infamous Treason
Felony Act of the Ws. To be wept over by
Wood, Wade, Garland, and the whole crowd of
his former gaggers ought to be enough to make
Holman’s gorge rise. ,
This is how the Australian correspondent
of one Maoriland paper described lou Ward’s
dash through Sydney en route to the Big Smoke :
His visit was admirably stage-managed ; his whole
journey is admirably managed. Seemingly he will reach
London, an Ambassador of Empire, before any of the
other Prime Ministers ; and be will have the colonial
stage to himself quite long enough to be able to show
England that his is the leading role. Mr. Fisher, for Aus-
tralia, straggling in several weeks later on, will find the
limelight pre-occupied. Once more Maoriland is lead-
ing, and competition follows a long way behind. After
three days in Sydney—one for “ Veni,” one for “ Vidi ”
and one for “ Vici”—Mr. Ward is on his way to Canada,
with triumphant cable messages preceding him, and con-
gratulations bobbing in his wake.
And after a few weeks in London Joseph will be
on his way back with the shadow of the trium-
phant mortgage preceding him and the sign of
the three balls gleaming in his wake.

Replying to a request that the btate
Parliament should be summoned to deal with a
request for more Wages Boards, John Weary un-
intentionally threw mud at his own action in
opposing the Referendum. Said he : “ What is
the use of such Boards if their determinations are
not obeyed?” Exactly! What is the use of
continuing to place industrial regulation in such
feeble, flaccid hands when a competent and
powerful authority like the Commonwealth
Parliament is willing to take over the job?
As forecasted months ago, the Victorian
Government finds itself stumped for land for the
new settlers, and is trying to induce would-be
irrigationists to take up Mallee and other “dry-
farming” blocks. El wood Mead’s settlers are
coming in steadily, and he and his Water Supply
Board are doing the best they can for the trickle;
but they can’t acquire land “ on their own ’’- they
must go to the Closer Settlement Board. And that
body finds the compulsory purchase powers hope-
lessly ineffective, as the House of Fat Landlords,
no doubt, intended they should be.
Willie Watt babbled the other day about
its “being 100 to 1 on the Wages Boards as
against the Arbitration Court.” and that “ the
State Parliament could be called together at any
time and could create 40 or 50 Boards in two
hours.” Whereupon one of the workers’ women
organisers reminded Watt that Boards for a dozen
trades had already been asked for—in some cases
repeatedlv—wherefore she invited him and Weary
to call Parliament together at once and get busy.
Then Watt’s silence was like the stillness of Ice-
berg Irvine, who was the last man to call Parlia-
ment to session in haste. But he didn’t do it to
create Wages Boards. Scarcely !
The recently-formed M.L. Labor party
has started to cut its teeth, and is going to try
their edges at the municipal elections this month
in Auckland, Wellington, Christchurch, and a
number of smaller centres. “Tommy” Taylor,
the “independent” member in Parliament for
Christchurch North, has signed the party pledge,
and is contesting Christchurch city mayoralty.
The other day the Const’toosh’n was al-
most fractured, the marriage-tie rent asunder,
and Capital hounded out of the land because an
inoffensive Scotchman named Carmichael pro-
posed to have a yarn with an old pal of his named
Fuller. One is the departing, the in-
coming, sub-excellency of Victoria, and Carmike
proposed to stay till his successor arrived and give
him a few points on local customs and manners.
But officialdom was aghast; the thing was with-
out Precedent. It was almost as bad as two
claimants to one throne colliding in one back yard.
So Carmichael is going to loiter about in Adelaide
until Fuller arrives, when the pair may yarn and
whisky until the boat leaves. One of the things
that Fisher might explain to the “ men in Down-
ing-street, ” when he speaks with them at Corona-
tion time, is that these absurd State Governors
simply don’t count, and that Australia really
thinks far less of a Mother that imposes them
upon it than it would if she recognised their
superfluousness and authorised the Chief Justice
to do the legal part of the business. The other
part doesn’t want doing.
A vigorous No-No canvas is going on
among the wheat-farmers in way-out N. S. Wales.
Farmers and settlers 5 delegates are telling
them that the proposals are “against their best
interests.” The delegates don’t explain why or
how—which is probably just as well. It might
be awkward, for instance, if the delegates started
to explain what a beautiful thing it would be for
the farmers to have a Trust in the implement
trade, so that they would have the privilege of
paying £lOO for harvesters and £45 for ploughs.
From that great patter comedian, Alfred
Deakin, M.P.:—
It is quite possible that the decision (on the Referenda)
would be given by only a minority of those entitled to
vote. The best antidote to this political lassitude which
my own experience enables me to offer is the presenta-
tion on every occasion of the positive, progressive pro-
gramme of the Liberal party, which comprises measures
adequate to the completion of our Federal Constitution
by the addition of all the powers essential to give it
effective national control of the issues which are sup-
posed to warrant the present inapplicable, injurious and
anti-Federal amendments.
After getting your breath you are entitled to sit
down and ask'where is the “positive, progressive
programme of the Liberal party ” ? The bulk of
the Deakin orators clamor against the Referenda,
but they are as silent about the details of their
amazing Liberal programme as the most taciturn
oyster that ever hung on to its rock at Stewart
Island.
In almost every neighborhood you will find
someone who, during the summer months, had a
severe attack of colic or English cholera, and who
had been put to the most severe suffering before
medicine could be procured or a doctor summoned.
A reliable remedy for this complaint should be
kept at hand. The risk is too great for anyone to
take. Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea
Remedy has undoubtedly saved the lives of niore
people and saved more pain and suffering than
anv other medicine in use. It can always be
depended upon for this ailment.*
Strange tales are related of the Willunga
railway poll mS.A. It is said that a dozen men
or more who have long been lying comfortably at
rest under flattering gravestones m Brighton
cemetery, left their beds to vote against giving 1
guarantee that the State demanded before it
would build the line. But even then the Ayes
won by a narrow margin.
Alfred Deakin, who used to be an Aus-
tralian Nationalist, says that “powers should not
be given unless they are to be used. lhe las-
manian Government has power to legislate for
the creation of wages boards, and doesn t use it;
yet Deakin doesn’t want the power taken from
the Tasmanian Government apparently. Vic-
toria has power to make laws for the gaoling of
the baby-murderers who sell rotten or reduced
milk : but it simply sits back and allows such men
to go on putting 4£d. into the poor box. Queens-
land has power to see that the sugar-farmer gets a
decent price for his raw sugar. The power isnt
used ; yet Deakin hasn’t said a word about taking
it away. Still, because the democratic Australian
Government wants more power, Deakin is beg-
ging Australia not to give itself a free hand,
because it mayn’t want to use every ounce of its
new authority. It sounds like bluff. It has the
appearance of bluff. It is bluff.
Replying to Hughes’s remark that the
Commonwealth Government won t necessarily
use all the powers it asks for, Alfred Deakin says
that power shouldn’t be given if it isn t exercised.
On the same principle, of course, power that
isn’t exercised should be withdrawn or resigned.
Under these circumstances The Bulletin is
waiting to hear that A. Deakin, barrister-at-law,
has applied to be disbarred. Alfred has power to
practise and doesn’t practise, wherefore on his
own showing the power to practise should be
taken from him.
Owing to want of foresight on the part of
the great railway expert Tait, Victoria is suffer-
ing from such a shortage of railways trucks that a
scarcity of coal is threatened in Melbourne, and
there is an actual firewood famine. Newport
workshops are said to be building vehicles at top
speed • and now Weary and co. talk of cabling an
order for 1000 trucks. If there must be importa-
tions, what’s wrong with dropping a word in the
rio-ht place that Victoria wants a heap of railway-
barrows and hasn’t enough men to make them as
fast as it wants them ?

Bv Deakin-ites and such that it

SPORTING
NOTION
The cheerful offer of Clarence Weber to meet
champion slugger Jack Johnson under Greek pancratium
rules has raised a cloud of dust. The excitement over
the Referenda is a trifle compared with it. Under pan-
cratium rules, both men are free to hit or wrestle ; so, in
the long run, Weber’s offer boils down to a proposal to
take Johnson on for a rough-and-tumble to a finish. And
that would not bean edifying spectacle—in fact, it would
be such a brutal exhibition that even the somewhat gory
United States would probably prohibit it. Anyhow, it
would if it were a civilised country. But if a wrestler were
allowed to wrestle only, and the boxer were restricted to
punching, a struggle would throw some light on a dark
question. The trouble is that so much depends upon the
men. Whether boxing or wrestling is the better method
of defence will never be settled. In any case, a contest
of the sort would be unsatisfactory, as the boxer would
wear gloves, but there would be no gloves in a strangle
hold. So to give any true index it would be necessary
for the boxer to wear gloves that weighed about loz.
each. And that again would be descending to brutality.
So, apparently, there is no satisfaction to be got out of
the matter.
As 10,000 argumentative bar-breasters and
sundry newspapers have already pointed out, boxing
as practised is an artificial method of fighting. When
one meets a footpad on a lonely road there is no time-
keeper and no referee to disqualify for hitting below the
belt. But the strongest footpad caught suddenly round
the knees and brought down on the back of his head is out
for keeps. A heavy swing right on the jaw from any man
of ordinary sense would probably have the same soporific
effect. Considering both methods of defence from the
purely utilitarian standpoint, the wrestler has the
advantage in that his art stands to him in the last stage—
that of “ holts.” A boxer who is agile enough to pre-
vent the wrestler getting a hold would, of course, win ;
but in that case it is his speed that saves him and downs
the other fellow ; wherefore the real art of self-defence is
ability to run and side-step. So what’s wrong with teaching
the rising generation to run? Agility, more agility, and
still more agility is what is wanted. When advancing
years make it impossible to show the agility, then it’s
time to take on jiu-jitsu.
Mick Nathan, ex-lightweight boxer, blew into
controversy and told how he had met a gentleman named
Duncan Ross, who, though he ought to have been Scotch-
byterian, was Yankee, in a boxer v. wrestler contest, and
knocked him out in a few minutes. Ross was allowed to
hit as well as wrestle, but apparently didn’t know how.
So the boxer got the best of it. Ross, who was beaten,
weighed 14st., while the boxer scaled only lOst. Which
meant that boxer Nathan undoubtedly had more agility,
and was able to keep out of the way of his heavier adver-
sary. It was very interesting ; but it threw no light on
what would have happened had Nathan met a wrestler of
his own weight and nimbleness.
* * * *
The aftermath of the ladies’ sculling match.
Miss Ryan was accused of having used a sliding seat,
whereas the conditions stipulated that the race should
be rowed with fixed seats. To the accusation, Peter
Kemp, who trained Miss Ryan, gave an indignant denial.
But his explanation of how the “mistake” occurred
bangs Banagher. Miss Ryan wore Canadian knickers,
and the back part of them was made of leather,
which had been polished and made slippery by
treatment with blacklead, or something of that sort.
On to the seat of the skiff was screwed a board, which
bad likewise been made slippery. And on this the lady
slid just as easily as if she had been in a sliding seat;
nevertheless, it is tri v ' ~.iantly claimed that the articles
were faithfully adhered to. The only result of the ex-
planation is to enounce the glory of Miss Lewis’s win,
for, although the articles may have been kept in the
letter, there is a grave doubt whether they weren’t badly
fractured in the spirit.
* * * *
Sydney First Grade cricket’s 10th round came
to an end on Saturday and provided some surprises.
The struggle between the leading clubs, Redfern and
Gordon, was the hub of public attention. On the pre-
vious Saturday Gordon had piled up 237, and Redfern
had accumulated 28 for the loss of one wicket. On this
Saturday Redfern struck trouble in the shape of a long
succession of single-figure scores ; and although Emery,
who carried his bat, made a great stand, his solid 83
could not save his side, which disappeared for 199, leaving
a margin of 38 in favor of Gordon on the first innings. At
one time Redfern had seven wickets down for only 83, and
it looked like a rot and a rout.. But Emery then put in an
appearance, and aided by Kerim, of Palestine, who made
34, the outlook lost some of its horror. All the same,
Redfern, like the turtle, got into the soup at the last.
Three centuries lent a glory to the cricketers’
afternoon sunlight. The owners were Norman Deane
(North Sydney), who piled up 162 ; P. Newton (Padding-
ton), who carried his bat for 123, and then saw his side
make only a draw'of it; and R. B. Minnett (University),
who smacked up 123 also.
Redfern’s downfall placed Gordon at the top of
the poll with 14 competition points to Redfern’s 12, Uni.
running third with 11. After that, single figures above
and below zero, except in the case of Middle Harbor,
which has distinguished itself by getting 10 behind
nothing. North Sydney downed Central Oumbeiland
with a margin of 188 runs. Petersham, having totted up
286, was disgusted to find that Glebe managed to
keep the evil hour off long enough to make a draw' of it
with 233 for eight w'ickets. Which was g>ood generalship,
anyhow. Burwood overwhelmed Waverley by 68 runs —204
to 272. Paddington, with its little 255, never had a hope
against University, which lashed up 436, leaving 118 to
sp ire. The defeat of Sydney, which wasanticipat* d, was
made real by Middle Harbor scoring 196 to Sydney’s 156.
Strange how some people can perform when the day of
grace has fled ! Sydney, back at the sticks in the second
innings, which did not affect, the match, promptly smote
up 163 (seven more than it made in the first innings) for
the loss of five w'ickets. But life was ever thus I
* * * *
“J.G.”: Re curious ways of getting out at
cricket (B. 23/3/T1), Years ago, when I fancied I could
play well enough to be in the Aus. XI, I w'as batting at
Ballarat. Getting a loose ball from the local Spofforth,
I banged it with the intention of smashing the boundary
fence. The hall, however, made straight for the umpire
at leg and hit the bat he was holding, then struck square
leg, and rebounded on to the wicket, and finally was
caught by point. Meanwhile, I was streaking for the
other end, and both my partner and I were out of the
crease when the ball hit the wicket. In a few minutes a
terrific dispute was raging as to whether it was a case of
run out or caught out, or whether the ball was dead after
it hit the umpire, so that neither of us were out. A
policeman settled it —I forget how.
* * * *
The Australasian Lawn Tennis Association finds
itself very comfortable, thank you. The assets stand at
£435, with no liabilities; and the visit of the over-seas
notabilities towards the end of the year, when the Davis
Cup will be lost and won, will bring more grist to
the mill. The N.S.W. Association, however, is in
a bad plight. The liabilities tot up to the sombre
amount of £357, and, as treasurer T. P. Carr
puts it, the “ Association has no provision for
an overdraft, all the assets being mortgaged.” In other
words, the Association is dead broke. A committee has
been formed to consider ways and means, and the Sydney
Club has handed over to it the right to control the State
Championships, a concession that will bring a few
sovereigns to the Association’s fainting purse. As for
the debenture-holders, they are smiling between out-
bursts of appropriate language.
* * * *
Aubrey Faulkner not only did not return to
South Africa with the other cricketers, but likes this
country so much that he is hard at work looking for land
to take up. The only doubt—it may have been decided
by this time—is whether Vic. or N.S.W. will get the
benefit of his spare moments. The news will be a horrid
jar to Africa. What it will do in England in 1912 without
the backbone of its XI is hard to imagine. Even for its
recent Australian tour, it had to send for Llewellyn,
who has been an English county player for years. As it
seems to be settled that no man shall play in Test matches
for more than one country—as witness Crawford’s idle-
ness during the recent series, though he is now a bona-
fide Australian resident—Faulkrier’s big cricket is pre-
sumably at an end. This is a pity, for if an absolutely
representative XI were being chosen at the present
moment—say the sporting fixture of This World against
the Next —Faulkner would be one of the certainties.
* » * *
The N.S.W. Ladies’ Amateur Swimming As-
sociation, which is horrified at the idea of men spectators
at women’s swimming races, is in holts with the Public
Schools’ Association. The schools had their annual carni-
val last month, and men spectators were present, the
Schools’ Association having followed their usual practice
of years past. Now the Association has warned all affili-
ated clubs that all its members who took part in this
carnival have been suspended, and if any club or clubs
allow any of these immodest persons to race in club
events the whole club will be disqualified. The Wowser-
ess Association, having gone this far, should go farther.
Lavender Bay is one of the Association’s chief centres,
and the swimmers in the baths are in full view of the
male passengers in the passing trains. It is reasonable to
suppose that rude men have at one time or other gazed
at every member of the Association. So the obvious
course to adopt is for the Association to disqualify itself
holus-bolus, and the difficulty will thus promptly end
itself.
. * * *
Probably the best story of the hundreds
that could be told of the lately-deceased Victorian turf
identity, A. F. Bradshaw, is associated with his pro-
prietorship of the Richfield (Vic.) suburban racecourse.
The V.R.O. had raised the minimum amount of stake
money to be raced for per day at a meeting. Bradshaw
got round the difficulty by adding the Yellow Hammer
Stakes to his programme, and appropriating to it about
half the value of the day’s prize money. Only horses
sired by Yellow Hammer (Bradshaw’s own property) were
eligible, and there were only two of them. Bradshaw
owned one and a friend owned the other !
* * * *
Motorists are rejoicing, for the Dunlop Rubber
00. has announced a reduction of 15 per cent, in the
price of motor tyres, which means 3s. in the £. The re-
duction is due to a fall in the price of Para rubber,
which has slumped to 7s. per lb.
* * * *
If Westralia succeeds with the fund now being
raised to send Finlay, the 220yds. breast-stroke champion
of Australia, to the Festival of Empire, Australasia will
be fairly well represented. Champion has left Maoriland
for the hub of the earth, and Hardwick, of N.S.W., is
also afloat. These two will have to decide which of them
is the better man, as only one representative is allowed in
each event. Champion left his Maoriland shore in a
blaze of glory, having covered 880yds. in llmin. 37 4-ssec.,
a feat which convinced the ricketty isles that the local
Champion is The One.
The motor invasion hits in all sorts of curious
ways. For instance, Adelaide Zoo complains bitterly
that, it cannot get enough broken-down horses for its
carnivora to eat since the horse-drawn vehicles have
been so largely superseded. Even the jackals are not yet
educated up to dining off a section of condemned rubber
tyre. Apparently the only thing to be done is to fill the
gap with broken-down chauffeurs and those convicted of
furious driving.
ft * * *
The fact that Douglas Mawson is taking with
him to Antarctica—if he can raise the money to go at all
—a two-seated aeroplane, suggests that the race of
daredevils is not yet extinct. One could understand him
taking some sort of dirigible ; but an aeroplane ! An
aeroplane is at the merey of a mere gust, while a dirigible
can at least run before a storm, although the bulk of the
storms in Antarctica appear to be altogether over the
odds. Moreover, petrol is always liable to freeze in that
awful temperature, or, rather, lack of it. And the
slightest stoppage of the engine means death to that
aeroplane’s reckless crew, for if the fall did not prove
fatal the cold would, unless they escaped scot free. The
aeroplane is to have a petrol capacity equal to 180 miles
—equal to 90 miles out and back again ; and in a somewhat
lonely place like Antarctica a fellow can get into a lot of
trouble in 90 miles. Mawson certainly ought to take a
dirigible on which to do the rescue act in the almost
certain event of the aeroplane breaking down. And,
anyhow, is the old Pole worth it all, or worth even half of
it all?
* * * *
George Gray’s natural preference for making
his losing hazards off the red rather than off the white
ball is likely to rationalise billiards still further by bring-
ing about an alteration in the score values of hazards
and cannons. Nobody knows why a cannon or a scoring
transaction with the opponent’s ball is worth only two,
whilst a hazard with the red is worth three. There
never was a reason for the discrepancy, except that it
had existed for ages. Next time the rules of billiards are
amended, the cannon will probably be raised to three in
value, and the hazard with the red reduced to two. Or
if this is left at three, the white hazard will be raised to
its level. Why not?
* * » *
The Hawkesbury autumn meeting indicated
that the ancient club has weathered the glacial age that
descended on it after the smash of the boom, and is re-
viving rapidly. Saturday last saw probably a record
crowd on the course, and some excellent racing. The
club is blessed with about the best racecourse manager in
Australia, Mr. C. S. Guest. The proceedings were marred
by one accident, in the Two-Yt-ar-Old Handicap. Wool
(what a name for a horse !) fell and smashed his rider’s
collarbone. No horses were injured.
Followers of form concentrated a lot of atten-
tion on the Autumn Handicap, for every one of the nine
starters is likely to be a trier in the Doncaster and Syd-
ney Cup, or one at least of these two classic and recently
re-gilded plums. Flavinius and Sandbath took the popu-
lar eye most at 9 to 2 and 3to 1 against. Apple Pie,
which, with Eric, had gone out at sevens, immediately
dashed to the front, and went like the wind, with Some-
thing Irish and Mil in hot pursuit. The two favorites
were far to the rear, but the second-last horse of the
bunch was Eric. At the straight, Flavinius was running
second, but at the half distance the field, coming with a
tremendous rush, closed right up. Then Eric and
Adagio, who had hitherto chased the others along, came
round on the outside with a great sweep, passed the
bunch, and dashed into first and second places in that
order. Sandbath was third. Eric won by half a length,
and Adagio beat Sandbath by the same distance ; so it
was a fairly close thing, and the Scriptures were fulfilled
by the last being first.
* * * +
The latest effort of “conscience’’ beats all
records. Recently the record-breaker with a load on his
mind sent along £5 to a Melbourne bookmaker, with a
note to the effect that Ikey Moses had overpaid this
amount. Ikey’s friends hope that the double shock will
not prove fatal. It is an article of most religions that to
take down a bookmaker is no sin, and one or two, rather
more sensible than the rest, regard it as a virtue. This
fellow is evidently a degenerate.
The scratching of Blairgonr for the Doncaster
Handicap last week was one of the pills which should
remove an inclination on the part of the public to try to
make money by backing gee-gees. The brute was brought
to Sydney for auction sale, and the new owner, after
careful inspection, decided that he was not fit to run ih
the race, and accordingly scratched him. Blairgour has
been the popular fancy for months, and is now one leg of
innumerable doubles and the subject of a fearsome crowd
of straight-out bets at long odds. The introduction of
the Tote at least would save the fool public from this sort
of spoliation.
* • * *
A feature at the Sydney Show is the Russell
aerial colt-catcher. From a post in the centre of the
ring an arm rotates so as to cover any portion of it. The
breaker is located in a sling depending from this arm,
and sweeping round he catches the colts from above,
without the danger that he would have to face in catch-
ing them from the ground. The man’s superiority is so
thoroughly demonstrated that the horse seems to give in
at once, and is captured without anv ferocious struggle
to damage the animal’s nerves and ruin his temper. Also
it accustoms the neddies to being handled in slings for
transhipment on to steamers, railway-boxes and so forth ;
and altogether it seems to be a big improvement on the
old and somewhat brutal style of doing things.
* * * *
The daily papers announce that the ancient and
dishonorable profession of “ whispering ” is to be abolished
at Randwick (Sydney) by some ground alterations. But
there is nothing in it. “Whispering” will go on merrily
as long as mugs attend races ; and the supply of mugs
seems like the mercy of God—inexhaustible and infinite.
“ Whispering,” be it known, is a humorous arrangement
by which a number of more or less disreputable small
boys and unshaven youths make a gullible, public keep
them for nothing. The “whisperer” is supposed
to have inside knowledge born of companion-
ship with the jockeys and stable-boys, and he im-
parts it on the understanding that, if the tip
comes off, he will get a small cut of the sugar-plums
won from theinnocent, bookmaker. And he always does.
If there are six horses in a field of 20 that have a reason-
able show, he tips the whole six to six separate mugs,
giving each mug a different horse. And whichever one
has backed the winner, the “whisperer” sticks to like a
guilty conscience. It must take some smartness to keep
the six mugs in view ; but, somehow, the “whisperer"
detective system seems to be infallible, as the mug never
escapes paying his toll. The new arrangements will
certainly have one good effect. In future the public
will not be able to surround the jockeys as they go to
mount, and the world will be saved the disgraceful exhi-
bition of smartly-dressed females ogling and ear-wigging
the disgusted horsemen for tips as they file to the course.
* * * *
Sydney Metropolitan Rugby Union is not dead,
nor sleeping; but it is going to have a very quiet year
indeed. Economy is the order of the day, and it is going
to be of a very stark nature. Players will not be
insured, unless they pay their own premiums, or the
clubs do it for them ; guernseys will not be supplied free
as of yore : and even the number of clubs is to be re-
duced, Sydney District being wiped off the football map.
The only outside event will be the match with Queens-
land, and though there is some talk of a Maoriland team
coming over towards the end of the season, no one
expects to see it. Meanwhile, the selectors are busy
picking and choosing, or finding men to fill the gaps in
the ranks. The gaps appear to he unusually large.
The Rugby League, on the other hand, is put-
ting forward a most alluring programme, and one that is
expected to knock the recent successful season into a
cocked hat. Maoriland and Queensland will both call in
and make for sport and big gates ; but what tickles the
players’ palates most is that gorgeous trip to Britain to
play the Northern Union. The Kangaroos will number
26, and will be picked from the players who show up best
between the start of the competitions, on the last Satur-
day of this month, and August 5, when they will board
the steamer. The chief danger ahead of the League just
now is that disastrous complaint known as swelled head.
By that sin fell the angels. True, our Leaguers are not
all angels.
Apparently, the Soccer people found that last
year they had spread themselves out rather thinly;
hence four whole divisions of the metropolis are wiped
out and joined to others, so that the number of First
Grade teams drops from 12 to eight. This would not
appear to be a slump, but due merely to an honest
endeavor to consolidate the game by having larger and
wealthier clubs. Football is, or.rather was, threatening
to become top-heavy in Sydney.
Laxo-Tonic Pills,
