The Brisbane premises of Messrs. Pike Brothers,
Limited, Gentlemen’s Complete Outfitters, have re-
cently seen great changes. To successfully cope
with their growing trade another storey has been
added, the frontage enlarged and improved, and
many modern conveniences have been applied for
the furtherance of their customers’ needs. An elec-
tric lift, driven by a newly-installed Gas Suction
Plant, has been provided. The up-to-date workrooms
are amongst the finest in the Commonwealth, and
are a tribute to the management’s foresight. New
departments have been added and the existing ones
enlarged, and, where possible, improved. A fully-
equipped Juvenile Clothing Department and a Ladies’
Ready-to-wear Coat and Skirt Department are among
the new departures. Only the best of everything is
offered at the keenest prices.*
Saturday was a sweltering day in Sydney, and
the people who were not seeking reputation at the noble
game of’oofball felt sorrv for the grimy, sweating, half-
roasted footballers who were playing their practice
matches. But it was a splendid day for shifting surplus
tissue, and the way those ’bailers left their original
dimensions was very gratifying. The whole eight clubs
of the League were abroad, and put in a strenuous after-
noon. The possibilities of getting into the English-
touring team spurred them on tCMioble deeds.
The Australian League footballers are going to
boom in Sydney this year. H. R. Denison, who started
the game in VVestralia 27 years ago, has again turned his
attention to it, and with his aid the League has secured
the freehold of the old Rosebery Park Racecourse, which
is an ideal ground for the purpose. But the body is
about to sustain a severe loss t hrough the departure of
J. J. Virgo, who made the Y.M.C.A. a collection
of manly young fellows and a credit to any city.
Virgo goes to London to be a large dog in the Y.M.C.A.
in that wintry land, and threatens to establish the Aus-
tralian game there. And Virgo is a man who has a knack
of keeping his promises. For years he has been vice-
president of the League, and it will miss him.
*,* * *
The action of the Ladies’ Swimming Associa-
tion moved the secretary of the Public Schools body to
do a cruel thing. In his mirth he told the truth, and
pointed out with barbaric frankness that “ ladies over a
certain age never swim,” because mostly they dare not be
seen in swimming' costume. Also, he pointed out that the
action of the L.S.A. is merely the first act in a tragedy of
suicide, for if the Ladies will not admit to membership an v
school girl who takes part in the schools carnivals, it will
practically have to import all new members* The Public
Schools body has done most valuable work, and, through
it, thousands and thousands of girls have been taught
and encouraged to swim, with the result that they are of
finer type and better development than the frumps "’bo
had not the advantage of swimming In early girlhood.
Now the Ladies’ Swimming Association is squirming, it
never expected such beastly straight talk from so mild-
mannered a man as secretary Lappin.
**. * *
Old Dick Coombes, off to the Coronation blow-
out as manager of the Australasian Athletic Teatn, has
had more to contend with during the last few weeks than
in all the rest of the 28 years he has beeh mixed up with
amateur athletics. Banquets, presentations, stnoke con-
certs and such like have fallen on the quiet, unassuming
manager, until his sleep is haunted with nightmares
of large bottles and floods of oily words. Coombes owns
up to having won 160 prizes at sport, and Australia owes
much to his sound knowledge of the games he has taken
an interest in.
* * * *
Lacrosse, which promises big things in Sydney
this winter, is better off in cash and members than ever
before in its 28 years’history. Three new cluhs will be
added to the first-grade competition, and two extra'
clubs will join in the second-grade struggle. Inter-State
teams will also liven the game up considerably, and,
generally, lacrosse players are beginning to regard them-
selves as among the salt of the earth. The real question
is where all these clubs and increased numbers are to find
biffing grounds. Looks as though sundry streets will
have to be closed to traffic to give room for sport.
* * * *
Adelong (N.S.W.) saw a horrible sight last
week, when the most revolting “contest" on re-
cord earned its promoters the heaviest sentence
possible for cruelty to animals. A. local reptile-
fancier challenged Australia to produce a snake with
more venom to the square inch than his pet “ tiger,” and
put up £2O to show his challenge was genuine. A
Cooma man heard the challenge, and rushed the
offer with a 6ft. Murray coppa, or some such reptile.
Rabbits were introduced as the third party to the argu-
ment. It was a one-snake affair, as the “ tiger’s” bite
killed the rabbit in 2£ minutes, while the bite of Kelly’s
reptile took four hours to prove fatal. This sort of thing
deserves attention at the hands of the police.
* * * *
Lester, who is billed to stoush Bill Lang in
Sydney, is not highly regarded by competent judges.
Though boomed by Tahmmy Burns, his methods are
described as more pyrotechnic than pugilistic. Re*
eently he had some bouts with an ex-policeman at
Tacoma (U.S.A.), and did no serious damage. The big
fellow has one virtue, however. He takes punishment,
with the equanimity of a cab-horse.

“Thomas Spook”: Spotswood (Vic.)
owns a little weatherboard chapel which believes
in the sweet uses of advertisement. Over its
entrance, in huge silver letters on a background of
blue, is painted the notice : “Prepare to Meet
Thy God ! ” I saw this notice through a railway-
carriage window; and, being pressed for time, I
could not alight and examine the other notices
which I feel sure must be there. For instance :
“ The best preparatory establishment in Victoria.
Only the best instructors kept. No. of passes for
1910, 365J —minimum fee, 3d. a week. Principal,
Rev. William Traybitt, D.D. Read Our Refer-
ences.”
A few days ago passengers on the Mil-
dura (Vic.) train were amazed at the sight of a
herd of about 50 deer close to the line between
Otiryo and Birchip. This is away up in the Nor’-
West, on the fringe of the rnallee, and a couple
of hundred miles or so from where any deer were
supposed to be. How they got there is a mystery.
There are herds of them about the coastal dis-
tricts of Gippsland—but that is 250 miles off.
Previously in good seasons they have been
known to make small incursions inland ; and
apparently the extra-good season has enticed
them further afield than they ever got before.

THERE are Pure Food Laws and Pure
Beverage Laws for the protection of
the people of Australia to-day.
Chateau Tanunda Brandy is distilled
under the direct control of the Federal
Government, and from the time the
grape juice leaves the presses until the
Brandy is thoroughly matured the Com-
monwealth officers watch every process
IN YOUR INTEREST.
The presence of that u G.R.” label on
the bottle means that your Government
guarantees the contents to be distilled
WHOLLY FROM GRAPE WINE by
a pot still, and that the Brandy has been
matured by storage in wood for a period
of not less than two years.
In no other part of the world is Brandy
made under conditions that offer such
complete protection to the consumer.
Ask for Chateau Tanunda—the pure
Australian Standard Brandy.

taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.

THE EX-CONVICT’S REVENGE.
Not a breath of wind did ruffle the trees
And the stars shone clear and bright.
As a squire reclined in his easy-chair
On his varandah one summer night,
When before him appeared a ragget tramp
Whose cheeks were worn and pale,
A poor disgarded innocent man
H e had sent for five years to gaol;
We meet at last, exclaimed the tattered wretch,
I’ve watched and waited all Day,
And he fastened upon his enemy’s throat
Like a tiger upon its prey.
The victim writhed convulsively
In the grip of the convict grim,
When the servants appeared upon the scene
He had choked the life out of him.
The miscreant slouched among the trees
In horror and guilty despair.
While with glaring eyes and contorted brow
The squire lie dead in his chair.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CONTRIBUTORS.
W.M.K.: Local interest only; the B. wants things
that will be looked at with equal interest in Thursday
Island and Fremantle. . . .
“Singemoine” : According to Sydney
Evening News, “ a census-collector asked a Do-
main dosser, ‘What is your religion? ’ ‘Scotch,’
he replied, and went to sleep again.” What mag-
nificent frankness ! We all of us have two re-
ligions : (1) the real (“Scotch,” beer, rum, or
girls, as the case may he); (2) the bogus (Wowser-
ism. Primitive Methodism, Imperialism or what
not). One of the things that will stamp the 1911
census on my memory for all time is the fact that
the authorities had to go, Diogenes-like, with a
lantern, to a damp, wind-swept cave, to find the
only really honest man in the community.
Thus Frank Dilnot, in London Daily
Mail, on William Archer Redmond (who
reaches Australia in a day or two), his maiden
speech, and the Redmond family generally :
He looked indeed the son of his father as he sat there
frowning with nervousness with a bundle of notes in his
hand, crossing and uncrossing his legs as he waited for
the opportunity which was so long in coining. He has
his father’s bold, hawklike nose, his square jaw, his
determined mouth, and there is the Irish Leader’s ex-
pression in his eyes. The resemblance would be pheno-
menal were it not that in place of the grey hair, thinning
rapidly, of his father, he has a luxuriant mass of dark
hair. When the Speaker at last called on him he rose
amid enthusiastic cheers from the Nationalists and
plunged at once into his facts and arguments. He
spoke fluently, but with just a trace of awkwardness,
which will disappear when lie becomes accustomed to his
environment. His voice is a strange blend of his father’s
and his uncle’s. Mr. John Redmond had a place at the
end of the bench in the comparative obscurity of the gal-
lery ; and one could imagine his emotions as he leaned for-
ward to lose no word of his son’s first speech. Mr. William
Redmond came hurrying in from the Lobby and stood at
the Bar listening to the young man with an expression of
happy reflectiveness. As to the members in general,
they divided their attention between father, uncle and
nephew. Everybody had temporarily forgotten Home
Rule, and for once in a way politics were far from the
minds of all.
Now it is , the Premier of France who is
introducing legislation to prevent trade mono-
polies,' the bossefi of the local alcohol trade being
suspected of a scheme to corner the market.
From everywhere in these days we hear of the
wicked Socialists interfering with Private Enter-
prise ; and no wonder the representative stout
gentleman pauses between his third and fourth
muffin at breakfast to remark that things are
coming to a pretty pass.
By one of those coincidences which will
insist on occurring, London Punch published re-
cently a cartoon of Balfour standing by the grave
of Preferential Trade, on the very day that his
younger brother, Eustace, died. Editor Seaman
expressed his regrets through the daily press, and
showed that the cartoon had been decided on 10
days before. Eustace was manager of the vast
Grosvenor estate, in London. The difficulty for
PUNCH would have been more awkward if he had
died the day before publication. It would have
been a very big thing to have suppressed the
number \ yet .su.ch things have been done—-a whole
edition of “ Whitaker’s Almanac ” was once sacri-
ficed because of a serious error.

ONCE=A-WEEK PIANO TALKS
One of the essentials in the construction of high-grade pianos is carefully-
selected and thoroughly-seasoned timber. For this purpose the services of
timber specialists are required. Our specialists examine every piece, accepting
only perfect planks.
DRIED and SEASONED timber are not synonymous terms. Timber
may be dried rapidly at the expense of developing cracks, but that is not
SEASONED ; because the sap is forced out and the timber is left porous
instead of the particles drawing together and consolidating, thus rendering it
subject to expansion or contraction by every change of temperature.
The timber used in BEALE High-Grade PIANOS is first of all
matured for years in the open air, protected from the sun and rain, and is then
finished gradually on scientific principles in patented seasoning kilns. By this
means it is properly SEASONED, and will remain straight and fiat for all time.
The Hon. Sir Arthur Rutledge, K.C.M.G., ex-Attorney-General of
Queensland, on visiting our factories, said: —-
“That which interested me most, as I thoroughly understand the subject, is the apparatus
and system for seasoning timber. Not merely is the timber exposed to the air, under cover, for
years, but, above all, the perfect method of uniform extraction of the sap without injuring the fibres
will enable the wood to resist all climatic influences. It is the only perfect system I have met with
in Australia, and 1 was not aware until to-day that it existed amongst us. Messrs. Beale and Go.
are enabled to use in Australia patents for timber seasoning which are elsewhere mutually exclusive.
Hence the complete success.”
Further, timber to be used in a tropical climate should be SEASONED
in a tropical climate. It is not to be expected that timber seasoned in, say,
Germany (where for months in the year there are heavy rains, snow, ice and
bitter cold), could withstand the high temperatures of Australia as well as
timber seasoned in this climate.
But in addition to all this, the cabinetwork of BEALE High-Grade
PIANOS is constructed on a patented system of cross-plies, which we have
already described, and which of itself is sufficient to render warping or crack-
ing impossible in any climate.
You are cordially invited to call and inspect the New Models of BEALE High-Grade PIANOS.
They are of excellent workmanship, the materials used throughoutare of high quality, and the tone is delightful.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Woomelang” re the quality of Victorian tweeds.
More than 30 years ago Albion (Vic.) productions
were being sold in Collins-street as best West-of-
England, and prices on that basis were paid by the
“nothing colonial” crowd. The same crowd is not
yet any wiser so long as the hat-band *or boot-tag
or trouser-button is branded “Made in England,” or
some other place that isn’t Australia. The Union
Mill at Geelong was started with the view of crush-
ing the Victorian industry, and for some years after
its foundation turned out shoddy, which was offered
by the “no Australia” crowd whenever Geelong
tweed vvas asked for. But the other three mills
wore this “no Australia” prejudice down, whereupon
the Union (now Collins) saw the error of its ways,
and the profit to be made by turning out the genuine
article. Since that change came over it, it has helped
to show what can be done by Australians in Aus-
tralia. As to there being anything wrong with the
Victorian article, the fact that for many months
past all the 10 Victorian mills have been working
overtime, and that a new one has just started in
John Weary’s town (Warrnambool), makes it look
as if the public doesn’t think there’s anything
wrong, anyhow.
* * * *
“0.K.”: Talking of the baked wheat industry
lately started in Melbourne, one point was over-
looked in a Bulletin “Plain English.” The food-
stuff in question—“Crispies” by name—is produced
by machinery made almost entirely in Australia;
and this fact, that special machinery is needed, may
explain why the manufacture of fancy wheats has
not previously been undertaken hereabouts. No
doubt also the Yankee firms’ profuse advertising has
helped to scare opposition away; for the retailer not
unnaturally prefers to handle goods whose alleged
virtues are shrieked into the public’s ear half a
dozen times a day. In Melbourne, at any rate, one
has to bully the retailer into supplying an order for
the locally manufactured wheat. True, it is con-
sideraby cheaper than the well-advertised foreign
brands; but as that probably means less profit for
the grocer, it doesn’t make him any the more eager to
push it. Still, he’s a reasonable person, as a rule
accustomed to weighing things, and when some of
his customers demand local wheatmeats in preference
to imported ditto, he will begin to speak kindly
about the goods.
* * * *
Luck has lately stuck doggedly to one Hansen, of
New South Wales. In a ballot for a Bananaland
block he won, and took possession of the little piece
of Australia. But a birthday-book put the show
away—-Hansen wasn’t of age. The matter got to the
ears of the Lands Office, and Hansen had to quit.
Then the land was thrown open again; but it hap-
pened that the matter was hung up just long enough
to let him have his 21st birthday. He accordingly
dropped in another application, and, winning in that
too, hastened back to the old love.
“Te Babarantiko”: Most people picture the South
Sea Island trader as a bold, bad man, something
a la cowboy of the Deadwood Dick type, ever ready
with revolver and rifle. The present-day trader
isn’t anything of the kind, sad to say. Circum-
stances are very much against him. The chief cir-
cumstance is himself. There are too many of him
in all Pacific Islands groups, and more of him in
the New Hebrides than elsewhere. In a district
where two traders would make a comfortable living
(not amass enough wealth to be burdensome, mind
you), one almost invariably finds from three to five.
The result is fierce jealousy, lying and belittling of
each other to the natives, and resorting to tricks so
mean and paltry one wonders if they really are
white men, after all, despite their color. That they
are under the natives’ thumb is, therefore, inevit-
able—the nigger is boss all right. For any white
trader to offend the noble native seems death as far
as his business is concerned—he’d be tabooed right
away. Instead of combining to stop the niggers’ in-
solence, the trader is apt to run to the Resident
Commissioner for redress against grievances entirely
of his own making, the remedy for which lies in his
own hands, and which no sane man would worry any
Government with.
ANY JAMES TO ANY JOHN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John, ’tis true charity surpasseth knowl’ge, know-
l’ge is science, cultiv. purity of mind. Your bro.
James. — From “Personal and Missing Friends” col.
of daily paper.
John, I must own that I received your letter
And read your most unbrotherly appeal:
You seem quite to forget you are my debtor
And owe me—well, I’ve lent you a great deal.
But let that pass; I fear I shall not see it;
These money-matters cause me only pain ;
If the Lord wills, I lose it—then so be it:
I beg you’ll not refer to it again!
But, John, I have good counsel now to send you—
Advice worth thousands, if it be applied ;
Was I not ever ready to befriend you ?
Did I not do so when our mother died ?
You used to think my zeal for eliurch-work
“funny”—
It would surprise you what the Lord has done I
The reason 1 inherited the money
Was just because I was a model son.
You write that Charity surpasseth Knowledge ;
No doubt, but what are either to a church f
You can’t blame me — l kept you on at college,
Some brothers would have left you in the lurch!
You never go to church—l’ve heard you say so ;
Too much to wine and women you’re inclined—
Oh, shocking! What is worse, you never pray, so
That proves to me you have a godless mind.
You hint that this time you are in great trouble;
“A hole,” you say, and add “my cash is short —
By great bad luck I barely missed a double
Which would have made me. . . Oh, this
wicked sport
Will be your ruin, John! Why will you perish?
If you could be like me, John, you would find
A higher and a holier hope to cherish,
And cultivate true purity of mind.
You know I’ve always tried to be a Beacon—•
A Better Influence—to your wild youth;
It is, of course, the province of a deacon
Whose life is dedicated to the Truth—
Ah, blessed Truth ! I wish, John, that you knew it—
The living Word that comforts and sustains
In this dark Vale, and helps, as we go through it,
To lift us from all thought of earthly gains!
I cannot —as a churchman and a draper—
Assist you in your wicked course of sin.
Suppose the story got into the paper?
My deaeonship would not be worth a pin!
No, .John! I bear a spotless reputation
While you, it seems, have stooped almost to crime;
I cast you off! although you’re a relation,
For sacrifice, if painful, is sublime.
1 fear your backsliding will bring disaster
Upon you and your family affairs,
Yet /, as a meek follower of the Master,
Will not forget you in my humble prayers.
Oh, let this be a source of consolation '
And like a fire, or wind, your hard heart search—
A brother with a blemished reputation
Is prayed for by a deacon of the church!
God bless you, John! But, ere this letter’s ended,
I must express once more my deep, deep pain
At finding that a brother I befriended,
And lent a hundred pounds to, asks again l
But let it go: lam content to lose it—
Thy Will, O Lord! .. . I Here our connection ends,
For I can plainly see, John, you abuse it:
Henceforth count me among your Missing Friends.
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

GOVERNMENT NOTICES.
fgd
Census of 1911.
ATTENTION is drawn to the fact that under the provisions of the Census and Statistics Act ITOR, and the J-egu-
lations made thereunder, every person who at midnight on the night between Sunday the 2nd and Monday the
3rd April was in any part of the Commonwealth, or was on a voyage between any two Commonwealth ports, is
required to fill in a personal card, and hand it to the Collector or to the nearest postmaster.
In the event of such a card not having been supplied to any such person, it is his or her duty to apply to the
nearest post office for a blank card and copy of instructions, and to fill in the card and hand it to the postmaster.
If such person was the head of a household at the date of the Census, he or she is, in addition, required to obtain
from the post office a householder’s card, and to fill in and hand it, together with his or her personal card, to the
postmaster. In no ca«e must two personal cards be filled in for one person, nor two householders’ cards for one
household. G. H. KNIBBS, Commonwealth Statistician,
“ The Rialto, '' Collins Street, Melbourne.
“ Oliver Odd ” : “Adelaide ” (B. 9/2/11)
'ays the Dago has no grip whatever on the fish,
fruit, oyster and catering trade in his village.
I’ll grant the fish and catering, but I’ll go four
that the Dago has more than a slippery hold on
the fish and oysters. There are two firms in
Adelaide that practically control the market for
marine vegetables, one openly doing business
under a Greek name, while the other is a doubtful
quantity. Further, the price of fish—ls. per lb.
for Murray cod and Is. 2d. for sehnapper—is
proof of the existence of a ring ; and a fish-ring in
Australia without a Dago somewhere in its cir-
cumference is too wonderful to contemplate.

IN BANANA LAND
The new Queensland Redistribution of
Seats scheme wipes out the Labor strong-
holds of Clermont, Crbydon, Bulloo and
Burke, and creates a number of fresh con-
stituencies, mainly about the capital, by way
of compensation. The new arrangement
will probably benefit the Denham party far
less than it was expected to, as jjolitical feel-
ing in and around Brisbane is steadily wax-
ing in Labor’s favor. In any case, Redistri-
bution was the correct thing, as the gigantic
anomalies existing were a direct negation
of the principle of One Adult One Vote.
Bananaland teetotalers are being whipped
up to vote “No,” on the ground that, if “Yes”
is carrV-d, the liquor traffic may be nation-
alised. These silly people don’t know, ap-
parently, that the traffic can be nationalised
just the same if “No” is carried. In fact,
it is being nationalised by degrees in Wes-
tralia, where various State pubs are in full
swing, and in South Aus., where municipal
pubs have made a start. A “Yes” vote
won’t make the least difference in the power
of nationalising the liquor, or any other
business.
There is a superstition that bore water
is valueless for raising crops in dry country,
owing to the soil becoming impregnated with
alkali after a time; but W. A. McVean, of
Oondoroo station, near Winton, says the
idea is all wrong. The trouble is not with
the water, he says, but with the land, for
he has seen the same water a huge success
on some soils, and a howling failure on
others. lie has produced fine crops of hay,
maize, sorghum and lucerne on deep, loamy
soil irrigated with bore water, but has found
that sandy and clayey soils became hard and
unworkable. While . managing Thurulgoona
station, on the Warrego, he took five crops
of hay off one paddock in successive years,
and in 10 years never had a failure. The
average crop was three tons per acre. He
didn’t trouble about the yield of grain, as
hay was wanted, but he made a test once,
and got a yield of 40 bushels to the acre.
“Bromide,” on the still-piebald North: —
The slimness of the sugar-cocky in dodging the
employment of whites while collecting the “white”
bonus will get him and his industry into trouble
some day. At the Palms and the Pleystowe, in the
Mackav district, the cane is tipped at “loading
stations” alongside the line. It is then loaded on
the railway trucks by colored aliens, nominally en-
gaged by the mills, who are paid Bd. per ton, which
is deducted when the farmer gets his returns. Thus,
the farmer is able to claim the bounty, as he does
not directly employ the aliens, although, as a matter
of fact, they handle his crop. In one sugar district
there is a plantation which belonged to a Civil
servant. To get the bonus he settled his house and
about half an acre of land around it on his wife.
He then fenced this piece off, and when he was
planting he had Kanakas working inside the fence,
preparing the plant-cane and passing it through the
fence to whites, who put it in the ground. His
elastic conscience afterwards allowed him to swear
that no colored alien was employed on his land.
For tricks that are mean the heathen Chinee isn’t in
it with some sugar Cookies. Australia will get
very tired of it all some day; and then bang will go
the bonus, and smash will go the industry.
From a man who ought to know all about
it: —
Ex-Premier Kidston’s long tenure of office has
left many marks of his personal love of economy;
and in this respect the Marine Department is very
conspicuous. Lighthouses and navigational marks
have not been increased in proportion to the raplS
strides that shipping has made; in fact, there is one
light-vessel less, and that one has been shifted from a
locality where it was sorely needed, i.e., Proudfoot
Shoal, where vessels from “the East,” bound for
Thursday Island, are liable to knock, and not be
heard. N. S. Wales, Victoria and South Aus., which
have one clear side where vessels can flounder and
harm no one, are all more liberally served. Im-
poverishment afflicts the whole Department, and,
naturally, the personnel has suffered; and in the
Pilot service the highly qualified man is the excep-
tion, and not the rule. The best interests of
shipping, the keeping of staff and plant in a state of
efficiency, and the development of navigational facili-
ties, to keep pace with the times, have all been
pushed into the background by a most virulent type
of Scotch economy. Plurality of duties and low
pay, is the watchword. The man who can fill the most
positions on the least pay is the man who gets
advancement, whether he has the qualifications or
not. To the lay mind a man filling the position
of Harbor Master, Pilot, Inspector of Fisheries, Light-
houses, Explosives and Shipping, Examiner and Ship-
wright Surveyor should be drawing close up to £lOOO
per annum. As a matter of fact he seldom draws
£3oo—and naturally the qualified man is not
tempted to either come in or stay in.
From an Ancient Mariner: —
The Yongala disaster has advertised the fact that
the Queensland maritime authorities are all behind-
hand. Notoriously one of the most difficult to navi-
gate, the Queensland coast is nevertheless the worst
lighted in Australia. Nor is it equipped with the
up-to-date methods of signalling adopted nearly
everywhere, such as Morse signalling by night.
Further, no attempt is made to warn shipping of a
threatened storm. Had there been any way of noti-
fying Dent Island of the approaching cyclone, as
there ought to have been, the Yongala could have
had ample warning to take necessary precautions.
Even the Chinese have better storm-signal systems
than Queensland owns.
During the Northern cyclone of March 16
a train entered No. 11 tunnel on the Cairns
line, and the passengers were entertained to
find that big boulders commenced to roll
down the mountain side and block their pro-
gress. Retreat was impossible, for huge rocks
had also closed up on the rear of the car-
riages. Night passed amid fear, trembling
and hurricane lamps, and rescue did not
come till next morning. Some day 500 ft. of
a mountain peak will wipe out the work of
Robb for ever and ever. Meantime the Bris-
bane Courier is excogitating a method of
sheeting home disasters of this kind to the
machinations of the Commonwealth Govern-
ment.

When I Am Gone.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I am gone, send up no supplication;
Pray not for me
Some fresh existence fair, or renovation
Of Life from Sorrow free.
Bow not in grief within some solemn portal,
And fondly beg the skies
That he may rise again, a proud immortal,
Who, softly sleeping, lies.
Pray just that neither joy nor care nor cum-
ber
May break the rest below;
In Nevermore’s Nirvana let me slumber;
And, Gabriel, Do Not Blow!
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 10, 1911.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
One of Brisbane’s best-known and most-
caricatured figures in municipal life passed
over on Wednesday—Aid. John Davies.
“Old John,” with his Dundreary whiskers,
his long frock coat, much-oiled hair and an-
gular figure, always reminded me of the
chemist in the fable who was mistaken by
the little boy for the skeleton. All the
same, the departed city father called for
more than a passing word of gratitude, for,
amongst other things, he was instrumental
in obtaining a fine park, now called after
him, for his constituents. He was at one
time chosen by the Tory plebiscite to run
with Willie Stephens for Brisbane South,
but Philp couldn’t stand his whiskers, or
the amount of invisible soap he always used,
or something, and turned him dowrn. As a
chemist Davies administered free pills to
many a hard-up sufferer, and generally acted
the part of guide, philosopher and friend to
the needy.
The marriage of Harold Parker (the Bris-
bane boy who hewed the lovely Ariadne from
her native marble) and Miss Robinson has a
double interest for Queensland, as the bride is
bonnie Jean, daughter of Agent-General
Thomas Robinson. The orange blossoms
bloomed last month.
Pretty Greta Baynes, in a frock of white
satin and Duchess point lace, with the usual
accompaniments, gave her word to be a house-
hold blessing to Mr. Arthur L. MacDonald,
of Yaamba station, on Wednesday. The
bridegroom is a son of the P. F. MacDonald
who .■ sold “CuHm-la-ringo” to the ill-fated
Wills, just before that unfortunate and
almost the whole of his party were killed by
the blacks. The bride’s father, Harry
Baynes, is one of Brisbane’s most popular
sports, and a lover of a good thing between
the shafts.
How one’s past does intrude itself, to be
sure. Many things have been said of Pre-
mier Denham, but it was left to the Daily
Mail (London) to announce that he “was
born at Langport, Somerset, and served his
apprenticeship with a local drapery firm dur-
ing the years 1872-77.”
A lady in the woolly West, in a burst of
generosity, has forwarded a letter to the Gen-
eral Hospital Committee, offering to donate
a certain proportion of the rental of a shop
in Allora to the funds of the institution, if
the committee will send along a tenant. Un-
fortunately, the idea of running a land and
estate agency has not yet struck the com-
mittee with any force. Besides, very few
people want to go to Allora.
There is yet another engagement to chronicle,
and it has to do with pretty Miss Fitzgerald
and Mr. Nigel Shand, of Toowong.
Miss Fanny Donkin will marry, in Syd-
ney, Mr. Cadell, of Deepwater, on the day
after this reaches you. The Cadells of
Deepwater are some of the best known and
liked of the highlands cattle and sheep men,
and Miss Fanny, whose father once drove
the smartest four-in-hand hereabouts, has
friends by the streetful.
Great sympathy is being expressed with
Mrs. Townley and her daughters at the un-
timely death of a son of the house, in the
Malay Straits. His father, Capt. Townley,
died last year, while the two girls were
visiting the brother.
Another well-known about-town whose
condition is causing his family some uneasi-
ness is John Forrest, until recently the gen-
eral manager of Moreheads, Ltd., and one of
Queensland’s biggest cattle men. Dengue
on top of other troubles has kept him down.
Amongst the names on the big passenger
list of the Orient liner Orvieto, which sailed
on Wednesday, was Mrs. Bocock, well known
in literary and charitable circles, and Mrs.
Cecil Palmer and hubby. The scribe lady
should get a good look in at the Coronation
shivoo, if letters from the Macgregor clan
cut any ice.
Ferman MacKinnon, who has for some
half-dozen years put his shoulder to the pen
for the local Telegraph, takes the editorial
chair of the Darling Downs Gazette, Too-
woomba, at Easter time. MacKinnon is a
prophet of practical Socialism, in addition
to his other virtues.
The Home Sec., with a party, has been
touring the Burnett district, and, during the
tour, called in at the ancient and interesting
town of Mt. Percy, where your Mario had
an opportunity of seeing copper smelting.
The sight of the hissing trays of molten
metal and the sluggish stream of slag pour-
ing from the furnaces like the overflow from
Tophet, produced a 6 x 4 thrill. If the
place which begins with a capital H is any-
thing like those smelting works, I’m going to
be good in future. The old mine, which
was going in the early ’7o’s, is now the deep-
est for copper in Queensland, the ore com-
ing from 1100 ft. down; soon it will be
grubbed for a hundred feet deeper. Even
with the metal at the present low price, the
works run at a small profit, thus bearing out
the faith of late mining-man Bennett, who,
in spite of public and official discredit, clung,
limpet-like, to his belief in the property.
A Northerner writes me:—
Alma Moodie, the Queensland child-violinist, is
getting good help in the Big Fog. The wife of
good Australian Coghlan (N.S.W. Agent-General) is
interesting herself in the youngster, and, as hints
have been thrown out that Royalty is interested,
Society is flitting to her aid. The child, even as
a three-year-old babbler, had a wonderful ear. She
could tell the notes composing any chord (with or
without accidentals) struck on any octave of the
piano. Her mother was a music-teacher —I learnt
from her —and 1 often saw the child at these re-
markable pranks. She learnt her violin from
Hage, of Rockhampton, who was recently asked to
make one of Lemmone’s eight violins for Melba’s
Australian tour. (He said “No,” by the way.)
Alma must be nearing 13 now.
Yours affectionately,
MARDG.
Premier Denliam, anxious to catch the
Temperance vote at the Enoggera election,
forecasted the introduction of a Licensing
Act Amending Bill at the commencement of
next session. The Alliance News (Rec-
habite) thanks him thusly :
Of course we must riot forget this promise was
made at an election, and our past experience has
taught us to be careful how far we trust expressions
of this kind.
Poor Denham! Bung doesn’t like him, and
the Wowsers mistrust him!
The ancient, insanitary custom of “kissin’
the book,” by way of taking the oath, has
been meeting with much public disapproval
in Queensland lately. In the Jondaryan
murder trial a doctor said it was an unclean
and disease-spreading practice, and he wasn’t
going to take any risks, when the risks were
supremely unnecessary. There was the
usual argument, which was bitten off by the
Judge telling him that he must kiss some
800k —either the Court’s cancer-carrier or
one of his own. Whereupon the medical man
did what most men do under the circum-
stances, and the farce was ended.

A GREAT OFFER
To all Lovers of Dickens.
No lover of books, no one who enjoys a
good story, with its blended elements of re-
freshing laughter, tender feeling, and wise
and humorous views of life —above all, no
family circle-bought to be without an edition
of the novels of Charles Dickens.
We have no hesitation in affirming that in
the London Edition of the Works of Dickens,
in thirty volumes, we are offering to the pub-
lic the most attractive and the most useful
edition yet offered to the public of all the
editions of Dickens that are continually be-
ing put on the market.
THE LONDON EDITION OF DICKENS
includes a splendid Life of Charles Dickens,
by the late F. G. Kitton. Each novel is pre-
fixed by a short illustrated introduction, giv-
ing the date and circumstances, in which it
was written, and interesting particulars about
the actual place in which the scene of the
novel is laid. And the most famous and real
of Dickens’s characters are traced back to
their original prototypes; this is a very in-
teresting and valuable feature.
CRUIKSHANK’S ORIGINAL
ILLUSTRATIONS.
No edition of Dickens’s works is complete
without the famous original illustrations by
“Phiz,” Cruikshank and Leech, Seymour and
Cattermole; for Dickens himself approved
their work as the best embodiment in picture
form of his own idea. These illustrations
have hitherto appeared in black and white
only, except in the case of one or two novels
issued at very high prices. But in this fine
London Edition, out of a total of 336 plates,
104 are beautifully colored, and this for the
first time is an edition at a popular price.
Besides these splendid plates, there are, in
the specially-written introductions to the
nevels, interesting illustrations of famous
places in Diekensland. These have been
specially drawn.
A FREE BOOKLET.
The Publishers have compiled a hand-
somely illustrated prospectus of “The LON-
DON DICKENS,” which will be sent POST
free to all who use this form. It tells all
about this fine edition, the division of the
volumes, etc., and gives specimens of the il-
lustrations. It also tells of their offer to
deliver the edition, carriage paid, for a first
payment of 55., the balance being paid by
small monthly payments.
To Mr. T. G. Lothian ,
100 Flinders Street, Melbourne.
Please send me, free of charge, and without
anti obligation , fullest information about
“The London Dickens,” with particulars of
your plan of monthly payments.

tioning The Bulletin.)

A CIGAR is the BEST
SMOKE OF ALL!
But why can’t you get a
Good Cigar in the Country?
A LL over Australasia the only places you can
r\ buy a really high-class Cigar is in the big
cities. In the country districts a good Cigar
cannot be had; because it’s only at the pubs that
you can buy Cigars at all in the out-back districts.
The publican knows that selling liquor pays him
better. He reckons that his customers only call
for a Cigar when they ve got a few drinks in and
want to dodge getting ‘‘fu’ the noo." Any old
Cigar does for that.
So he buys a few hundred at a time from
some back-yarder"-real “Flor de Cabbage"
with big gold pictures all over the box and
mighty pretty bands round them—made from the
waste leaf and the cheapest growths, but called
by some high-fain tin’ name. (The making of these
Cigars is another story-I’ll tell you about it
another time.)
sfďghf
Thestorekeeperor tobac-
conist in country towns does
not sell good Cigars either.
If he does, he has to add to
the selling price of the Cigar,
the maker’s profit, the profit
of the warehouseman he
buys from, the profit of the
traveller who calls and sells
them to him, and his own
profit.
So that a good Cigar
that sells in the city at 6d.
he’d have to charge gd. for,
and not grow fat on it then,
because the number he would
sell would be small; and
good Cigars have to be kept
in a uniform temperature,
free from exposure to the
air, or else they’ll go off.
Those are just some of
the reasons why you can’t
get a good Cigar in the
country parts of Australia,
and also why so very few
Cigars are at present smoked
in the country.
Buy a 100-box of my
DIADEMAS,” smoke 5 or
6 of them, and, if they’re
not as good as any 6d.
Cigar you’ve ever smoked,
you needn’t pay a penny.
That’s the way I’m in-
troducing my Cigars into
Australia. I know Cigar-
making from A to Z. I know
these DIADEMAS Cigars of
mine are just the coolest,
smoothest and most luxu-
nous smoke any man could wish for. Cigar-
smokers of a lifetime say so; and the more you
know about a good Cigar the better you’ll like
them.
My DIADEMAS are made from the very
finest, mild, rich, mellow, Java leaf, the filling or
inside heart of the Cigar being all straight long
Brazilian. The Cigars are hand made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before it
goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed to
burn free and draw easily. The Cigar is 4 inches
long, and the picture at the side is a copy of it,
exact in size and shape.
Now, this DIADEMAS Cigar is as good as
any 6d, Cigar, sold in the cities by high-class
tobacconists, as I sell it direct to the customer—
saving the cost of wholesalers’, salesmen’s, and
retailers’ profit. I only charge you 22/6 the box
of 100 DIADEMAS, postage paid right to your
address.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade. Please add exchange to country and
interstate cheques.

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
Catarrh is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
v~nt relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.
RAMEY'S MEDICATOR cures Catarrh,
Hay Fever, Catarrhal Deafness, Head-
ache, Neuralgia, Colds, Bronchitis, Asth-
ma. Write for booklet free, or send
Postal Note i.os. for Medicator complete,
with 4 months treatment, post free anywhere.

REXONA.
ONE of the most sensational star items ever seen on an
Australian Stage is the acrobatic “ Danse du Vaurien, ’
given by Miss Ivy Schilling and that noted artiste and
dancer, Mr. Fred Leslie, in the record-breaking Musical Comedy,
“Our Miss Gibbs,’’ sLill playing to enormous business in its
seventh month at the Theatre Royal.
While touring with the “Jack and Jill” Pantomime, Miss
Schilling won the Maskelyne and Devant Prize for Beauty at
Christchurch, N.Z.—a valuable gold watch and chain, which she
wears daily.
Miss Ivy Schilling is the winner of West’s Pictures Surf
Sirens’ Competition, in which close on 100,000 votes were •
recorded. Her figure is a perfect model of athletic symmetry
and grace. To preserve its litheness, its vigor and its strength,
she uses REXONA.
This is what the admired acrobatic actress says :
Theatre Royal, Sydney,
Manager, Rexona, March 17, 1911.
Sydney,
Dear Sir,—l have much pleasure in recommending your
Rexona, for I have been using it constantly for some time. As
a rub-down after acrobatic dancing, and as a magical reviver to
strained muscles, I have found it excellent. After surfing, it. is
invaluable as a protection against sunburn and freckles. It is a
wonderful healer, and I always keep a tin of it handy.
Yours sincerely,
(Sgd. ) IVY SCHILLING.
REXONA is a reliable Remedy for all Eruptions and Irritations of the Skin, Eczema, Pimples,
Sores, Chilblains, Chapped Hands, Sciatica, Itching or Bleeding Piles, Cuts, Burns, Bruises,
Scalds, Bad Legs, Stings of Insects, and all kinds of Inflammation. Rexona is sold only i 1
triangular pots at 1/6, and in larger pots, containing nearly four times the quantity, at 3/-. All
Chemists and Stores will supply it, or it will be sent post free upon receipt of the price upon
application to “Rexona,” Box 974, G.P.0., Sydney.

Dark Stars.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SINGLE suns that, slowly burning, m their
orbits swing and press;
Planet-souls and spirits turning own
paths of nothingness !
Blackened lives, remembered only by the aze 0
vanished light—
These are sweeping, stark and lonely, throug 1 ie
Everlasting Night! ..
These are drifting where no laughter in the a-S
of Silence rings;
Where the dead shall drift hereafter with t leir
sirens, priests and kings !
Suns long set—their day all over ; as the clouds
in terraced bars
Round the far worlds lift and hover, swing the
dark and perished stars !
Stormy sunsets, lurid-glaring—who remembers .
Who regrets ?
Vanished leaders —these are faring down the
Nights’ blown parapets;
Suns whose splendor paroxysmal lit the way of
creeping Man,
Fallen down to depths abysmal from the Army s
shining van !
Cold and heavy lie the vapors, choking out the
fire of life ;
Quenched stars and flameless tapers that were lit
in days of strife !
Dim and red, in shadows sweeping—shining once
like peerless Mars ;
Where the suns are vigil-keeping troop the dark
and nameless stars!
Stars that hold their proud dominion, beacon-souls
that shine and guide ;
Lo, with these on silent pinion earth’s long-
vanished leaders ride !
Where the ages loom and darken Christs and
Buddhas nameless are;
None may heed and none may hearken —star rides
on with comrade star !
Not alone !—with dark star-shadows, Nazarenes
whose day is done ;
Signal-souls whose El Dorados were in dreams
ecstatic spun !
Side by side, the Rebels holy ride upon Olympic
'cars,
Where the suns are sinking slowly, fade the dark,
ouce-lustrous stars !
Souls to-day are world-ward landing whilst their
sands of glory run ;
You and I, half-understanding, at the death-bed
of a sun;
Out of Space and Time appearing—out of dark-
ness into light,
Whilst the dying stars go veering down the high-
ways of the Night!
You and I—of star-dust fashioned, shaped on
earth, we think and see,
Whilst the suns new-dead, unpassioned yield their
shining panoply !
Perished globes—l see them dying, going down
like sinking spars;
Where the mist-wrapt worlds are flying drift to
dark, unanchored stars!
Sphere-like souls, their light departed, ’thwart
the seas of Space disperse;
Stars unknown, unnamed, uncharted, rolling
through the universe;
Retinues of souls slow-marching o’er the ages
stretching wide;
Where the pallid moons are arching souls of
vanished leaders ride !
Planets cold, beyond our vision, wandering light-
less and forlorn,
Till the hour of soul-collision, when the bright,
new stars are born !
Souls that sweep on through the darkness, waiting
for new avatars,
Where the dead worlds roll in starkuess lie the
one-time splendid stars !
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
Wilson, the Crocodile and the
Cabman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rri HERE is a certain garden in a Mel-
bourne suburb which contains a
stuffed crocodile. It is an unusual
sort of ornament, and was placed there
many years ago by an ex-squatter from
Queensland. He did not attach the thing
very firmly to its stand, and the gales of
years have left it supported only by the
midship section. The ugly head, with its
half-open jaws, and the big tail hang loosely
above the ground; and when the wind blows
they wag slowly from side to side, giving
the thing a horribly life-like look, especially
if the light isn’t very good. Short-sighted
people have fled from it even at high noon.
**. * *
It was 2 o’clock of a misty moonlight
morning when the cab pulled up under the
pine trees that fringe the Garden of the
Crocodile.
“’Ere you are,” growled the cabman, open-
ing the window at the back of his seat, and
addressing the man in crumpled evening--
dress who lay dozing in the corner.
“Don’t sound like it,” muttered the fare
without opening his eyes. “Y’ didn’t drive
over any gravel. Don’t sound like it a
bit!”
“Well, it looks like it,” retorted the cab-
man fiercely. “It’s th’ place you de-scribed
an’ I’ve drove yer to it, an’ now I want you
t’ get out and give me me fare!”
The passenger yawned immensely, and
without any show of agitation, remarked,
“I bleeve I’ve met you before somewhere.”
“Never mind about that now!”
“Voice sounds fam-famil’r,” muttered the
man in the dress-suit. “Swear I’ve met you
before somewhere. What’s color of yer
hair —red?”
“Look ’ere, you,” said the cabman wildly,
“you wake up an’ shut up an’ pay! I
won’t take that sorter thing from anyone,
an’ I’m not goin’ f drive yer any more t’
night! Gome on now!
The fare’s sleepy eyes opened, and jested
for a moment on the cabman s flushed face

he mlSbS 5 “Same man! Always wants
’ "Welh d ain’t it natclier'l?” shouted the

Pa -Ts five shillin’s ’mount of your fare?”
‘‘Yes it is ! ,J
“Do you always charge same ’mount from
Club?” ,
“Yes —an’ you orter know it!
The fare held out his right hand. Well,
give us ten shillin’s change and shurrup
’bout it!” ,
The cabman turned and spat over the
wheel on to the road. “Tork sense!” he
sneered. “Yer never gave me no suvrin, an
if yer sez yer did, well, then I sez that ye’re
—that ye’re wrong !”
The man inside sat up, and, leaning for-
ward, leered in the enraged driver’s face.
“Don’ you remember drivin’ me once be-
fore?” he inquired.
The red-haired cabman looked hard at his
customer, and started. “Now I come t' look
at yer, I do. An’ a rotten bad time yer give
me —kep’ me messin’ about arf’ th’ blanky
night.”
•‘Qui’ ri’,” said Wilson, triumphantly.
“Yer tried t’ give me in charge, an’ charged
me ten shillin’s, an’ I gave you las’ sov’rin
I had. Give’s ten shillin’s change an’ shur-
rup !”
“Blimey!” yelled the cabman, aghast, as
soon as the full import of the words had
soaked into his understanding. “Lor’ blimey,
you’re a bosker, you are! Yer give a man
a few bob over and come at ’im for it as
change ’bout two year afterwards. W’y
don’t yer tork sense?”
“Shurrup, and give me ten shillin’s
change,” growled Wilson, aggressively, from
the interior of the cab.
“You don’t get no change outer me,” said
the cabman desperately, speaking through
the trap-door.“ That sorter thing won’t ’old
in law, you see.”
Replied Wilson: “I don’t wanter see yer
father-in-law! I want ten shillin’s change!”
“I didn’t say nothin’ about me father-in-
law !”
“Well, f’r th’ Lord’s sake, don’t! I don’
want yer t’ drag yer relations inter a pri-
vate matter of this kind. There’s no neces-
sity t’ drag old gentleman inter it at all.
Daresay he’s got troubles enough of his own,
poor old josser.”
“Oh ! ’old on! I didn’t say nothin’ ”
“’Scuse me one minute! There’s one
thing I partic’ly dislike ’bout cabmen and
washerwomen —they always wanter drag
their relations into argument, and tell yer
how many children they’ve got t’ s’port.
That’s not satisfact’ry way doin' business
—it isn’t business-like !”
“Oh, ’old on a minit! I didn’t say nothin’
about me father-m-law —I ain’t got one!
Wot I said was that that there gag o’ yours
wouldn’t -’old-in-law! It’s no good tryin’ t’
put me orf. I want me munney!”
A scornful laugh was the only reply.
“An’ what’s more, I’m goin’ t’ yet it!”
“Qui’ ri’!”
“It’s no use you tryin’ any funny bizness
on me! I know my rights, and’ I’ll stick to
’em!”
“Qui’ ri’, my boy !”
“You don’ get me drivin’ yer round all
th’ blanky nigiit f’r nothin’! I’ve been at
th’ game too long !”
“Bet you haven’t been at it as long as yer
rotten old horse!”
The sally maddened the driver. ‘lts a
good enough horse f’r th’ likes o’ you!” he
yelled. “If yer wanted t’ get ’ome quick,
w’y didn’t yer tell yer bloomin’ shover t’
bring yer bloomin’ moter for yer?”
“’Scuse me one minute,” said Wilson with
dignity. “I wasn’t complainin’ ’bout the speed
of th’ thing; I was referrin’ t’ th’ scrap-
heap’s age. You know d —n well that it
ought t’ be in th’ Benevolent ’Sylum.”
“Never mind where it oughter be ! It’s
finished with draggin’ you roun’ orl th’
bloomin’ night f’r nothin!”
“Wrong again, me boy ! It isn’t draggin
me roun’ f’r nothin, I paid for this funeral
two years ago!”
“Well, this ’ere’s th’ cemet’ry, then,”
snapped the cabman. “You get an’
bury yerself an’ lemme get ’ome!”
“Hoi’ on!” said the fare hurriedly. “You
take me up th’ drive. I’m not goin’ t’
walk!”
“I’ll get somethin’ extrv outer ’im f r this
w’en ’e’s sober,” muttered the cabman as he
turned his horse and steered the conveyance
through the open wide gates.
As they crossed the pathway, Wilson
stirred in the recesses of the vehicle, and said
loudly: “They’ve been an’ put up new gates
since tea! D —d ’stravagance !”
Next instant there was a loud shout of
horror, and Wilson, who was a heavy man,
shot head-foremost out of the cab, dragged
the driver from his seat, and ran him with
resistless strength towards the border oi
pine trees. “Quick, f’r God’s sake. he
panted as they reached the biggest. “Climb
like blazes! Make room f’r me!” He
seized the protesting cabman’s _ leg and
hoisted him up on to the lowest limb. “Up
with yer cabby! Quick —climb!” Then,
with astounding agility, he swung himself
up after the man he had saved, and started
to prod and push him skyward through a
splintering maze of branches. “Up, ole
chap! Up higher!” he kept hissing. , The
terrified cabman’s whines of “What tli’ ’ell’s
th’ matter?” and “’Oo th’ devil’s chasin us?”
went totally unheeded in the terror and con-
fusion. Their frenzied progress skyward
ended only when they had reached the tree-
top—or within a few feet of it. The sway-
ing of the mast-like trunk was very notice-
able at that height. The garden and the
dwai’fed stationary cab, seen through the
branches, seemed fearfully remote —the
clouds and moon absurdly near. The wind
moaned and bent the tree, and Wilson re-
marked breathlessly : “By George ! narrow’st
escape I ever had !”
“Was it burglars?” whispered the cab-
man, with his arms and legs tightly wound
round the limb on which he sat.
“Burglars! No! Didn’t yer see th’alli-
gator?”
The cabman almost lost his hold. “’E’s
got the ’orrors!” he whined miserably. “’E’s
got the ’orrors, an’ now I’m up a green tree
with a blue loonertick!”

( ou shut up!” hissed Wilson, angrily.
Open yer eyes and look at it waggin’ its
tail at yer rotten old horse! It’ll start t’
eat it in a minute, an’ serve th’ brute right!
Never seen such a rotten horse in me life!”
The cabman, by dint of craning his neck,
at last managed to get a good view of the
stuffed alligator. It looked horribly alive
in the misty moonlight as it ogled the droop-
ing horse in the shafts of the cab. Still the
wretched man was loath to believe his eyes,
and had sufficient courage left to state the
fact. “I don’t b’leeve it’s a real alleyga-
tor,” he faltered.
“Well, go down and kick it in th’ teeth,”
sneered the rescuer. “Go on, climb down!”
The cabman made no move.
“If it isn’t a real alligator, what th’
devil do yer think it is—a dragon out of a
Chow laundry ?”
“’Ow could sich a thing git ’ere?” de-
manded the suspended cabman, with sudden,
sheep-like ferocity.
“Don’ be fool! Aren’t things always
gettin loose from museums an’ circuses an’
quariums ? Wliat ’bout those lions that
got outer th’ circus the other day in Noo
South W ales ? Only damnfool like yourself
wants t’ go an’ pinch th’ lionelephantigator
to see if it’s alive. Man with presence of
mind like meself gets out of way quick.
You would be inside that d—d old wliat-
d ye-call-um now if I hadn’t pulled you up
here. You oughter be thankful, ole chap,
that you’re alive!”
‘lll be thankful w’en I know f’r certain
that th’ d—n thing ain’t stuffed !”
“Well, my boy, you sit tight where you
are, an watch it eat your rotten ole horse.
By th way it’s wrigglin’ in front of him, I
guess it’s tryin’ t’ make up its mind which
end of him it’ll eat first!”
“Any’ow, ’’retorted the cabman, “if that
there orse is et, you’ll ’ave t’ pay fur it.
You made me drive inter th’ garden.”
“Now, look here,” said Wilson, standing
precariously on a swaying branch, and tap-
ping the cabman on the knee: “I want you
to distinc’ly understan’ that I refuse abs’-
lootely t’ pay f’r anythin’ at all! If th’
what-d ye-call-um eats th’ horse or th’ cab
or both of ’em, I’m not goin’ t’ pay you one
penny! Remember that!”
“Orright, then. We’ll see what th’lor’ll
do about it!”
“No, m’ boy ! We’ll wait t’ see what th’
d—d old allidile does first!”
‘ You’re tryin’ t’ say alleygator, ain’t
you?”
“Oh, no —nothin’ of th’ kind ! I meant
croclcagator! ”
“Try agen!” jeered the cabman.
Certn’ly, my boy! Anythin’ t’ 'blige
yer! How does allicrock suit yer? Are
yer satisfied now, ole chap?”
“I will be wen I get me property back
an lose you! Ullo! There’s a pleece-
man now. I’m goin ’ down!” He made a
move as though to slip off his perch; but
Wilson, with a large fatherly hand, re-
strained him, and peered down through the
branches at the policeman standing at the
gate. ‘ LOOK OUT!” he yelled lustily.
"Be careful of the crocodile!”
The constable, in his endeavor to locate
the spot whence the warning came, rotated
three times on his axis. He appeared to be
unduly indignant; probably he took the
warning for a mere musical comedy frivo-
lity.
“Run !” howled Wilson. “Don’ be a fool!
RUN! TH’ CROCODILE’LL CATCH
“Come down out av thot!” shouted the
constable, approaching the tree. "Stop yer
nonsense an’ come down!”
“Climb th’ tree!” advised Wilson, in a
frenzy of excitement.
The constable took it for a defiance. “I
will, me boy, if you don’t come down whin
I tell you to!”
“’E’s mad!” wailed the cabman, making
futile efforts to escape. “’E won’t let me
come down!”
“How many of yez is up there?” de-
manded the policeman, endeavoring to peer
up through the branches.
“’E’s drunk,” added the cabman. “’E’s
drunk, like ’e alwiz is!”
“An’ phat th’ devil are you! ”
“I’m th’ cabman !”
“He’s a rotten cabman,” interjected Wil-
son. “I’m sorry I saved his life!”
“Oh, I’m a rotten cabman.”
“Yes, my boy, you are —you’re a real
rotten cabman!”
“Hould on there!” roared the constable.
“I’m not satisfied in me inoind about this
at all! I don’t like th’ look av it!”
“Why, you d —d fool, that jus’ why we
got up !”
“SHUT UP, YOU !”
“It’s no use!” screamed the cabman.
“You carn’t get ’im t’ tork sense —’e’s
drunk !”
“It’s my furrm o-pinyin that ye are
both drunk!”
“Thas qui’ ri,” cordially assented Wilson.
“Th’ cabman is drunker than I am, though !
How drunk d’ye reckon you are?”
There was a pause. Then, in a voice of
cold determination, came the answer: “Not
too drunk t’ go up after you if ye don’t
come down right away. Come now, th’ two
av yez, an’ give over talkin’ nonsinse. It’ll
save bother.”
Without more ado they descended —
slowly and with a great snapping of twigs
and much snappish argument. At last, the
long legs of Wilson emerged from the woody
maze and felt for the earth. About liis
shoulders played the busted boots of the cab-
man on the same quest. The policeman
reached up immense arms and assisted them
both tenderly to the ground. They were both
dishevelled and dusty, and Wilson’s top-
hat was crushed over his eyes. It was some
time before the policeman recognised his
captive. When at last he did so, he saluted
smartly with a broad smile: “Mr. Wilson,
sorr ! I didn’t know ye !”
Wilson shook him warmly by the hand.
“Don’t mention it, old feller! Mistake’s
mutual, I ’sure you! HULLO! What’s
old Tom Charlton doin’ here this time of
night?”
The policeman and the dusty cabman
turned round and beheld a large, half-
dressed man with a stick in one hand and a
stable lantern in the other, hurrying to-
wards them.
“Hullo, Tom, you old rotter, shouted
Wilson at the approaching householder.
“Good Lord! it’s Wilson!” gasped the
householder. “What the blazes brought
you here?”
“That rotten old cab, my boy. I fell
’sleep an’ th’ driver mixed up directions
like a fool and drove me in by mistake, I
think.”
“I didn’t make no mistake, at all!”
“Oh! do shut up, ole man . . . W T hen I
got in I saw your d—d old tame alligator
an’ climbed th’ tree, shovin’ this cabman be-
fore me. I don’t know why I saved him;
suppose I must have wanted him t’ drive
me home. It was d —d plucky of me!”
“It was d—d silly of yer!”
“Look here, my frien’! If you don’t shut
up, I’ll pay you only strict legal fare —
whatever it is—an’ you’ll have t’ go ’way
and die of a broken heart. .. . Will you
shut up?”
“Blimey, you think you can say anythin’
to a man, you do!”
“That’ll do, now — that'll do!”
“Anyone ’ud think you was th’ Tgh
Commission’r th’ way you go on!”
“Quit it!” snapped the constable, de-
cisively. “Can’t ye see that Mr. Wilson is
only jokin’ wid you? ’Tis his way.”
The cabman, grumbling in his throat, sub-
sided.
“When did you leave the club, Billy?”
laughed the proprietor of the alligator.
Wilson shoved his belltopper to the back
of his head and scratched his eye. “Blowed
if I know, ole man; but it seems years since
I had that last drink!”
“Well, it’s time we all had another,” said
Chai-lton. “Hold on a minute, all of you—
I’ll bring it out to the garden.” With the
swinging stable lantern he disappeared into
the house, and returned in a minute with a
bottle and siphon. From the pockets of
his dressing-gown he deftly extracted glasses
and handed them round. Then they gather-
ed round the cab, and the chuckle of poured
whisky and the soothing hiss of the siphon
made a whole Peace Conference in itself.
It was the policeman who finally broke
up the gathering, just as day was breaking,
by saying that he had to meet the sergeant
at the corner. Then Wilson arose from the
alligator’s back, and taking his top-hat from
over the brute’s left eye. where it had been
roosting at a rakish angle, offered the officer
a lift.
Charlton, his lantern still burning
bravely, waved the farewell as they drove
out the gates; and the whole three of them
—Wilson, the cabman and the constable—
waved back and cheered. Of the departing
guests, only the horse appeared to be de-
pressed. But then, it had not shared in
the whisky.
KODAK.
Mordialloc.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
riIHE haze along the hillsides, the sparkle on
I ’ the Bay
-**- Marconi forth the message of Spring’s ad-
vance to-day.
Now all the fields are blazoned with epaulettes of
gold ;
Now all the trees their standards of Victory
uphold.
In gallant cohorts riding, white gloves upon their
hands,
The soldier seas are marshalled along Port Phil-
lip’s sands.
So dream we here together of all sweet things
that be.
By drowsy Mordialloc, a-facing to the sea.
To-day no red-robed Angel of Discontent shall
mar
Our peace with drums of discord or clash of social
war ;
To-day this land Aegean of roses, love and wine.
In all its lotus splendor is wholly yours and mine,
While Commerce, coarsely strident, plays out her
vulgar part,
Romance shall be our handmaid, with Poesy and
Art.
We left the mart behind us, in Flinders-street,
that we
Might find at Mordialloc a temple by the sea.
Your hair hath meshed the sunbeams ; your voice
is low and sweet
(French heels enhance, ’tis certain, the play of
pretty feet).
This Southern land that gave you your roses and
your pearl
Is maiden yet to conquest; like you—a careless
girl.
These meadows all around us, to me they but en-
fold
Your daintiness—a ruby, set in Australian gold,
The spirit of my own land, vivacious, young and
free,
To-day at Mordialloc, expression finds in thee.
So falls your voice in rhythm ; so beats my heart
in tune ;
While by a velvet stairway creeps down the After-
noon.
Till lo ! at Evening’s doorway she standeth soft
and shy
To wave in sunset blushes duenna Day good-
bye. . . .
Now creeps the slow sail shoreward across the
darkening Bay;
Now close in silent thousands, the blossoms of the
Day ;
The bare- hath left the foreland, the light hath
left the sea ;
White stars, by Mordialloc, burn over you and
me.
l’envoi.
But when the wheels of Action once more around
me whirl,
I’ll think of Mordialloc, a sunset and a girl ;
And where upon his service—the red-robed Angel
—I
Go forth his will proclaiming, all days, until I
die,
Though Duties stern command me, though proud
ambitions call
The waves by Mordialloc shall sing beyond them
all !
And, golden as thy tresses, with inward sight I’ll
see
The sun on Mordialloc go down—in memory.
VICTORIA.
EDW. J. BRADY.
